It often happens that there is a flow of blood from an artery which has been cut at the site of a wound which occurs externally or at the edge of an abscess or when cauterizing a limb and suchlike procedures; and it becomes difficult to stop it.
So when it happens to anyone, then quickly reach with your hand towards the orifice of the artery and place your index finger over it; press on it hard until the blood is dammed under your finger and nothing comes out from it.
Then place small olive oil cauterizing irons into the fire and a great number of them; blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, small or large depending on the wound and where the artery has been cut; place the cautery iron on that vein after you quickly remove your finger, and hold it there until the blood stops flowing.
But if the blood is forced out when you lift your finger from over the artery orifice and you put out that hot iron then quickly take another one of those which are prepared in the fire.
Thus do not stop doing this with one after another until the blood stops flowing.
And be careful not to burn any nerve which is there or some other injury will occur for the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to cut it off; especially when the artery is large, unless with one of four methods. Or by cauterization, as we said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its ends contract, and the blood is cut off. Or it can be tightly tied with a strong binding thread. Or place on it medicines with the property of stopping blood, and bind it with a suitable compress.
He who causes its ceasing with a binding and compression using a cloth, or the positioning of burning objects and similar things, does not bring it about completely, except rarely.
If it happens to someone and neither a doctor nor medicine is present: then begin with the placement of the index finger on the vein's orifice as we described, and compress it strongly until the blood is obstructed from the upper part of the wound and over the artery. And the finger must not be removed from it with cold water of intense coldness always until blood solidifies and swells and is cut off.
And meanwhile one should consider what it needs of cauterization or medicine.
On incision, perforation, and bloodletting for wounds and similar things.
We have already mentioned in Chapter One, every illness that is suitable for treatment with fire and burning medicine, and its opportunities, causes, instruments, and forms of cauterization.
And I placed chapters there from the top to the bottom.
And I will proceed in this chapter along the same path, so as to relieve the enquirer of his quest.
And before I begin it, you must learn, O sons, that this chapter contains nothing of the terror of what is in Chapter One about cauterization.
And for that reason, greater care must be taken because the operation in this chapter often involves evacuation with blood, which is essential to life when opening a vein or making an incision over an abscess, ulceration, wound, or extraction of an arrowhead, as well as incisions over stones and similar procedures; all of which cause trembling, fear, and death for many.
And I warn you not to fall into hesitation on this point because sometimes there present themselves to you in this art different types of people with different types of illnesses. Among them is the one who becomes angry with his illness and for whom death is light; because of the intensity of what he experiences from his illness, and the length of its duration. And with sickness is fear that signifies death.
And among them is the one who offers you his flute player and enriches you with it, hoping for health while his illness is deadly.
You must not agree with him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be more intense than your greed and avarice. Nor should you presume anything about it without subsequent certain knowledge which verifies among you that for which the outcome is praiseworthy.
And employ for all your patients the art of prognosis and indication concerning what is conducive to their recovery appropriately.
For in that lies for you help in acquiring fame and glory, memory and praise.
May God inspire you, my sons, with his understanding and not withhold from you guidance and insight: for that is in his hand.
And I indeed arranged this Chapter by distinguishing it into Chapters, as preceded in the Chapter of Cauterization, from the neck down to the feet: so that your search for what you want from it and need may be alleviated.
On the treatment of water that accumulates on children's heads.
Chapter 1.
You should know that this illness often befalls children at birth: or when the midwife grips the child's head without carefulness.
And whenever it also happens from an unknown hidden cause which is not known.
And I myself have not seen this sickness among others but only in children; and death hastened for all whom I saw it in; therefore, that sight seemed to me to preclude intervention in the matter.
And I have indeed seen among them a boy whose head was filled with water, and his head was growing larger every day until he could no longer sit up by himself due to the size of his own head; and this wetness increased until he died.
And this dampness, it either gathers between the skin and skull; or it gathers beneath the skull bone above the brain.
And the procedure for this is that if there is dampness between the skin and skull, and the abscess is small, then you must make an incision into the middle of the head in a single line across its width; and let the length of the cut be about two finger-widths long until the liquid runs out.
And this is the shape of a sharp, thin knife.
If therefore the dampness is more increased, and the abscess larger, then make two such incisions in this manner.
And if the dampness is beneath the skull bone and you can tell because you see the joints of the skull opened on all sides; and water is pressed down when you compress it with your hand towards the inside, and this is not hidden from you: then you must make three incisions in the middle of the head, arranged like this.
And after making the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the incisions with cloths and rags. After that, apply wine and oil to it from above until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bindings and treat the wound with stitches, and ointments.
And do not leave off binding his head with bandages. Feed the invalid with dry food of little moisture until his member is strengthened and healed.
Another method of incision is to consider where the size of the abscess appears and the collection of water.
Because sometimes it is more in the occipital bone or in the forehead, or on the right or left side.
Therefore, ascend with the incision where the abscess and water accumulation appear to you, and split it as best you can.
Take note not to cut an artery and cause a flow of blood; the invalid may die from that flow with evacuation of moisture.
On incising the arteries which lie behind the ears.
Chapter 2.
When catarrhs happen to a person's eyes or chest and it is protracted and the treatment with medicines does not help, then the ultimate cure for that person lies in incision of these two arteries.
Therefore when you want to make an incision into them, the invalid ought to bow his head down. Then scrape the place with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear. Then let the invalid bind his neck tightly with any piece of cloth he has. Then consider where the vein pulses. And their places are the depressed areas behind the ears; rarely is it hidden except in some people.
Then mark with wax on both; and cut each with a spatoula sharpened on both sides, the incision reaching to bone, and let it be as wide as the head.
And if you wish, introduce the spatula beneath the artery, and cut it open towards the upper side with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two joined fingers.
For when the vein is cut, blood flows out in pulsating manner, jumping up frequently into the inside.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then you should measure from the ear the quantity of three finger-widths. Then mark it with encaustic wax, and find up to the bone.
And that which ought to be emitted from the blood is six ounces, and perhaps you emit more or less of it. All that according to the quantity which appears to you from the strength of the patient, and the fullness of their artery.
Then look at the wound. Then if anything remains down to the bone, then cut it away so that a warm abscess does not occur; then bind up the wound with linen cloths.
Then take care of it with ointment until it is healed.
On the extraction of arteries which are in temples.
Chapter III.
When it happens to a man that he has an old hemicrania, or acute catarrh on account of acrimonious humours, and hotness in the temples, or ancient soda, very severe, and things similar to them: and when one is treated with methods of medical cure, but does not recover from it: then we have come across in these sicknesses extraction of arteries out of the temples, or cauterization of them, as we have related.
The method for this operation is to shave the hair from the cyst and direct attention towards the artery which can be seen protruding from it. It is apparent to you from its pulse and rarely hidden unless in a few men or severe coldness.
If it is hidden from you, the patient should tightly bind their head with any cloth; then rub the area with a rough cloth. Alternatively, they should steam the place with hot water until it becomes apparent to you by clear signs. Then take the spatula I made earlier. Use it to scrape away skin easily until you reach the artery. Now drop a hook into it and extract it upwards until you remove it from the skin and make it escape all the clothes underneath.
If however, the artery is thin; then roll it up with the tip of the hook. Next cut away from it according to the length its ends extend and retract so that there is no bleeding. For if it's not cut away and severed, the blood will not stop flowing entirely.
Then remove six ounces of blood until three remain.
If the artery is large you should tie it in two places with a double strand of strong silk thread or tough cord so that putrid matter does not spread to it before the wound heals and cause bleeding.
Then cut away the excess tissue between the two ligaments. Do it at that moment, or soon after.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a knife-shaped cautery tool down to the bone; until its ends have been cut and it remains in place of the same operation.
And this is better unless if the patient has a fever or a warm constitution. For the cautery is one of those things which help to dry up moistness. So it provides better assistance.
And after the arteries have been extracted, it is necessary to fill up the place with old cotton; and irregular pegs should be placed on top of them.
And after cleaning, it should be treated with dry medications that promote flesh growth; as well as lint dressings until the wound has healed.
If during the operation a flow of blood from an artery should occur, then hurry to cut it off with the cautery tool or fill up the place with wax; and squeeze your hand until the bleeding stops. If none of these is available to you, then place your finger over the spot until the blood clots. And bathe the place with very cold water until the sharpness subsides and it contracts as needed.
And for the easier and gentler method of extracting arteries, it is to cauterize the vein with this tool having two knives; with the sharpness of the two blades similar to that of almagdate. However, they are much less sharp than two knives. Since if they were as sharp as two knives, it hastens cold for both: and does not cut meat with speed. As there is a bit of thickness in them, the heat of the fire is held within and meat is cut quickly; with this, therefore, work is better, lighter, and easier.
And here is the form of the caustic.
And let the space between the two knives be equal to the thickness of a finger; after you have marked two spots with encaustic: place both very hot until they reach the mouth and cut the vein at two places, so that what is between its ends is extended. For these do not heal completely with this caustic.
Chapter four.
Paragraph on treating constant acute inflammation of eyes with tears.
When tears are constant and there is a flow from veins that are on the outside of the head; if you do not apply any medicine to treat it with drugs, and see that the patient's face has already turned red, feeling ants crawling in their forehead: and their eyes are strained, wet so that now their lashes have been worn away by corrosion, and eyelids heated from the sharpness of tears. Then ancient doctors required this operation for treating them; they named this treatment 'with a sword'.
And it is as you prescribe to the patient to shave hair that is on their forehead. Then make three incisions on the forehead evenly spaced along its length; let each incision be about two fingers long and have one incision at the midpoint of the forehead equidistant from the nose in length, a second according to the length slightly from the movement of the lizards, and a third from another side: and beware of cutting any arteries which are on either side; extend your hand from the junction of the two jaws, and let all the space of the incision be three connected fingers in quantity: and have ready for you a piece of sponge or many dry cloths with which to dry the blood.
Then introduce a spatula with two sharp ends of this shape that follows. From the first incision that is from the side of the temple up to the second incision, and scrape with it all the skin which lies between the two incisions, together with any scar which may be over the bone. Then do likewise from the middle incision up to the second one. Then extract this spatula and introduce into the first incision again another implement which is called a sharp scalpel with one side blunt, not sharp on the other side. And this is its shape.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh and bone attached with skin, and its blunt side towards the bone; push it until you reach the middle incision; and cut through all the vessels which run from the head down to the eyes: except for what continues beyond the incision up to that point where the skin appears.
Then do likewise in the middle incision up to the other one.
And after an equal quantity of blood flows out, squeeze the areas with clotted pieces of sponge.
Then place a piece of old cotton lint over every crack and put on top of it compresses soaked in wine, oil, or vinegar. And see to it that no hot abscess develops.
On the third day, loosen the bandage and apply a great deal of liquid with warm water. Then treat it with healing balm and other things that cure wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
On the treatment of acute tearing and catarrh affecting the eyes due to internal head conditions.
When someone has acute, bitter, persistent catarrh and you see their eyes are tired, small, and their vision is already weakened; and the eyelids have become ulcerated, hair falls out from them, and there is sharp, painful head pain in the depths of the skull causing sneezing: then take from these symptoms that this material and the catarrh do not arise except from many veins.
And the best cure for them is this: And it is necessary to advise the patient to shave their forehead. Then make an incision in the forehead, one crack in the middle or another slightly across, and start with a fissure from the left temple to the right; let the incision be made down to the bone; stretch out your hand from the temporal muscles which move during chewing until you feel the bone. Dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the crack with old cotton or linen strips. Then press down from above with gauze pads; soak the pads with wine and oil to prevent hot abscesses.
And when you remove it, and see that the hot abscess has now diminished; then it is necessary to rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that makes flesh grow; for instance if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and prepare a plaster from these, apply it to the birth of flesh on such wounds.
Chapter 6.
On the cure for that which falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a mineral stone; or something like a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a liquid substance, such as water, vinegar and similar things; or some kind of animal.
When therefore a stone or stony substance falls into the ear from something that is not added to it nor grows in the ear, expose the ear to the sun; if you then see the stone, drop into the ear some of either oil of violets or jasmine. Then perform its extraction and make him sneeze with a powder puff, and close his nostrils at the approach of the sneeze after you put around the ear a wad made from cloths or wool, and stretch out the ear upwards.
Many times it comes out with this cure.
But if it does not come out, then perform its extraction with forceps (or pincers); which look like this.
If he walks with it, it's good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a slender blind hook of little curvature.
If therefore it does not exit through that, then make a copper tube, and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole very deeply, and plug what is in the circuit of the tube with softened wax mixed with oil, so that there may be no way for wind except by the tube; then extract it by your strong drawing force of breath.
For indeed many times it exits with what we have related. But if it does not exit this way: then take from helkalimbat, or from wisco, that is, prepared gluten with which birds are caught, a small thing: and place it at the end of a stick after you wrap around it a rag decently placed: then insert it into the ear hole with ease, after you dry out the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not exit with all that we have related, then hasten to cut before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the way of cutting is such that one should weaken it first with a leech on its head: and draw out blood from it according to the measure of its strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands, and turn their ear upwards, and find a small cut in the root of the ear by its fat in a depressed place there: and let the cut be of lunar figure until you reach the bone: then remove it with what is possible for you from instruments.
Then sew up the cut by your hour with speed, and treat it until it is healed.
If the substance that has fallen into the ear is one of the grains which grow and swell up, then proceed with its removal.
If it does not yield to you for its removal, then take a thin, slender spatula like this one: [insert diagram here].
Use it to scrape out that type of grain which has fallen into the ear and do not attempt its removal until you think that the grain is softened by the moisture of the ear canal; instead, break it up into many pieces first: then remove these fragments with a hook or a slender probe or through suction, as we have said, for its removal will be made easier.
For water which has entered the ear, the patient must first sneeze repeatedly while holding his nostrils closed; he should then fill his ear with warm old oil and lie on the side where the water is. If it comes out through this process then it is good.
And if it does not come out: then take many stones the length of a finger, thin and smooth, and heat them slightly over fire; and the patient should introduce one of these into his ear canal and jump on to his foot on that side while striking with another stone against the stone which is in the ear. He must not cease doing this with stone on stone until all the water comes out.
And once the water comes out like this, you should take a piece of dry straw or reed and introduce one end into the ear canal while lighting the other end with fire until it is mostly burnt away. Then repeat with another reed and do this several times until all the water has come out; and remove it along with the cannula as described above, from the stone.
When extracting an animal that has entered the ear, if it is of a small body, such as a flea, and similar to it, take care of it as I said in the division.
But if it is of a large body, which can be sensed: then proceed with its extraction using forceps and hooks; and its removal will be easier than all those that are retained inside the ear.
When extracting worms that have been generated inside the ear, and you treat it with what we said in the division and in the treatise of distillations, and your treatment does not work; then you must examine the ear in the sun. If therefore something of the worms appears there, then remove it with forceps or with fine hooks.
If however nothing appears there, then take a cannula of this form, narrow below and wide above; and introduce the fine end into the ear to the extent that the patient can tolerate it; then suck strongly with it. Do this several times until all the worms have come out.
If however they do not obey you with their exit, then wrap some wax around the circumference of the cannula as I said for the stone.
If they do not come out with what we have said, then apply the distillations that the ancients already knew for killing worms; and you will find it in the treatise of distillations.
And let your discharge be with oil and medicines poured into the ear using this instrument, whose form is depicted here. Make it like you see it from silver or brass, narrow below with a small hole, wide above.
Let the plunger that is inside the cavity of the pipe be eccentrically made from brass. And if you wish, take a rod and wrap its end with cotton suitably; then pour oil or juice or any of these medicines that you want into the pipe; let it be placed in the ear; then insert the rod with the cotton above and express it by compressing it with your hand moderately until oil is pushed into the cavity of the hearing organ, and the patient feels it inside; and what is infused into the ear should now be heated a little over fire: and avoid anything that drips cold into it; for medicines do not tolerate such.
Chapter 7.
§ On the cure of obstruction of the ear.
Sometimes some infants leave their mothers' wombs unpierced.
And it also happens that some people have blockage of the ear due to injury or tissue growing inside them.
And this blockage sometimes occurs at the bottom of the ear cavity which sight cannot follow; and sometimes it happens towards its outer openings a little, where sight can trace them.
Therefore the one which occurs at the bottom of the ear and which sight cannot grasp is difficult to heal in most cases.
And for the one which follows vision, you must place the patient's ear in the sun, and look into it; then if you see an opaque appearance, open it with a fine spatula whose form is as follows, its end being somewhat wide and slightly pointed: and let the remaining part of the spatula be two smooth sides so that it does not injure the ear.
If therefore the opacification is flesh born in the ear canal: then grasp it with a fine hook, and cut it gently but firmly until all the flesh is removed.
But if the opacity is deep within the ear: then take a fine and smooth probe, heat it with fire for a moment, and insert it into the ear canal.
If you perceive the opacity with the instrument which the patient found difficult to hear, attempt its opening gently; and be careful not to injure the ear nerves.
Then place into the ear a linen plug to fit the size of the opening you have wrapped with Egyptian ointment; do this for several days until you are sure that the wound has healed.
Or take linen, soak it in rosewater, sprinkle over it some pounded Egyptian ointment powder and use this.
But if you see the ear already forming a pustule, then remove the linen and leave it with another piece of linen which has been wrapped in cerate made from rose oil until the abscess becomes warm; after that take care of it until it is healed.
If however there is an outflow of blood, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it over the ear; and use the rest of the treatment described previously in the section on distillations to stop bleeding.
The treatment for inflammation of the eyelids.
Chapter 8.
These inflammations which occur on the eyelids of the eye, sometimes are wet, and sometimes they are dry.
Therefore you should hold them with forceps; or with a hook, and cut them out from their roots with a small knife. Then place on the affected area some powdered ginger.
But if any blood flows from them, then cauterise them with a fine lens-shaped cautery iron.
And to cauterise them is best after cutting. For often they return when some of their root remains. And when they are cauterised with fire, those roots are burnt, and do not return.
The treatment for pimples on the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter 9.
It often happens on the eyelids that there is something similar to a pimple in its strength and hardness, and for this reason it is called a pimple; and it is an accumulation of thick moisture on both upper and lower eyelids.
The operation for them is as follows: you should then consider whether it is a pimple proceeding from the eyelid which appears on its surface and moves around in every direction, and whose substance is soft.
Therefore make an incision across it lengthwise. Then scrape out its contents from every side with ease until nothing remains. Then hang it with a hook and cut it off.
However, if its removal is not prepared for you unless after you penetrate the eyelid with a slight incision, then it harms the patient somehow.
But if the crack is large: then gather it with stitches: and take care of it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For the ointment restores it and fleshes it out.
And if the pebble is sloping towards the inner depths of the ear towards its interior surface, then turn the eyelid back and hang the pebble with a hook; besides which you need an incision, and press it from every side.
But if the eyelid is stretched by an incision, it does not harm at all.
Then wash the eye after the pebble's incision with saltwater: and take care of the place along with that which fleshes it out until the patient heals.
On curing axillary swelling in eyelids.
Chapter 10.
Axillary swelling is fat that forms under the eyelids of the upper eyelid; it happens most often to children, and it burdens their eyes, cataracts occur in them at daybreak, and they are unable to look at sunlight because tears run into their eyes, for this reason you see them lying down on their faces all the time, and sleeping on them, and their eyelids under their eyebrows are wet, which inflammation has already covered; and indeed the eminence of Axillary Swelling is apparent to the eye, and when you press that place with your fingers, you feel axillary swelling between them.
The method of this operation is for the patient to place their head on your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare some lint on it, and make from this lint a circle of the same size as the area around the navel on all sides. Then place this on them and press with your fingers from all sides so that the navel gathers in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of this moisture with a knife-like scalpel for splits across its width and do not make a split greater than that made during bloodletting in depth.
Or you should split the skin entirely until you reach the navel because in many cases the navel proceeds from a split according to the shape of a piece of white suet.
Extract it therefore with a cloth that you have saturated between your fingers to the outside and turn your hand back and forth until it has been dispersed and take care not to insert into any splits so as not to reach the eye with your knife-like scalpel and damage it.
But if the navel does not appear to you at the start of the split then insert into it a little with some ease until the navel proceeds. Then extract it as we have said.
Then immerse the cloths in vinegar and water and place them on this spot, and bind it with bandages.
And from some people there are those who rub salt and place it into the cavity of this split so that what remains of this moisture is dissolved.
Then treat it until it has healed.
If there is a warm abscess on a location, treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
The ways of the twitching eye, that is, when the eyelid softens the eye, causing it to wrinkle as it shouldn't.
Chapter 11.
When extra hairs grow on the eyelids beyond the natural course of hair growth and they thicken: these then harm the eye and cause symptoms such as frequent tears, softening of the eyelids, and whiteness until it becomes a reason for loss of sight.
The twitching eye is indeed treated in four ways. Or by cauterisation with fire. Or with sharp medicine, as mentioned earlier in the chapter on cauterisation. The twitching eye is treated by incision and suture. Or with tubes, as I will explain.
It is necessary to place the patient's head in your lap. Then turn the eye's eyelid with your left hand.
If it is thus turned, that is good. And if not: introduce a needle into it, which has a thread attached from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle with thread penetrate from above, and have this be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid where the hair grows: and draw the thread upwards along with the eyelid, and turn it with your finger.
Then find on the inner side of the eyelid under the added hairs with a spatula inserted from the greater tear duct to the lesser tear duct.
Then extract the thread and place underneath a small piece of cotton or cloth; then mark over the eyelid with hot wax in the form of a myrtle leaf.
However it is necessary that the shape be made according to its size and elevation of the eyelid; since this differs among people.
For there are some who require that an incision be made from the eyelid of significant size according to the amount which softens from the eyelid.
Then with a spatula find the two lines which you marked and begin from the greater tear duct to the lesser tear duct and let there be one fissure close by the natural hairs, of about the thickness of a spoke.
Then introduce a hook into one of two corners of the skin. Then scrape all of it off. Then bring together the two lips with a needle and fine wool thread; wipe off any blood and stick down any excess threads over the eyebrows using some sticky stuff if you wish to do so; otherwise don't worry about it.
Then leave the suture and threads in place for three or four days; afterwards care for it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without suture and care for it with something that dries and tightens it. For the eyelid will be raised by the seal of the wound and its closing; but it shall be better still.
The ancients taught this method of treatment.
However, there is concern for the patient, and it results from good work, and there is no fear in it.
And there is another method in Ataxmir as well: it is to make a sign on the eyelid in the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we described.
Then lift the eyelid with three separate or combined hooks, according to this shape that follows, then cut away what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this shape, with equal cutting.
But if you are unable to hold the hook and it does not equalize: then take a needle in which is a thread, and insert it into the middle of the figure; and insert another thread near the larger lachrymal, and a second thread near the smaller lachrymal; and gather between your fingers the threads with equality.
Then lift your hand with equal elevation; and lift the skin over which is marked the boil, as we described.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with stitches; and take care of it until it is healed.
If however an abscess occurs at your incision or a crack: soothe that abscess with a compress and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens also to the eyelid, that its hairs turn inwards.
It is therefore necessary that you use on it what we said about incisions; but stitching is more artistic for both. On the treatment of Ataxmir with tubes.
And it is necessary that you turn back the eyelid and split open the wound inside, as I have described. Then craft two thin canes or sticks with a length equal to the eyelid and a width less than that of a spatula, and you have already cut into both ends on either side where you hold the thread.
Then gather each of them carefully over the excess eyelid tissue and tightly bind together both canes on either side with a strong strapping, leaving it in place for several days. For the skin will die off once bound, it will darken, and rot away until it falls by itself.
If then you delay, cut it with scissors. Then care for it until it heals.
Once consolidated, lift the eyelid, but do not pluck any hairs.
And this is the form of two canes which you will make according to this figure, with respect to length and width.
Chapter 12.
Regarding the removal of hairs inside the eye using a needle.
The procedure is to take up a thin needle and thread it with fine, soft silk. Then gather its ends together and knot each with a very fine knot; the length of thread should be one palm.
Then fasten another thin thread with flax underneath at the short end, and knot its ends if you like.
Then have the patient place their head on your lap and be close to the sun so that the operation is illuminated: since it is a delicate or minute operation.
Then introduce the needle with the thread into the root of the eyelid and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract it above a natural eyelash.
Then pull the eyelash upwards using the thread and introduce that eyelash into the needle; if there is one or two or three, no more.
Then pull your hand with it until the eyelash exits along with the thread from the eyelid alongside a natural eyelash.
But if the thread is pulled and the eyelash does not exit with it, pull the thread downwards with the filament you have tied onto it until the thread exits from the upper hole and see it by eye; then pull the thread and filament and extract both of them, close the eyelid and leave it closed for two or three days until the eyelash attaches to its place and flesh grows over it.
If the eyelash is short then either attach to it a long one from the natural eyelashes and lift them together or leave it until it grows longer and then raise it.
Chapter 13.
When this abscess occurs in the eye it is called a Leporina.
And this abscess is either natural or accidental.
An accidental abscess thus arises from the healing of an injury or incision or cauterization or similar things.
And the method of operating on it is for you to cut open that union and separate its lips, and place between them a linen covering, and bind it until it is healed.
And you should not use things that dry and constrict for its treatment; for if you do so, the Alxatrati will return worse than it was before. Instead, use things that soften, such as fenugreek: and gargle with water in which althea has been cooked, flaxseed: and diaproton ointment, which has been liquified with one of the oils: and wrap with this linen covering, and treat (the wound) with it.
And your aim is to use all skill at your disposal to ensure it does not heal with the same form as before.
And this operation we have described is not a complete correction of the Alxatrati, so that it does not return with the original shape and form.
§ On the treatment of an Alxatrati which occurs on the lower eyelid; it is a wrinkling or contraction upwards towards the right and left.
Chapter 14.
This Alxatrati which occurs on the lower eyelid is what is truly called an Alxatrati. And it is either natural or accidental.
And the accidental type occurs from a wound, or tear, or burn, or something similar.
And the method of operating on it is to take a needle with double thread and dip it into the flesh; then make it penetrate from left lachrymal bone to right lachrymal bone until the thread comes out at both ends.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with a needle, and cut it with a spatula that is one span wide. So if the shape of the eyelid returns to normal, it is good.
And if not, take a rod and place it in the slit, then turn with it the eyelid: and make two slits on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two slits be from the two corners of the incision until they meet and form an angle; until when they are brought together this shape is made, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount which is its sharp side below from what follows the eyelid. Then bring together the separated parts: or sew it up with wool thread.
Then treat them with what we have said, of medicines that soften, and with lint, until they heal.
And if any aloes fall out from the slit or suture, or are cauterised: then you must make a flat slit underneath the eyelashes of the eyelid, also according to what has preceded. Then separate between the two lips with lint, as we have said.
And the essence of the treatment for aloes, when it is on top or below, is to proceed with the operation according as it has been prepared from the form of the aloes.
For it has many variations in form: and a prudent surgeon prepares his skill with whichever way is possible for him until he returns the shape to its natural form or close to it; and even the patient himself, according as suits him from the operation and instruments in most situations.
Chapter 15.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva, or to the cornea.
Sometimes this adhesion occurs in many people, so that the upper eyelid becomes adhered to the white of the eye, and prevents it from its natural movements.
You should therefore insert the end of a thin stick under the eyelid and lift it up; or extend it with a hook; then cut the adhesion with a very fine spatula which is not sharp like a cutting spatula, but rather blunt: so as not to disturb the patient during the operation, and remove the spatula, and hurt the eye with its sharpness; and let your incision be such that you scrape off that adhesion with ease, until the eyelid returns to its natural shape, and the adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, wash it with water; or distill into it from a sieve made of this, of zinja dissolved with water.
Then separate between the eyelid and the eye with a piece of fine linen; and place over the eye wool soaked in egg white; and after three days use sief and solidatui until it is healed.
On the incision of the nail and the prominence of fleshy tissue over the tear ducts.
Chapter 16.
Know that the nail is of two kinds. Or it is nervous, and is like a hard, thin sieve. Or it is not nervous, similar to white frozen moisture; which when it touches cold iron, or you try to grasp it with a hook, it breaks off, and the hook does not become fixed in it.
Both types do not begin unless from the larger lacrimal, as far as it gradually goes, until it covers vision and prevents light and eye movement.
And the method of operating its incision is: that the patient puts their head on your lap. Then open his eyes and lift the eyelid with your hand. Then gather the claw with a small hook and extend it upwards. Then take a needle and insert into it a hair from a horse, cow or goat; and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the claw: and pierce it with the needle, and tie the claw with the thread, extending it upwards: and scrape with the hair the side of the claw that follows the pupil as if you are plucking it with hair right to its extremity.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the lacrimal with a fine spatula or small scissors: and leave the natural lacrimal flesh so that there is no persistent course of tears from its incision.
And sometimes the claw is extended with just a hook: or with thread, as we have said. Then scrape it with a gentle spatula of this shape.
And be careful not to touch the spatula with the hard tunic, lest there is a rupture in it and the pupil is perforated.
Then after the incision, throw into the eye some crushed salt, or something made of zinjar, and leave it until the next day. Then treat it with whatever is necessary until it heals.
If the hoof is not loose and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor does a hook penetrate it, then this type of hoof condition is often treated with medicines and heals as I described in the section on divisions.
If you wish to cut it, then open the patient's eye and take a fine, soft, blunt spatula of this shape. And scrape the hoof from above with the edge, carefully. If you see that it becomes thin and breaks up, and the edge presses into it, then drop into the patient's eye every hour from your freshly-made infusion of zedoary, or red lead, or bruised salt: and bind their eyes until the next day.
Then repeat this procedure over it until all of it is removed. Unless there is also a hot abscess in their eye; then leave it alone and treat the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat this procedure over it with the edge until it heals.
However, if a lump of the tear duct flesh is causing great harm to the eye, hang it with a hook and cut off its end; but do not go too far in your cutting so as to avoid damaging the tear ducts.
Then drop into the tear duct some red lead or an infusion made from zedoary until it heals.
§ On cutting the Alguardeen ulcer and flesh growing in the eye.
Chapter Seventeen.
A reddish fleshy growth is sometimes born in the eyes of some humans, either composed on top or overlapping until it covers the pupil, approaches, or constricts above the eyelids and perhaps turns the eyelids outwards; and it resembles a rose bay.
And the operation on this is also such that the patient should put their head in your lap. Then open their eyes and gather all that flesh with suitable hooks; hold it with one pair of forceps or tweezers.
Then cut the flesh first until all of it is finished with a scalpel. And take care to avoid the eye so that you do not injure it during the operation; and let your cut be with a lancet, which we described in cutting fingernails, or with small scissors like those used for trimming eyelashes, as will come according to its form after this chapter.
When your cut is complete and all the flesh has been removed, then fill the eye with ground salt; or distill into it red powder and similar corrosive medicines; and put on the outside of the eye a fresh egg white, so that it may be protected from hot pus.
But if any of the flesh remains and hot blood rules you, and you fear a hot pus; then leave the eye alone and treat it with what soothes the pus.
Then repeat over it with an operation until it is healed.
And do likewise with additional flesh that happens to appear in the eye of this type.
However, it is necessary that you treat all weak eyes by surgery using iron instruments, either via the natural route or via an accidental one; and do not insert your hand into any of these operations until you have seen that that limb can tolerate that operation.
On the discharge of pus from the eye.
Chapter 18.
Pus is red veins that form over the eye, preventing its function; and it weakens the eye with each passing day.
You must therefore first consider whether the eye with pus is strong and has no other illness besides the pus; then you can discharge that pus from it.
And you should instruct the patient to place their head in your lap.
Then you should lift those veins with one hook or two according to your discretion; and let the hooks be slender doubled ones of this shape, or two graded into one body of this shape.
Then you should collect those veins with slender pincers carefully and wipe the blood away every hour until you see that they have been removed from the eye and broken up; and be careful not to injure the eye with the ends of the spatulas.
And you should conduct your surgery at midday under the sun's rays and be very strong in your actions; so that you do not cut except for those veins.
And when you have finished, then you should instill into the eye either green or red sieved liquid; so that its sharpness eats away at whatever is left of the pus.
If you cannot collect it all in that hour, then apply eye-plasters to it with what will ease the eye pain; leave it for a few days until its pain subsides and is sealed off from pus by heat, then repeat this procedure on it until it heals.
And here's the form of the scissors.
On setting an arm bone back into place at the nose.
Chapter 19.
Physicians call an Arissati fistula which you treat with the cauterizer or with a burning caustic medicine according to what has preceded its account.
And if it does not heal, then there is no skill in it; unless you cut over the abscess at its birth and extract all the moisture that is in it and the pus until the bone is revealed.
When therefore the bone is revealed and you see it corrupted or blackened, then scrape it with an instrument whose form is this:
And it is called the rough-head.
Let it be made of hardened iron and let its head be round like an awl which is pitted with a file or the fine pitting of a chisel; and place it over the corrupted part of the bone.
Then roll it between your fingers and press your hand for a while until you know that the corruption is now scraped off; and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying, styptic medicines.
If therefore the place is consolidated and flesh grows there and the course of pus is cut off; and it remains for forty days without renewal or abscessing or mishap then you will know that it has healed.
And if not, then there is no skill in him unless he can bring the fistula to the nostril opening, according to this form.
And it is to uncover the bone from the second with iron or sharp medicine; and when the bone is uncovered, then take a drill in this form, let its tip be iron, triangular: and its wood should be a pyramid that tapers off at one end as you see.
Then place that over the bone itself, let it be close to the tear duct; and extend your hand away from the eye a little. Then turn your hand with the drill until it penetrates the bone and you feel weakness there; by this air escaping from that place when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore that place with astringent drying medicines as I taught you.
When flesh forms then hardens in that place, the matter which was discharged outwards returns to the nose; and thus it is lighter on the weak spot.
§ On reducing eye prominence.
Chapter 20.
When the eye is prominent with its entire self and no harm or loss of sight occurs, then you must relieve this weakness; thereafter bleed it; afterward place leeches on the neck without any incision but only by their suction.
Then apply over the eye an ointment made from gum arabic (acacia), aloe vera gel, frankincense resin and scotch myrtle resin. Then bind the eye over with a plaster containing many fine feathers.
The method of dressing with compresses is as follows: take soft cloths, many of them, and fold each cloth four times; make many such folds; then place them one on top of the other according to the size required for the eye; finally, bind them firmly in place with a hard cloth.
Repeat the cupping with suction without scarifying.
Then remove the cups, and leave the eye bound day and night.
If however the bandage becomes loose again, it must be tightened considerably; then remove the bandage after a day and night, as we have said.
If therefore you see that the eye has regained its normal colour, it is good; and if not, then repeat the poultice, compresses, binding, and cupping until it is healed.
On incising the white of the eye.
Chapter 21.
When a rupture occurs in the white of the eye and then protrudes outside the eyelids like a grain of white matter, disfiguring the face because of it and prompting you to want to cut it, an operation is necessary on it as follows: it must be done by inserting a needle into the root of the white from below upwards.
Then introduce another needle with doubled thread from the tear duct side and let it penetrate; leave the first needle where it is.
Then cut the doubled thread and bind with it the white around the needle; tie it very tight.
Then extract the needle and place over the eye wool soaked in albumen of egg; leave it until the thread comes away with the white.
Then treat the eye with something that strengthens it until the wound heals.
And sometimes, when the eye is pressed from all sides with a circular cloth until it produces a great swelling.
Then it is pierced with a thin needle that reaches the depth of the eye, and the white liquid flows out, and the eye collapses, and you must enter it immediately; then press on it until it heals.
On the cure of alcomnia.
Chapter XXII.
This disease called alcomnia is nothing but pus that accumulates in the eye, like water dripping down, and it is not itself.
And the way to treat it is to have the patient sit on a prepared seat or remain upright.
Then take his head in your hands from both sides, and move it until you see pus reach the lower part of the eye with your own eyes. Then it is bound so that it does not flow down, and the vision is restored; and the patient sees things as he used to see them: and if it does not go below [the eye], know that it is water.
If however the pus does not descend as we have said, then have the patient sit between your hands. Then take a thin needle and search with it above the corneal flap near the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea until pus comes out.
When it does come out, then drip into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey; or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey; then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
The treatment for water coming down into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
I have already mentioned the different types of water in division, and what is suitable for boring with words that declare and explain it, then take the same according to health.
Therefore, you should make the patient sit opposite your hands on a square facing a light near the sun: and tie their healthy eye, pulling it tightly closed; then with your right hand close the eyelid of their left eye if that is where the water is, or the right eye if it is in the right.
Then take some alum with your right hand if the eye is left, or your left hand if it is right.
Next place the tip of the alum close to the white part of the eye near the edge of the pupil; then push it gently and revolve your hand with it until you feel that the alum has reached the empty space.
The distance that the alum travels must correspond to the depth of the space between the pupil and the end of the black part, which is the crown of the eye. You will see it within the pupil because of the brightness of the cornea.
Next, place the alum at the spot where the water is located. Then push it downwards with one finger after another.
If the water comes down immediately, then as soon as the patient opens their eyes they will be able to see it alongside the alum in their eye.
Then rest for a little while. If therefore water descends, then place it down again, except that almagda comes out of it.
When therefore it is mixed, and does not rise up: then extract the almagda with ease; and you turn your hand little by little.
Then dissolve something of clear crystal salt in water; and wash the eye within with it. Then place outside the eye a whole piece of cloth or wool dipped in rose oil and egg white; and bind with it the healthy eye.
We however, and those who are of our time, place on it powdered cumin with egg white.
If almagda does not obey you and enter the eye due to its hardness, since it is of human origin, whose eye is very hard; then you must take a spoon called an *alberid*, which has this form:
Therefore pierce with it only the conjunctiva and do not push your hand into the hole; and there is nothing else for you to do except make a way to the almagda in a subtle place.
Then introduce the almagda as we have said.
And at the end of your work, prepare for the patient a decently-made bedroom in which they can sleep on their back in a dark house; and prevent them from all movement and coughing: and give them food that soothes their nature; and do not let them move their head to the right or left at all.
The bandage should remain in place according to its placement until the third day.
Then you must loosen it in that dark room, and test his sight; make him see things. Then apply the bandage again up to the seventh day.
And it is not necessary to do so at the time of treatment or immediately after the operation; rather one must avoid it because water rises quickly through intense heat.
If however a warm abscess occurs then one must loosen the eye dressing before the seventh day and treat it with what will settle the abscess until settled; after that restore his sight completely and place over his face some sort of visual aid to regulate his vision for a few days, while he is in that dark room.
Then let him leave the house gradually and return to his home.
And remember that the student is not to be exempted from the sight of the procedure on several occasions because he will then dare to perform the surgery himself.
And now one of them has come to us from Alayra who said that a water-drawing woman was perforated in Alayra.
I therefore have not seen anyone in your land do such a thing nor read about it in any of the ancient books and so it is possible that this may be something new.
These are the different kinds of covers for the eye as they should be applied by someone who is ignorant of such things.
They should be made from copper and the ends must have this same pointed triangular shape and sharp point.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
Occasionally various fleshly growths appear in the nose, one of which resembles a scorpion with many feet; this is carcinomatous flesh, stony to the touch, dark-coloured: and there is also soft flesh that isn't dark-coloured.
So when you have soft, trustworthy flesh that isn't carcinomatous, you should make the patient sit between your hands facing the sun with his nostrils open; then cast a hook into those growths and draw them out; then cut what you've got hold of on one side with a sharp thin spatula until you're sure all the flesh is removed.
But if there's something left which can't be cut off, then scrape it away with one of your fine instruments as painlessly as possible until nothing remains of it. But if there's a lot of bleeding or the patient gets a hot abscess, then give him whatever treatment is necessary; but if it's one of those malignant kinds of abscess then burn it quickly with a red-hot iron until all the blood stops and every bit of flesh is removed.
Then after you've cut it out, pour vinegar or water into the nose, or wine. But if the patient has an open nose with a discharge down to his palate, then you know he's healthy again.
If there's no discharge as you'd expect then you know the flesh has grown inside on the upper bones of the nose to which your instruments don't reach with their cutting.
You should then introduce a thread of linen with some thickness and make many knots in it; place between each knot the length of a finger or less; have the patient insert one end of the thread into their nose together with a stick or similar object that is possible for him, after you've made it similar to an arrow; pull on the other end until it reaches the wound and comes out over his throat.
And indeed many times boys do the same thing like this at school: and it is an easy task for whoever wants to.
Then place two ends of the thread: one end that comes out over the nose, and the other which remained inside the nose. Then with your assistant close the wound with the flesh-hook using the knots which are on the thread. Do this until you're sure that the flesh is now tied with the knots of the thread.
Then remove the thread and place in the nose a linen cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment after cleaning away any blood; do this for three days or more until all of the remaining ointment is worn away by the flesh.
Then finally put into the nose at the end of the treatment a leaden tube for several days until it heals.
If however he requires drying treatment, use the following method:
For the device used for distillation of oils and other medicines into the nose – make this out of silver or brass; it should be like a small lantern with an opening similar to that of a shell, and its nozzle likewise.
And if you wish make it closed like a tube.
And the small shell of the instrument for making a head purging, let it be full and exposed: which has a handle at its end, with which you hold it when you heat oil in it, or whatever liquid or fluid.
Chapter 25.
Often there is born at the end of the nose a lump which becomes larger, and increases with days until the appearance of it becomes ugly.
And because of that it is necessary to cauterize it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all of it. Then put over the place either a cautery or burning medicine which stands in its place.
If therefore the incision of it is delayed until it has grown larger, then consider whether it is stony hard, dark in colour and insensitive; then do not touch it with the iron, for the ulcer is cancerous.
For I have often seen one who incises this ulcer, and there happens great harm to him who has it.
And if the ulcer is soft to the touch, not dark in colour, and you see that incision of it all over is possible, then evacuate the pus, and incise it without fear or dread; and treat the place with what dries and contracts it until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
On the suture of the nose, lip, and ear.
You know that when a break in continuity occurs in one of these cartilage pieces, surgery is of little benefit to it, except for some people.
One should therefore, when something like that happens to someone, consider whether it is a fresh wound with its blood; and then bring together the lips of the wound with suture; then treat the wound until it heals.
And if the break in continuity has lips already separated, and each part is made healthy; then one should scrape all parts from their skin until it bleeds. Then bring together the two lips with suture and tighten them, and sprinkle over the blood dragon's blood and crushed frankincense; and place on top of the powders a piece of dauphine plaster, and another from the consolidated plasters; and leave it tied for two or three days. Then loosen it, and change the medicine; and leave it until the threads fall off by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suture is to bring together the break in continuity either with needles as we described for the abdomen; or bring together a break using thread as I have made you aware.
Chapter 27.
On removing lumps of lips.
Many people often have hard growths like herb seeds inside their lips; and some are smaller, others larger.
One should therefore turn the lip over, and cut into every lump of it; then lift it with a hook, and cut it from all sides.
Fill the place after the incision with a mixture of egg yolk and powdered gallnut until the bleeding stops.
Next, it should be washed with vinegar, and the place where there is a styptic effect should be treated with it until the wounds heal.
Chapter 28.
§ On cutting flesh born on the gum.
Many times flesh grows on the gum which the Ancients call Abuluss.
Therefore you should hang it with a hook or hold it with forceps and cut it at its root allowing the pus to flow out, or blood if that is what there is.
Then apply over the place a mixture ground up, or other styptic, drying powders.
But if that flesh returns after healing as it often does then cut it a second time and cauterise it for it will not return after being cauterised.
Chapter 29.
§ On the treatment of teeth with an iron instrument.
Sometimes rough, ugly bark-like matter accumulates on the surface of teeth from within and without as well as between the gums; sometimes they turn black or yellow then green until that corruption spreads to the gum making the teeth unsightly.
You should make the patient sit between your hands with their head in your lap then scrape away with a knife any bark-like matter you see as well as that which looks like sand: do not stop until nothing is left and do likewise for blackness, greenness and yellowness until they are clean.
If therefore that which is in them from the beginning of reason be removed, it is good; and if not, then repeat reasoning upon them on another day, a second and third time, until finally you arrive at what you wish to study.
And you should know that razors of various forms and many shapes are needed; as is prepared for your operation: for the razor with which the tooth is shaved from below is different to the one used between teeth, according to other shapes.
And these forms of razors should be prepared for you as you see them.
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of toothache.
You should take care to cure the tooth of its pain with all your skill, and proceed slowly in its extraction. For there is no substitute for it when extracted; since it is a noble substance: until such time as there can be no excuse for not extracting it, then when the patient is compelled to extract it, you should make sure and confirm that the painful tooth is indeed the culprit.
For often the pain deceives the patient, and they think it is a healthy tooth and extract it; yet the pain does not go away until the sick tooth is removed.
We ourselves have seen this from the operations of blood-letters.
When therefore you are sure that the painful tooth itself is to blame, then you should make an incision around it with a small spatula, in which there is something sharp, until the gum is loosened on all sides; then move it with your fingers, or with fine forceps first little by little, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps on those places in a good position, and hold the patient's head between your knees; now you have held them well, so that they do not move; then pull the tooth according to its straightness, so as not to break it.
If it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it underneath from all sides gently, and try to move it as you did initially.
However, if the tooth is perforated or corroded, you must fill that hole with cloth; and tie it tightly with a thin piece of stick so that it does not break when you grasp it with forceps.
And make sure that you carefully scrape around the gum from all sides.
You must also be careful with your strength so that you do not break the tooth and a piece of it remains inside causing worse pain for the patient than before.
Beware of doing what ignorant bloodletters do with their haste and boldness when extracting teeth, other than the methods we have described above.
For often they bring about great harm to people when they break teeth and leave some or all of the roots behind; or extract them along with parts of the jawbone, as we have seen many times.
After extraction, it should be rinsed with wine or with vinegar and salt.
If bleeding occurs from the site of extraction (which often happens), then take some powdered frankincense and fill that place with it; if not, cauterize it with a hot iron if you do not have any frankincense.
The shapes of fine forceps with which the tooth is first moved should be long at their tips and short at the handle, thick so that they do not double up when pressed against a tooth: you will see them made of thicker handles, yet when you squeeze them together, they will not pinch themselves nor bend; the tips are to be short and made out of iron or good steel with straight tips. There should be teeth on these, some of which fit into each other and close firmly with a strong grip.
And sometimes the tips should be made in the shape of the beak of a bird called a stork, which has a strong grip.
Chapter 31.
Regarding tooth removal, as well as extraction of fractured jaw bones...
When a bit of tooth remains and needs to be removed, you should place over the site a poultice with butter for one or two days until the area softens; then introduce into it an algest or forceps: these have tips that are shaped like the beak of a bird called a stork. The shape of these forceps is as follows...
Their tips should be made like a file on the inside; or like an awl.
If it does not obey you and come out with these forceps, then you should cut over the root and remove all the flesh, and introduce a small instrument called a palanca, of this shape...
Its tip should be short, thick but not too much so that it does not break.
If the root comes out through it then that is good; if not, you should help with these other instruments of which the shapes are as follows...
The shape of the first triangular end, which has some thickness.
The shape of the thin triangular end.
And together with other instruments and tools that have preceded it in regard to teeth extraction.
It is also helpful to use this instrument which resembles a large hook, of the following shape - twisted triangular end with some thickness so that it does not break; and ensure it is not soaked.
And know that there are many dental instruments; similarly other tools cannot be counted and a skilful practitioner, skilled in their craft, will invent for themselves the tool which is signified to them by the task and specific illness.
Since from the many [teeth] there are some for which an instrument has not been described by previous authors owing to their diversity of type.
If however a jawbone or other bone of the mouth is broken or has decayed then examine it in its place with that which suits him from amongst the instruments and forceps I have described for extracting roots; and assist using a lever of this shape.
There should be some thickness to it gradually so that the bone is grasped or held and does not slip away nor slide until the bone comes out; and restore the place with suitable medications for it.
But if there is slight decay in the bone then scrape it from its decay and treat it until it becomes healthy.
Chapter 32.
On locking prominent teeth above others.
When teeth emerge not according to their natural course, it becomes an ugly deformity; especially when this happens with women and girls, namely those of noble birth.
It is necessary that you give consideration to it. For if a tooth is born after another and does not find its place, or its socket is impossible as well as the filing of it; then pull out this tooth: and if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it together with that other using this instrument which has this form.
It is like a small beak, and it is made of very sharp steel.
And let your incision be made over many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that no other may be moved from among the teeth.
But if it is an exposed prominent tooth that needs filing; then file it with a steel file whose form is this.
It is all made of steel, and its handle has very small points like the file with which needles are made: with this the tooth is gradually filed over many days easily so that it does not move nor fall out; then soothe it afterwards and finally shave it smooth using some scrapers.
If there is a tooth from which a part has broken and it injures the tongue; then you must also file that one until its roughness caused by breakage be removed and levelled; and smoothen it so that it does not injure the tongue nor corrupt speech.
Chapter 33.
Regarding setting teeth with gold wires.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and commotion due to blows or accidents: and the patient is unable to chew without food falling out; and you have treated them with astringent medicines, but the treatment was not successful; then it is appropriate to bind them with gold wire: since silver turns green and decays after some days, while gold never changes.
And let the wire be of moderate thickness and fineness according to the amount that fits between the teeth.
The form of the binding is as follows: take a length of wire and insert twice its thickness between two healthy teeth; then weave it with both ends of the wire around the loose tooth or teeth until you reach the healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving process back to where you started; and tighten your hand with care and wisdom until it cannot be moved at all: and make sure that your binding is tight around the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose.
Cut off the excess ends of the wire with scissors; then gather both together and bend them so they are concealed between a healthy tooth and a loose one, taking care not to damage the tongue; finally, leave the binding in place until healing is complete.
If it comes loose or breaks, bind them with another wire, and it will remain in place for the whole period of healing.
This is the arrangement of teeth and form of binding for two healthy teeth and two loose ones, as you can see.
Sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their place after falling out and are retained as we have described; but this is only done by a skilled and experienced practitioner.
And sometimes a replacement is made from calf bones; it takes the form of a tooth and is placed where the missing tooth was secured and fastened, as we have said; it remains there and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Sometimes this ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, resulting from a wound that has already healed.
And the operation for this is to open the mouth of the patient as wide as you can with your hand, then lift their tongue; next, cut that sinewy ligament horizontally until the tongue is freed from its restraint.
If there is any hardening and knotting in it due to the healing of a wound, then place a hook on this area and pass the hook across horizontally until you cut through the ligament and release the knot.
And take care not to make an incision too deep into the flesh so as not to cut through the artery there and cause a flow of blood.
Then let the trace of the incision be rubbed with rosewater or vinegar or cold water.
Next place some flaxen lint under the tongue which the patient should hold there every night; this will prevent a second healing.
Should any flow of blood occur, then place on the area some pounded egg white. But if the bleeding defeats you, then cauterize the area with a fig-shaped cauterizer appropriate to this; next treat it with the remaining cure until it heals.
Chapter 35.
Sometimes there occurs under the tongue a pustule resembling a small frog, preventing the tongue from performing its natural function; and perhaps it grows so large that it fills the mouth.
And for treating this condition, you should open up the patient's mouth facing towards the sun: then inspect the pustule.
If you see that it is of a dark or black colour, hard, and the patient feels no pain upon touching it; then do not touch it for it is cancerous.
And if it slopes towards whiteness where there is moisture, then thrust into it a hook, and cut it out with a thin scalpel, freeing the whole of it. If blood defeats you during the procedure, then place on it bruised figwort until the bleeding ceases; afterwards return to your surgery, until you have removed it and all of its contents.
Afterwards it should be treated with vinegar and salt: then tend to the wound using appropriate dressings until it is healed.
The treatment of an abscess in both tonsils.
Chapter 36.
Sometimes there occur between the throat and the jaw, two glands resembling cotton balls that sit externally; these are called tonsils: when you treat them with what we have described elsewhere and they do not heal, then consider the following.
If it is an abscess that is hard, dark in colour, with little sensation upon touching; then do not touch it with the iron [scalpel].
If it is red in color and its root is thick, then do not touch it even with an iron [instrument], fearing a gush of blood; rather leave it until it matures. Then either you will pierce it, or it will rupture by itself.
And if it is white in colour, round, and its root is slender; then you must cut into it.
And the procedure is as follows: consider before performing it whether its abscess has cooled to complete quiet or diminished somewhat; then make the patient sit facing the sun with his head in your lap and open his mouth so that a servant takes hold of [the tongue] between his hands, pressing it down with an instrument of this shape.
It shall be made of silver or copper, thin as a knife.
When you press it against his tongue with this and the abscess is revealed to you, catching your eye: then take a hook and fasten it into the tonsil and draw it outwards as far as you can without tearing anything from its coverings.
Then cut into it with an instrument of the following shape: It resembles scissors; however, its tips are curved so that the opening of each is directed to the point of the other, and care should be taken that it be made of iron-inlaid silver or bronze dipped in alum.
If this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula of this shape: Let one side be sharp, the other blunt.
And after you cut one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner.
Then after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
But if there is bleeding, let him gargle with water in which boiled pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves and similar astringents have been steeped until the bleeding stops; then treat it until it heals.
And sometimes other abscesses appear in the throat besides the two tonsils. Cut them accordingly, as I have said about the incisions of the two tonsils.
Indeed, I once treated a woman for an abscess that had formed in her throat and was causing it to swell, with very little movement; the swelling almost completely blocked her throat, so she could only breathe through a narrow opening. She could no longer eat or drink water, and she would have died within a day or two if left untreated.
The abscess had grown so much that it sent out two branches until they came out through the nostrils.
I therefore hurriedly seized one of them with forceps. Then I pulled it out, drawing forth a large portion of the abscess.
I then cut through it where I could reach from within the nostril.
Then I did the same for what had come out of the other nostril.
Then I opened her mouth and pressed down on her tongue; afterwards, I seized the forceps at that abscess, cut a portion of it away without much bleeding, so her throat was cleared out. The woman could immediately drink water again and then she started eating.
I did not cease to cut out that abscess many times over a long period; and an abscess followed in the place of each one I cut out until it became too extensive along with the one around it.
I devised a plan and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
I stood back from adding anything; then I walked away from that side, and did not know what God did with it after me.
§ On the cutting out of an abscessed uvula.
Chapter 37.
When a discharge flows down to the uvula and an abscess forms there, and it is long; then it itself is called "perpendicular"; and if it is on the lower part thick and round, then it itself is called "uva".
When it is treated with what we have said in the division, and the cure does not come about; and you see that the abscess has cooled off from that, and it is thin; then you must cut it out.
And that which is on it round and bunched together, and has no length, and is bloody or of a dark colour or black, and has no feeling; then you must deepen the cut into it. For in that there is fear over the sick person.
You must therefore when you see it according to the manner I have described from its whiteness and length, make the sick person sit facing the sun; and press his tongue down with an instrument, which has been previously described; then fix a hook into the uvula, and draw it downwards; and cut it off with one of two instruments that I mentioned for cutting out two tonsils.
You must not cut away any part from it unless it is what naturally protrudes without causing an extension.
If you remove more of it, you will harm the voice and speech.
After the incision, use what we have described for incising two tonsils: and treat it until it is healed.
However, if the patient is afraid of the incision: then you must use skill without fear or trepidation when cauterizing him.
The manner of the cautery is not proper unless with boiling water: and it is best to put the patient's head on your lap. Then press his tongue with the instrument that we mentioned. Next, take some of the water that I told you about in the chapter on cauterization, and mix it with unslaked lime, making it neither thick nor thin; fill the hollow part of this instrument, which is shaped like so.
Let the end of it that holds the medicine be hollow like the bowl of a spoon.
Place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself: and let the patient lie on his side so that saliva may flow out of his mouth from within the medicine, so that nothing runs down into his throat and harms him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine over it. You must hold it over the uvula for a space of time equal to half an hour's length until you see that its color has already turned black and the pungency of the medicine has subsided.
If you wish to apply the plaster and wrap it around the end of the radius bone, pour the plaster mixed with medicine into a tube, and insert the radius with the cast inside the tube until the plaster adheres to the wound.
Do this many times until you achieve the desired result with regard to cauterizing the wound. Then leave it; for it dries up and falls off by itself after three or four days.
If you need to apply more medicine, do so; and after the cauterization, clean around the wound with a plaster soaked in butter, and cleanse it with whatever medicinal ointment is available; then wash it with cold water.
And treat the wound externally with embrocations (ointments) and internally with gargles (mouthwashes), until it is healed.
This type of treatment, then, is more beneficial than cutting open the wound and less frightening.
Sometimes the wound is even healed with a method gentler than cauterization and incision; it is healed by fumigation (smoke inhalation), as follows. Take chamomile, hyssop, marjoram, rue, savine, wormwood, southernwood and similar herbs; put all or some of them into a glazed pot, cover with vinegar and boil; the pot should have a hole in its middle, over which place an instrument shaped like a ladle.
It should be made of silver or brass; and insert the end with the pomegranate grains into the patient's mouth until the vapours rise to the wound through the tube, so that the wound is greatly darkened.
Repeat this process over it many times until it dries out.
And be careful not to do this treatment at the beginning of the appearance of abscesses. It often adds to the abscess: and you must not do this unless it is at the decline of its hot stage.
