But if you don't have this instrument available to you, then take a reed and attach sheep's skin to its end so the patient's bone is not burnt because the sheep's skin prevents the vapour from burning the bone; and this is good treatment with successful results.
On extracting thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter 38.
Often there is a bone or fish spine or other object stuck in the throat.
You must therefore extract any object that is visible to the naked eye after you press against their tongue with an instrument under a bright light and then tell them what it is stuck in their throat.
And if you cannot see it and it is hidden in the throat, then you must make the patient vomit before their food has been fully digested by the stomach. Perhaps it will come out with whatever is brought up through vomiting.
Or have the patient swallow a piece of turnip, or lettuce root, or a dry rusk; or take a piece of dried soft sea sponge and tie it with thread then have them swallow it. When it reaches the place where there are spines or bones and is extracted, then they come out.
If therefore it does not exit with what we have said, then use a lead instrument in this form.
It should be thicker than a radius and at its end there is a curve that can easily be inserted into his throat; he himself should have his head elevated upwards.
And take care that it does not touch its epiglottis, so that coughing does not happen to him, and push with a bone or spine, or the doctor should insert his hand himself.
Whatever the patient inserts is better: because of his knowledge about what is attached there; and push down or pull up with his hand holding an instrument, all that is prepared for him until it comes out.
On the removal of leeches.
Chapter 39.
When a leech is treated with what I said earlier when dividing the cure by medicines and it does not take hold, then look at the patient's throat facing the sun or near the sun after you have pressed its tongue with an instrument as described to you.
If your sight falls upon the leech, remove it with a small hook or with slender forceps as shown to you.
If it is not possible to do so with those: then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat near the leech; next, place inside the cavity of the tube a red hot iron over fire. Do this many times; let the patient abstain from water for that whole day.
Then give him a cup full of cold water and let him open his mouth into it; have him rinse his mouth with it and do not allow him to swallow anything, he should move the water around in his mouth for an hour with his hand. For as soon as the leech feels water, it drops immediately.
But if it does not leave with what we have described, then fumigate the patient with ground beetles or with asafoetida using the instrument that I described for fumigating the ear.
Do this several times because it will fall out by itself.
And the method of treatment by fumigation is to take a pot containing live plums with fire inside; and the pot should be covered with a lid which has a hole in its centre, and the end of the instrument is fitted into this hole.
Then apply the fumigation and let the patient put his mouth on the end of the tube and close his mouth so that no fumes escape until you know that they have reached the leech. For the leech will fall out immediately.
But if it does not come out, then repeat the fumigation several times.
And let the patient endure thirst, eat spicy food, garlic, onions, but not drink water.
It is therefore necessary that it comes out with this treatment regimen.
And this is the shape of the instrument with which a leech is extracted from the throat when it falls on it with its eyes open.
It is like forceps as you see; only it has this curve which is inserted into the throat. And its end is like the beak of a bird called a stork, in which there are rasping points; when it grasps anything, it never lets go entirely.
On the opening of abscesses, their incision, and treatment in general.
Chapter 40.
There are many types of abscesses, with numerous ways they can develop; this will be recalled when considering one particular type in this book. They vary in how they pierce and how they are incised depending on two methods.
One method is for an abscess that forms within itself and contains humours.
The second method depends on the location of the abscess, which develops from an injury to the body.
Since an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess appearing in the anus, and since one that appears in soft tissue differs from another appearing at a joint; each has its own considerations regarding treatment.
Some abscesses should not be pierced unless they have matured and drained completely; others must be pierced when raw and unripe.
Such as those near joints.
When an abscess occurs near a joint and extends its length until what is within it rots, the surrounding ligaments or nerves of that joint may be corrupted; this is why there's greater cause for that limb to contract.
Or it is an abscess on a principal limb; for if you delay piercing it until it has matured, it will harm that limb or the person.
Or it is near the anus; pierce it while still immature. An unripe abscess should not be pierced unless it's near the anus so that rot doesn't spread deep into and lengthen towards the interior of the anus, creating a fistula or becoming untreatable at its terminal end.
You must know the hour of the abscess's perforation when it has matured according to its fullness; and this is at the settling of pain, retreat of fever, reduction of redness, throbbing, and sharpness of the head of the abscess; and other signs.
And you must make the same perforation in the lower area of the abscess, and the most prominent part; and let this be a piercing that goes lengthwise through the body, if there are abscesses on parts of hands or feet, and in places like arms, wrists, tendons, nerves, arteries, and lastly in all equal locations where there is no curvature.
In those which curve outward, go with the perforation according to that limb.
When the abscess is in fleshy areas, it is better that you postpone its perforation until its digestion has been confirmed, as we have said according to its fullness.
Because if you pierce it before that time, the course of its discharge is prolonged and results in much filth and foulness; possibly its lips are hardened and so is its depth.
Some abscesses are pierced breadthwise through the body when necessary, or according to that limb's needs.
And you should use a small piercing on small abscesses and large ones on big abscesses; or make multiple cuttings depending on the size of the abscess.
There are some abscesses where the skin must be scooped out; and it is to be cut when already made like a piece of cloth and has reached its limit, which means that it is already dead tissue; as happens with many types such as carbuncles and buboes.
And among these are those that are cut with a three-cornered incision. And among them is one which is cut like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like an abscess in the groin.
And among these are those in which a round incision is made, and a lunar one; and similar to them among incisions.
But that which has no head, like soft superficial abscesses, must be pierced with a shallow piercing, or only flat.
And when an abscess is large and has already collected a lot of pus, and you pierce it, do not rush and remove all the pus at that moment; instead, extract some of it. Then dress the abscess up to the next day. Then extract some more of the pus.
Do this many times in stages, until it comes out; especially if the patient is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old decrepit person: for often the animal spirit of one animal is dissolved with the exit of pus: and perhaps the patient will die, but you won't perceive it.
Therefore, take great caution in this chapter.
And after your piercing, with which you pierce these abscesses, it is necessary to wipe the wound; and consider... If therefore the wound of an abscess is small, or it is a flat incision, then apply lint made from linen, or old cotton. And if it is a large abscess and there are many perforations and fissures, then you must insert a licinia into each fissure until they meet.
And if you have cut away part of the skin from the abscess or scooped it out around its circumference, then you should fill it with old cotton, or as when one dresses a wound with cloth, or scrape it with line lint without moisture; and bind it tight for three days.
Then wash it and dress it with the ointment required until it is healed.
But if there is a flow of blood during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after the cloth has soaked them up; and place it on the bleeding spot many times.
But if the flow of blood persists, then you must apply the regimen and treatment and powders which I have described in many places throughout this book.
But if it is wintertime and the location of the abscess involves many nerves, then you must fill poultices with warm wine and oil; and place them on the affected areas.
And if it is summertime and the areas are fleshy, then you must apply poultices soaked in water and oil, or with wine and oil, and keep them all cool. Until the third day, as I have said, you must then open the abscess, clean it, and treat it with what is suitable until it heals.
This is, therefore, what is necessary to know about the universal treatment of abscesses.
According to the path of distinction, I have already spoken about every abscess and how it is a step-by-step way towards its cure individually.
Chapter 41.
On the incision of abscesses that occur on the skin of the head.
There occur on the skin of the head small abscesses, and they are of the types of nodules which are contained by membranes like sacks; it is as if they were chicken scabs themselves.
And there are many types of them.
Among them are fatty ones; and among them are those which contain moisture like mud; and among them are those containing moisture like gruel and porridge; and similar to these; and among them are hard stony ones.
And in the incision of all of them and their extraction there is no fear as long as an artery does not oppose during their incision.
And the operation for their incision is that you try it with an instrument whose shape follows below and which is called "Intromission" until you know what it contains.
For if it is one that contains moisture then dry it by incising it lengthwise with a superficial incision; according to this shape.
And start with the incision from line b to line g. So when the moisture is evacuated then scrape out the sac that contains this moisture and do not leave anything in it at all.
For many times it returns if something remains from it.
Then immerse the gauze in Egyptian ointment if it is available; and if not, in water and salt; and fill with it the wound and leave it until another day. Indeed, it corrodes what remains of the cyst.
Then repeat on it with the second Egyptian poultice, and the third, and if you need it, do so until you are confident that nothing remains of the cyst; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
If the abscess contains a fatty lump, then cut it open in this form, with the incision taking the shape of a cross; and place hooks between the lips of the incision, and scrape it from all sides: and try your best to remove the membrane that contains it.
However, if an artery gives you trouble, then do what I shall tell you next.
And do likewise with an abscess, if it is stony, from the incision and treatment as we have said.
And the incision above the stony abscess is easier, because it has little blood or moisture. And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are on the heads of certain men, with which there is absolutely no moisture.
That is because I cut open an abscess on the head of an elderly woman, and found an abscess like a solid, hard, rough stone which could not be broken; and if anyone struck it with something sharp they would injure themselves.
However, in other abscesses on the head, apart from these abscesses which occur on children's heads and at the roots of ears, their incision should be a superficial one.
Or make your incisions in them always from the lower part of them so as to ease the course of pus downwards.
Then treat it with whatever is appropriate for them from among treatments.
Regarding the removal of goiters in the neck.
Chapter 42.
Many times these abscesses occur in the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groin area: and sometimes they occur elsewhere on the body.
And with regard to those that occur in the neck, they may be singular or multiple: and they form from one another: and each goiter among them is within its own membrane, just as is the case with nodules and head abscesses, as we have described.
And there are many types of these goiters. And indeed some of these goiters are stony; and among them are those that contain fluids; and among them are malignant ones which do not respond to treatment.
In the case of those that you see to be of a good disposition upon feeling them, whose color is close to that of the skin tone, and which move with every part, and are not attached to the neck nerves, nor to the guide-veins, nor to the arteries, and are not deep: it is necessary that you cut these away using a superficial incision from upper body part to lower body part along the line, b. to line, g.; and scrape them clean on all sides; and stretch out two skin flaps with a hook or many hooks if need be, as we said regarding head abscesses; then extract it little by little.
And take caution not to cut into the vein or nerve.
And let the knife not be too sharp; so that it does not cut into your hand during the incision, or disturb the patient, and cause you to cut what is unnecessary.
If you cut a vein or an artery and it prevents you from operating, then apply to the wound ground zege or certain powders that stop the blood flow, compress the wound, and leave it until the swelling subsides and the wound softens: or until it starts and is inclined towards putrefaction.
If the bleeding has been stopped, return to your operation until it is complete.
Then probe with your index finger to see if there is any remaining strophe or small stones; therefore cut them out and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the root of the strophe, then you must not cut that strophe from its root; instead, you should tie it with strong doubled thread and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old cotton that you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then take care of it.
If the strophe is cut through entirely, then you must bring together the two lips of the wound and have them bind themselves immediately once you see that there remains no excess at all.
If you see that there is excess left over due to the size of the strophe, then you must consider cutting and removing its root; and apply the suture we mentioned before.
And those strophes that contain moisture, pierce them with a shallow puncture so that you can see the place of their maturity; and make the puncture below what follows from the body, as we have said before.
Then apply, after the puncture, licinia with ointment and similar things to consume what remains of corruption until you see that the wound has now been cleansed; then treat it with generative flesh ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
On the removal of the epiglottis due to an abscess occurring in the throat.
The ancients called this procedure a 'section' of the epiglottis, and I have never seen anyone in our land perform it; here is their account.
In those with severe sore throat, it is best to avoid the procedure of epiglottis removal; for there will be no relief from it because all the veins and lungs are affected.
In those with a hot abscess in the mouth or throat, or on both tonsils, where there is no disease in the windpipe, it is necessary to perform an epiglottis removal procedure as otherwise they may die from suffocation.
Therefore when we wish to perform it, we must cut the epiglottis below its three or four circles with a small knife across its width in that part which lies between two of these circles; this is the appropriate place for cutting and not at any cartilage: because this place has no flesh attached to it, nor are there veins bearing blood near by.
If the surgeon is timid, it is best to first stretch out the skin of the throat with a hook. He then makes an incision in the skin until he reaches the windpipe; he should avoid any vein which may be seen.
Next he cuts the epiglottis as we have described; and the procedure is signalled by an abundance of pus issuing from it with a breaking apart sound and loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
When the hour passes and pre-gangrene is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound with skin, but without cartilage.
Then apply medicines that promote flesh growth until it is healed.
Albucasis says so. The full explanation of this procedure we have described is only apparent when they see that one of these abscesses has already closed the patient's throat; and the patient is elevated towards death, and wishes to be cut in order to relieve their breathing: they go to cut the epiglottis so the patient can breathe over the site of the wound with a certain type of respiration, and thereby save them from death. And for this reason, they used to advise leaving the wound open until the illness is exhausted; and that takes three days or nearly so. Then, therefore, they advised suturing the wound and treating it until it was healed.
And what I myself have seen is that one maidservant took a knife and inserted it into her throat, and cut a portion of the pulmonary canal with it.
And I was called to treat her, and found that she was moaning like the moan of a dead person.
So I lifted her from the wound, and found that only a small amount of blood had emerged from it. So I was afraid that she herself had not severed the vein nor the artery; and air was escaping from the wound.
I therefore proceeded with the suturing and treated it until it was healthy; and nothing happened to the maidservant apart from a slight hoarseness in her voice: and she returned after some days, having improved.
Hence I say: there is no fear in the incision of the epiglottis.
Regarding the incision for a throat hernia.
Chapter 44.
This abscess, which is called a throat hernia, is a large one, of the same colour as the body; and it occurs frequently in women.
And it is of two kinds: either it is natural, or it is accidental.
Certainly, the natural kind is not innate.
The accidental type, however, is of two kinds; one of which resembles fatty tumours. And the other kind is similar to the abscess that forms from an arterial blockage. There is a risk with this procedure, and it should not be attempted presumptuously with the iron except when it is small; you must try it, and investigate it with your finger. If you find it resembling a fatty tumour and not suspended by any vein, then cut it out as tumours are cut; and extract it with what is contained of the cyst if there is one; but if there is none, then investigate it entirely. Then treat the site with what is required of its healing, until it is cured.
Chapter 45.
Regarding incisions over the kinds of tumours.
There are many kinds of tumours, and I have already spoken about all their kinds in the classification; now it is necessary that I explain here the difference between a Tumour and an Ulceration, since this causes doubt.
I therefore say that when there is exiting heat and fever, as well as violent pains, until the superfluous boiling subsides and putrefaction is complete, then indeed do fever and heat quiet down.
When there is no heat nor fever and the pains contain them within a sifachin bag, which is their own sack, and it matches the colour of the body; and they begin like chickpeas, becoming like a batecha, growing larger or smaller.
And there are two types: either fatty; or they contain moisture. The colors of the humidity are many, as I said before in my classification.
You must therefore, when you come to treating a Knot, examine it and inquire about it first with the instrument called an Intromission, as its form will be described in the chapter that follows.
And the way to inquire about abscesses and all Knots is to take this instrument and insert it into the moistest place you find in the abscess: then rotate your fingers with it gradually until you know that the instrument has now penetrated the skin. Then stretch out your hand according to the size of the abscess.
Then withdraw the Intromission, and consider that which has come out on its track.
If therefore moisture comes out running, whatever its colour: then dry it with a superficial incision, as I said before about other abscesses.
And if moisture does not come out on the Intromission's track, then know that it is fatty: therefore make an incision above it in the form of a cross like this, as I taught you; and suspend it with hooks, scraping the skin all around gently; and keep watch over the bag if you can until the Knot comes out whole.
If the cyst is ruptured during the operation and it is not possible to extract it whole, which often happens, then remove it piece by piece until nothing remains of it.
If any part of the cyst, small or large, is left behind, the lump will return for the most part.
If it overpowers you and a small part remains: then fill the wound at the end of your procedure with some sharp corrosive powders; bind up the wound and apply to it a hot poultice, and treat it with the rest of the cure until it is healed.
If therefore it is a large lump, then its two lips, and treat it with that which promotes flesh growth.
If on the other hand you have a pulsating or non-pulsating vein: then be quick and fill the place with pounded fig; leave it tight for two or three days until the wound decays and the boiling of blood subsides, then return to cutting that which remains of the lump.
Chapter 46.
A note on the instruments required for cutting and piercing.
Of these there are three kinds of Introducers.
For one is large, another is medium, and the third small.
They should be made of iron rods with square ends so that they go swiftly into the abscesses.
These are the forms of the Probes, that is to say, piercers; one of them is called *beris*, that is, piercer and there are three kinds of it.
Among them are large, medium and small ones.
They agree to investigate the ulcers, abscesses, fistulas, and tumors caused by what is between them among the bones.
And for others they are made round, smooth and soft like large needles with which they probe; these are of gold, or of bronze, or iron, or silver.
And the best among them are those which are made out of gold.
And there are also made of black lead, which they call "Experientia", and these are suitable for probing into the cavities of fistulas so that when reflected with its softness it reflects those same reflections gently.
And they too are again three kinds. For among them there are long ones, medium-sized ones, and short ones according to the size of the fistula's cavity and its constriction.
The shapes of probes, and they too are of many kinds.
For among them there are simple ones, that is to say those with one curve; and they too are three kinds as you see, namely large, medium and small.
And among them there are blind probes; and they too are three kinds.
And among them there are hooks with two curves and they too are three kinds; and among them there are hooks with three curves and these also are of three kinds; and all these are necessary in their proper place.
And these are the shapes of scalpels with which they cut and scrape away nodes and ulcers; and they too are three kinds since among them there is a large one, a medium-sized one, and a small one.
The ends of the instruments that are used for cutting are sharp, and their other ends are not sharp; they are designed this way so as to aid in the removal of nodules without causing damage to veins or nerves, and also so that the patient may be cured by cauterization rather than excoriation, finding some relief from the burning sensation instead of that experienced with abscesses.
They are made of brass, and resemble the rod with which alcohol is produced; at its wide end is a hidden spatulum, and it moves inside and out as you wish, as you see.
The shapes of the spatulas that are hidden between the fingers when piercing abscesses; these are not perceived by patients; and there are three types, large, medium, and small.
The shapes of the tourniquets with which blood flow is cut off; there are three types, large, medium, and small.
They should be made of brass or citron wood, round and slightly long, as you see; they must be thin.
And it is necessary that you have these two types of tourniquet with you, large and small; so that you can quickly stop the flow of blood in an emergency, or when medicine is not available.
However, do not use them to stop blood in any part of the body; only on fleshy parts like the calf and thigh, upper arms, breasts, belly, groin, or other similar areas, such as moist fleshy limbs.
And sometimes small instruments are made that resemble almonds in shape; they stop the flow of blood at a bleeding point or an incision into a vein or artery; and they are also sometimes round like this.
Chapter 47.
The treatment of male breasts that resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men become swollen during sleep completion (that is, when they begin to wet the bed), until they resemble those of women; and they remain unsightly with disfiguring swelling.
Therefore, if anyone detests this (i.e., the swollen breasts), they should cut over the nipples with a lunar incision like this one: b to g. Then scrape off all the fat. Afterwards, fill the wound with flesh-coloured medicine (i.e., ointment). Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture, and treat it until it is healed.
If the breast hangs down and softens because of its size (as happens with women), then it should be cut on both upper sides with two similar incisions shaped like a crescent moon; each containing the other at their ends, so that there is one larger line enclosing the smaller according to this diagram: b to g. Afterwards, scrape off the skin between the two lips and remove any fat; apply what we have said about sutures and healing powders until it is healed.
If you cannot complete the incision that needs to be made due to the patient's restlessness or because of a flow of blood; then it is necessary for you to fill the wound with cautery sticks dipped in corrosive ointment, and leave them there until they corrode what remains of the fat. Then treat it until healed.
On the perforation of an abscess, which happens under the armpit.
Chapter 48.
These abscesses which happen under the armpit are of the hard type of staphylomas that contain cysts, and among them are those that contain fluids.
Those that contain fluids should be cut open with a crescent-shaped knife, like this: from line b to g. When all the pus has been drained from the abscess, fill it with an old piece of cloth and let it remain until the next day.
Then remove the cloth and dress it with an appropriate ointment until it is healed.
If the abscess is of the type of nodular staphylomas, then cut it open over the top as we have said above for staphylomas generally.
If the wound extends such that it discharges pus, then apply a caustic to it as we have said above.
Chapter 49.
On cutting open an abscess that happens next to an artery or vein.
When an artery is torn and the skin above it heals over, then often an abscess forms there; similarly, a vein can also form an abscess or swelling.
The signs by which you can recognise that an abscess and swelling are next to an artery or vein are as follows: when the abscess is next to an artery it will be long, collected deep within the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers you feel a sort of hissing sound.
And what lies over the vein is rounded and apparent on the body.
And the incision over this abscess is a mistake, especially when it is under the armpits, groin, neck, and many other places on the body; and it is very dangerous. Therefore, it is advisable to avoid treating it with iron, and one should also avoid what comes out from the ends of it or from the head.
With regard to what comes out from the swelling of the artery opening, make an incision on the skin lengthwise. Then open the incision with hooks, afterwards the artery; and free it from its coverings until it is uncovered.
Then introduce a needle under it, and make it penetrate to the other side; then tie the artery with double thread in two places, as I have taught you for the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temples.
Then prick the place which is between the two ligaments with a spoon until all the blood comes out of it, and the abscess will be healed.
Then apply a treatment that generates pus until the ligaments fall off; afterwards treat it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
If the abscess is due to the incision of a vein, then you must hold with your hand as much of the abscess together with the skin as possible. Then introduce a needle under the place you held with your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be double thread in it until you extract it from the other side. Then bind it to the same abscess with a good ligament: as I told you about binding *uva*, from two parts with two ends of the thread.
However, if you are worried that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread under the abscess throughout the entire length of the needle’s insertion: push and tighten your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle until what is inside comes out: then cut off the excess skin and leave tied that which was bound to it.
Then place on it a poultice that you soaked in wine and oil. Then administer treatment which is done with compresses and ointments until it heals.
Chapter 50.
On an abscess that happens due to nerve torsion.
Just as an abscess happens to the artery and the vein, likewise an abscess happens to nerves: which occurs either through bruising or from excessive exertion and similar causes; and it mostly arises in them at the sites of attachments or at the heel, and in every place where joints move: and it is a serious abscess.
Its colour is similar to the rest of the body, and it presents with many different appearances without pain: and when it is pressed vigorously, the patient feels a faintness like swooning.
And it is not an abscess that collects deep inside the body, rather it lies under the skin and shifts to every side; it does not move forwards towards the front or backwards towards the rear.
Therefore whatever is among them that occurs at joints should not be invaded with the iron [i.e., scalpel]. Perhaps contraction may occur.
Remove the skin from it with a spatula if it is on its head or forehead. Therefore, if it is a small abscess, hold it with forceps and cut it off at the root.
And if it is large, then lift it with a hook and scrape it; then remove it and dress the wound with stitches: and take care of it until it heals.
Chapter 51.
On cutting open an abscess which occurs on the belly.
Some people often get swellings on their bellies and other parts of their bodies which are called fungi because they resemble mushrooms.
Whose root is thin and whose head is thick and the lips are turned outward. And there are some which are small, and others that become very large.
And I myself have seen a man with two such swellings on his belly which were more like mushrooms than anything else: there was no difference between them, both being white and having small roots, and their lips turned outward and they broke open and moisture kept running from them.
So I cut them open and found one weighing about 10 ounces and the other 6. And in operating on them you must consider then whether the patient is moist-humoured, and the colour of the swelling white, and its moisture thin and slender: then cut it open with a broad spatula and have your cauteries ready over a fire.
For often a great deal of blood is expelled during its dissection: therefore act quickly if the blood beats you to it, then cauterize it.
If you see the patient afraid and timid about being cut with a knife, then take a decently made lead wire, and tighten it with an atheroma in the way we have described, leaving it there for two days.
Then add to the lead constriction... So you must not stop tightening the lead until it presses into the testicle so that it falls off by itself without any effort.
However, if the testicle is of a large root, then its dissection must be done with some fear and caution; especially those found in the belly.
Yet it is possible to cut half of it out. Then cauterize it so that it does not grow back.
Then look after the wound until it is healed.
And beware of cutting into a testicle that is dark in colour, with little sensation and an ugly appearance; for it is indeed a cancerous abscess; and I shall be back to speak more on Cancer.
Chapter LII.
§ On the treatment of the navel hernia.
A navel hernia arises from many causes. Or from a tear of the sac above the belly; through which escapes either omentum or intestine, depending on what happens with other ruptures. The blood that flows from the vein or artery, according to what has been stated above. Or from an obstruction caused by windiness within it.
But if it is due to a crack in the bone and an exit for pus: then its colour resembles that of the body's complexion; and it is soft, without pain; and a different site appears. And if it is due to the exit of the intestine, then its site differs even more from what we have described. When you therefore press it with your fingers, it becomes hidden; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it increases greatly upon entering the bath and during heavy labour.
But if it is due to moisture, it is soft; then when you press it with your hand, it does not become hidden nor constricted, but it increases.
If however it is due to blood, then with these signs an abscess appears towards a blackish shade of the skin's complexion.
But if it is due to flesh born within the body, then it is an abscess that is serious, thick, hard and confined according to one quantity.
If however it is due to windiness, then its touch is gentle.
And the operation regarding it is as you should then consider that if the prominence of the navel is due to arterial or venous blood, or windiness; then you ought to be prohibited from its cure. For in it is fear and dread, as I have taught you in the chapter where I said abscesses due to arteries and veins.
If the bulge around the navel is due to the intestine or the womb, you should instruct the patient to hold their breath and stand while stretching out their body. Then mark with an encaustic around the entire navel. After that, instruct him to lie on his back between your hands. Then encircle with a broad spatula around the navel over the area you marked with an encaustic. After that, extend the middle of the abscess upwards with a large hook. Then bind him tightly around the circumference with a strong thread or with a cord of firm silk binding: and let the knot of the binding be on the right side.
Then open up the middle of the extended abscess above the binding. If you find it already caught by the binding, then extract with the right hand and insert your index finger into it, pushing the intestine towards the interior of the belly: and if you find the womb, take hold of it with the hook and cut off its superfluous parts.
But if an artery or vein opposes you, then encircle it firmly. Then return to your operation. Take two needles and insert into them two strong threads: then introduce the two needles you have inserted around the abscess in a cross-like manner until they penetrate it.
Then tighten the abscess in four places over the needles and if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place until the stretched flesh putrefies and drops off by itself; or cut it when it ulcerates and putrefies.
Then treat it with whatever ointment is required until it heals.
If the navel has a flesh-colored knot or an indentation filled with moisture, then one should treat it as if it were a concave abscess, as we've mentioned. And draw out the blood or moisture that you find within it.
Then treat it with whatever hardens the wound.
Chapter 53.
On treating cancer.
We have already mentioned the types of cancer and how to treat them with medicine; we've also warned against treating it with iron lest it be ulcerated; furthermore, we spoke about cancers formed in the womb and precautions regarding their treatment.
The ancients have said that sometimes cancer is located where it can be entirely removed; for example, cancers of the breast or thigh and those in similar external locations can all be wholly extracted, especially when they are small at the outset.
But once it has spread and grown large, one must not approach it.
For I could not heal anyone of it, nor have I seen any before me who had succeeded in curing it.
And the treatment for it when located as we've described is to precede and cleanse the sick person of black bile several times. Then you must bleed them if they present with an apparent fullness.
Next prepare the sick person for treatment by placing them within it.
Then throw into it hooks suitable for this cancer. After that, hollow out the entire ulcer from every side with a probe, thoroughly examining it until nothing remains of its roots: and let the blood run freely without cutting away the ulcer quickly; instead, squeeze out the places [of infection], and wipe off all thick blood with your hand or whatever is possible there among the instruments.
However, if a flow of thick blood from an arterial or venous incision opposes you, then cauterize the vein until it cuts off the blood supply. Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
On the Treatment of Dropsy.
Chapter 54. We have already discussed the different types of dropsy and how water accumulates; we also gave the signs for each type as well as its treatment with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron, it is only the type of ascites; and it cannot be treated with iron for tympanitis or hyposarcia because these are deadly.
When you have already treated this type of dropsy called tympanitis with medicines but it did not yield any results, then consider the following... For if the patient has become weak or is suffering from another illness in addition to dropsy, such as a cough or stomach pain and similar ailments, then beware of treating them with iron since it could be dangerous.
If however you see that the patient has an abundance of strength and is not suffering from any other illness except dropsy, and they are neither a child nor an elderly person, then this is how to proceed: lift the patient up between your hands while a helper pushes on their stomach with their hand forcing water downward with comb-like motions.
Take a sharp awl, shaped like this. Let there be a straight line divided into two parts with the longer one ending in an acute angle just like your awl. However, let there be some degree of shortening so that it does not pierce too far when you are operating and damage the intestines.
Consider this. For if the swelling of dropsy originates from the intestines then you must extend your incision three fingers' breadth downward from the navel, straight down to the pubic bone.
But if the dropsy is due to illness of the liver, then let your incision be on the left side three fingers' breadth from the navel.
And if the dropsy is due to illness of the spleen then let your incision be on the right side; and it should not be made on whichever side he wishes to lie either so that the superfluities do not flow there, weakening him further.
Then pierce through the entire skin with your instrument. Then introduce your hand into the incision and lift it with your awl between the skin and flesh as if you are scraping it; let that which is scraped off be of a quantity equal to a fingernail or thereabouts.
Then pierce in this way until the awl reaches an empty space; here is where water will be found. Then withdraw the awl and introduce this instrument into the hole: And it is like a tube; it is made of silver or copper or bronze, flexible and clean: at its lower part there is a small hole and along its sides are three holes, two on one side and one on the other: and its end is cut with a slit like that of a reed, as you see here: at its upper end there is an encircling ring.
For when the instrument reaches water, it immediately sinks onto the instrument; therefore, remove it from the water within an hour's time.
If you remove it too soon, within an hour the patient might die from a loss of vital spirits or they may suffer a swoon that is near to death.
But remove it according to its strength, and according to what the patient's condition indicates to you regarding the strength of their pulse and their well-being.
Then withdraw the instrument, and keep the water.
This is because it adheres immediately to the skin that retains the hole that is above the navel; of which I told you how to pierce it in that manner.
On the next day, reinsert the instrument if you see that the patient can tolerate it; then remove a small amount from the water as well.
Do this for several days without fear, taking care to avoid error, until there remains only a little of the water.
But if you fear for the patient and let out a large amount of water, then treat it with burial in hot sand, and profuse sweating in the bath and the sun, and watch for thirst; and also treat it with drying medicines until it is healed: and cauterize over the stomach, flanks, and spleen after the extraction of water, as I described earlier.
On treating children who are born with their urine passageways unpierced.
Chapter 55.
When any child emerges from its mother's womb with the head of its penis unpierced, or with a narrow hole, or in an incorrect location.
We must therefore provide immediate assistance with its perforation as it occurs using a very thin spatula, in this manner.
Then place a thin lead peg into the hole and tie it there; and keep it in place for three or four days.
When he therefore wishes to pass urine, it should be removed, and he may urinate. Then return him there.
And if you do not place the lead there, it is possible that the urine which passes over the wound will not allow it to close up.
However, the man whose wound is narrow should be treated with lead as we have said, for many days until it widens.
Those in whom the wound is not in its proper place will be injured. This is because in those who are born with such a wound it is located at the end of the penis; hence they cannot pass urine until they lift the penis upwards with their hand. Nor is it conducive to generation for him because the semen cannot reach the uterus directly; and this is a very foul sickness.
And the manner of its operation is that the patient must lie face down on his back. Then take hold of the end of his penis with your left hand and stretch it forcibly; thereafter cut off the head of its glans from the place where it joins onto the shaft, using a knife or an extremely sharp spatula: the incision should be like that made in a reed, or as if you were cutting roots, so that its middle becomes like the glans: and ensure that the wound falls exactly in the middle as required.
And take care during your operation to avoid blood flow. For it often happens thus. Therefore oppose him with that which cuts off the blood supply, and tend to the wound until it is healed.
On the various occurrences of inflammation and putrefaction, blackening, and sticking together which happen to the prepuce and glans penis.
Chapter 56.
These inflammations often occur on the penis and there is an ugly fleshy swelling.
And one of these is malignant while another is not malignant.
Therefore the non-malignant one should be treated by suspending it with a fine hook and cutting it off until you have cleaned it all. Then place over it lint soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then take care of the whole thing afterwards with dyaphinicon ointment until it is healed.
If the inflammation is malignant and of an ugly colour then it should be treated with a cautery after its incision and scraping.
However if the inflammation is on the foreskin of an uncircumcised person and part of it lies within the foreskin while another outside then it should be treated by removing first that which is inside until it becomes firm, then treat externally; because when you treat both at once the foreskin won't heal without a perforation.
And these occurrences of blackening and putrefaction also happen to the testicles. You should therefore hollow out all that is already blackened and what wants to be corrupted, or has already been corrupted.
Then sprinkle over it after that with honey what has already been crushed with flour of pomegranate rinds and powdered herbs.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
However if there should be bleeding, then administer the lunar caustic with a cautery, in this form.
The caustic is indeed beneficial for two conditions at once, namely bleeding and wounds when corrupted.
But if the head of the rod corrodes and is destroyed entirely within an hour: then you must insert into the penis a leaden tube so that the patient urinates over it.
In cases of the foreskin sticking to the head of the rod; and this sticking does not happen except in someone whose foreskin is healthy, and has not been cut over it: and sometimes sticking happens because of injury or swelling: you must therefore scrape it with a blunt spatulum until the bond dissolves, and the head of the rod becomes free from all sides.
If it is difficult to distinguish them precisely, you must therefore scrape something from the head of the rod and from the foreskin. This is because when the foreskin is thin it might be pierced quickly on account of its thinness.
Then separate between the foreskin and head of the rod with a fine linen cloth, which has already been moistened in cold water, so that they do not stick together again.
The wound should then be treated with astringent wine until it heals.
Chapter 57.
Regarding the cleansing of infants and the treatment for mistakes occurring during this process...
Circumcision is not just a solution to restoring continuity like other wounds; however, as it is our doing and at our will – especially for its use being proper in boys – we must mark this operation with an improvement and provide a smoother path leading to their health.
I say then that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books since they do not use it in their laws and it is only what we have acquired through experience.
That is because I have found that most craftsmen and phlebotomists perform cleansing by cutting with a razor and scissors; they use cauterization and ligatures with thread, and incisions with a nail.
Indeed I have tested all these methods and found no better way of cleansing than with scissors and a thread-ligature.
For when one uses the razor for cleansing, skin often escapes (and is missed). Because the foreskin's skin has two layers; therefore perhaps the upper layer is incised and the lower one torn - forcing another incision and causing pain. And with the cleansing done by cauterization - that is, with a moxa cigar which cobblers use – the genital member's tip incision is not sealed since it may enter its hole.
But with the cleansing by nail, perhaps the iron pierces (the member) and ruins your operation; boys' skin is short by nature and they are often born in such a way that they do not need this cleansing – I have seen it myself.
The method of wound excision using scissors and ligating with thread has proved a good technique for me: because the scissors are proportionate to the size of the incision; therefore, that which is sharp above is like that which is sharp below. When you thus hold your hand, the two sharp edges are brought together and make an incision with one motion in a single movement of time; and it makes a compression with a similar shape containing the skin of the rod from every side without any slip or mistake at all.
The way of performing this operation is to make the patient believe, especially if it is a child and one who can understand somewhat, that you are not doing anything but tying a thread around their rod; and then let them go until another day.
Then make him enjoy himself and be happy in every way you can for that day from the fact that his understanding has received this knowledge. Then make him stand between your hands which are positioned to support his weight and not have him seated; and hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot so that the child does not see them at all nor any of the instruments.
Then insert your hand onto his rod and blow into the skin; and pull it upwards until the head of the rod emerges. Then clean it from what dirt has gathered on it.
Then ligate the marked place with doubled thread. After that, tie slightly lower down another similar ligation below it. Then hold with your index finger and thumb the lower ligation place with a good grasp; and make an incision between the two ligations. Then lift the skin upwards quickly and extract the head of the ligament. Then let the blood run gradually (for it is better) and place powder on top; then put a cloth over the powder which has been spread with the yolk of an egg boiled in rose water, beaten together with fresh good rose oil; and leave him on this until another day; and treat it with the rest of the cure until it heals.
This is the shape of the scissors for cleansing.
There should be two sharp ends and they must converge; the nail of which shouldn't protrude from them; and let both edges have a length equal to that of their handles.
But an error occurring during cleansing is when the entire inner skin or part of it escapes at the incision site.
Therefore it is necessary that you stretch it with your finger for some time before the incision and cut it evenly.
If you cannot hold it with your finger then extract it with a hook and cut it.
If however three days have passed and what is under the swollen ligament remains as an abscess then leave it until the abscess becomes warm and scrape it with a lancet, and cut it according to your preparation; and beware of the head of the ligament. For it won't harm him.
Treat it with what heals wounds from the powders we described in the treatise on powders; and if it is cut from skin above a measure and protrudes upwards then that won't harm him either.
Take care of it with what we have said, until he is healed.
Chapter LVIII.
On the extraction of retained urine from the bladder.
Retention of urine occurs when the bladder becomes obstructed by a stone, or from congealed blood, pus, flesh, and other such things that we have mentioned among ways to treat these conditions as outlined above.
And if the urine is not resolved and you see that his retention is due to a stone which already exists in the neck of the bladder, then it must be that the patient should be placed bent over his knees. Then his feet should be placed upon his back and the patient must extend his belly from the upper part of his spine, and he should draw in his breath as much as possible. Thus he is forced with the neck of his bladder towards an expulsive action for the stone, which will then result in a discharge of urine.
But if this does not resolve as we have said and the matter is severe for the patient then you must proceed to its extraction with an instrument that is called a syringe, which should look like this: It should be made of silver, and fine, soft, concave like the quill of a bird in the fineness of a pencil lead, long as the length of one palm; it should have a small nozzle at its tip.
The manner of urine extraction with it is as follows: Take a doubled thread and tie some wool or cotton to its end, but securely; and insert the end of the thread into the lower part of the syringe, and cut off any excess from the wool with scissors, then push the end through the nozzle like a ramrod.
Then lubricate the syringe with oil, or butter, and an egg white; and make the patient sit on a seat, and insert the catheter, and moisten his penis with oils, olive oil, and warm water.
Then insert the syringe into the penis with ease until it reaches the root of the penis.
Then double it above on the upper side from the navel.
Then push the syringe inside until it reaches the bladder, and the patient feels that he has now reached an empty space; and this does not happen according to this order unless because of the meatus, where it goes in, is twisted.
Then pull out the thread with wool gradually. The catheter will follow the wool.
Then pull it out and make the urine flow through its path; and repeat with the syringe, and do not stop doing this until the bladder is evacuated and the patient finds relief.

Chapter 60.
On extraction of the stone.
I have already spoken about the types of stones and their treatment with medicine, and I described the differences between a kidney stone and bladder stone, along with its signs; I mentioned a stone which requires manual intervention or surgical incision; I also spoke about a true bladder stone, and one fixed to a stick. I am now explaining this with clear exposition and brevity.
I say therefore that a true bladder stone most commonly occurs in children.
And from its signs are that the urine comes out of the bladder like water in its thinness; and there appears in it white grit, and the sick person rubs his penis and labours with it, and hangs down frequently. Then he stands up, and the anus comes out with it in many of them.
And healing is easy for children until they reach the age of fourteen. And it is difficult in old people. However, for young people it is moderate; that is, for him who lies between the two extremes; for the one whose stone is larger, healing is easier; but if small, his cure is the opposite.
When we reach the point of treatment, it is necessary first to cleanse the patient with an enema that extracts all feces from their intestines; for this can sometimes hinder the discovery of the stone during examination.
Then the patient should be taken with their feet first and shaken and moved towards the lower parts of their body so that the stone descends into the bottom of the bladder or leaps from its high position several times.
Then have him sit between your hands while prepared, and his hands should be under his hips so that the bladder is completely sloped towards the lower parts.
Then examine him, and touch him externally.
If you therefore feel the stone in its place, then hurry immediately with an incision upon it.
But if it does not fall under your touch at all, you must clean your left-hand index finger with oil for a child or the middle finger of an adult and insert it into their anus to search for the stone until it lies beneath your finger; then turn it gradually towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger and force it to the exterior opposite the location where you wish to make the incision; instruct one assistant to press on the bladder with their hand while another stretches the scrotum with his right hand upwards, and the other removes the skin beneath the scrotum away from the place of the section.
Then insert the scalpel, which has this shape: [a picture of an ancient surgical instrument]. Find the place between anus and testicles but not in the middle on the left side of the buttocks: make the incision above the stone itself with your finger inside the anus so that the cut is made transversely to be wider externally than internally according to the size of the possibility for the stone to exit from it, no greater.
Perhaps the finger compresses a stone that is near the incision and it exits without difficulty.
And you should know that since the stone has angles and edges, its exit is difficult because of them.
And if it is smooth, like a gland, and rounded, its exit will be easy.
Therefore, to those with angles and edges, add it to the wound.
If it does not come out in that way, then you should use a hook on it: or compress above it with an off-center lever, the end of which is like a crescent moon, to press on the stone and not emerge from it; or insert below it a slender instrument with a curved end.
But if you cannot above it, then enlarge the hole somewhat. If something resists you with blood, cut away that part with scissors.
But if there is more than one stone, then push the larger towards the bladder's mouth first. Then make an incision above it. Next, push the smaller one after that; and do likewise if there are more than two stones.
If it is very large, however, you must not cut above it with a wide incision. For it happens to the patient that either he dies or has frequent dribbling of urine: on account of this, since the wound does not heal completely.
But administer his evacuation until it comes out; or insert hooks into its fracture with scissors until you extract it piecemeal.
Once you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with incense, aloe, and dragon's blood; compress it, and place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: to settle the hot abscess.
Then turn the patient onto their front over their neck, and do not release the ligature until the third day. When you have released it, bathe the place with water and lots of oil.
Then treat it with duafalan ointment and basilian ointment until it is healed.
If an abscess forms in the wound with added heat and corrosion, or similar; like if blood freezes in the bladder and prevents urine from passing: it is known by the passage of blood with urine. Insert your finger into the wound and draw out that blood. For if it remains, it will provoke the corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt; apply every kind of suitable treatment to it until it is healed.
During the entire course of treatment, it is necessary that you bind together both thighs and draw them close, because of the medications being placed on the site.
But if it is a small stone and lodged at the opening of the penis, and stuck within so as to prevent urine from passing; then treat it with what I described before you resort to cutting. For many times one may be spared the knife through this treatment, for I have already proven it.
And it is necessary to take a perforator made from steel iron, its shape being as follows: triangular tip, sharp point, fixed in wood.
Then take a string, and tie it with that to the stick under the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the piercing iron into the stick with ease until you reach the stone itself; and turn the piercer by hand on that same stone little by little: and try its puncture until it emerges from the other side.
For urine is immediately released.
Then press your hand on what remains of the stone from the outer side of the stick. For it will break, and exit with urine, and the patient will be healed.
But if this treatment is not prepared for you due to something that prevents it; then tie the string under the stone and another string over the stone. Then cut above the stone on that same stick between the two bindings. Then extract it. Afterwards untie the binding and clean frozen blood that forms on the wound.
And it is not necessary to bind the string under the stone unless you wish to prevent its return into the bladder: and another binding above [the stone] unless when you untie the string after exiting the stone, skin returns to its place and covers the wound: and for that reason it is necessary for you when you tie the upper string to raise the skin upwards so that it returns at your completion of the procedure and cover the wound as we have said.
Chapter 61.
The removal of stones from women's bodies.
Stones rarely form in women.
However, if one of them has stones, then the cure is difficult and is prevented because of many modes.
One of these is that perhaps the woman is a virgin.
And the second is that you do not find a woman who reveals herself to a doctor when she is chaste or among those with husbands.
The third is that you do not find a woman who knows this art well, especially surgery with the hands.
And the fourth is remoteness from the stone's place; hence it requires deep incision, which involves fear.
If necessity drives you to it, then you should take a female doctor who knows well; and she is rarely found.
If you lack that, then seek a chaste and subtle doctor; and present to him an obstetric woman well taught on women's matters or one who knows part of this art: so make her present, and chiefly instruct her to do everything which you order concerning investigation above the stone.
First is to look if the woman is a virgin; then you should put your finger into her anus and seek for the stone.
If you find it, hold it under your finger; then instruct the obstetric woman to put her finger into the patient's vulva and look for the stone after placing her left hand on the hip and squeezing it well.
If you find a stone, then you should move it gradually from the urethral opening towards the lower parts with all your strength until you reach the hip joint with it.
Next, cut over it at the midpoint of the groin near the hip joint on whichever side it is prepared for him and feel the stone on that side; and his finger should not be removed from the stone, grasping under it; and let the incision be small at first. Next, insert your finger into that incision.
Next, insert your finger into that incision. If you feel the stone, then make the incision larger according to how much the stone protrudes from it.
And be aware that there are many types of stones. Of these, some are small, some large; some smooth, others rough; some long, and others round with branches.
Know then its type so that it may be shown to you by this over what you want.
If blood overpowers you, then add some powdered frankincense to the place and hold it there for an hour until the bleeding stops.
Then return to your operation until the stone comes out; and do as I said when extracting stones from men's bodies, and prepare those instruments with you to assist in your operation.
If a flow of blood overpowers you and you know that it is coming from the artery which has been cut; then place some powder on the spot and bind it with suitable compresses: do not change it: leave the stone alone and don't extract it. Perhaps the sick woman will die. Then treat the wounds.
When therefore the sharpness of the blood is abated after some days, and the place becomes corrupt, then return to your operation until the stone comes out.
Chapter 62.
On cutting open a watery hernia.
A watery ram is not anything other than a collection of moisture in the spermatic cord, which lies beneath the skin containing the egg; and it is called a spermatic cord.
And sometimes it is in its own sac which nature has prepared for it on the side of the scrotum where the egg lies; and this is another skin of the testicle between the spermatic cord that we spoke about, and this happens only rarely.
And indeed the generation of these rams is from a weakness that befalls the testicles; therefore this material is poured out upon them. And sometimes it happens after a blow on the testicles.
And this moisture is of many colours. Or else its colour is greenish-blue, or it is red like blood, or swarthy, black, or white and watery, and this last is the most common.
And the signs by which it may be known where the collection of water lies, are these. For if it is in the spermatic cord that we spoke of, then there will be a round abscess like an egg for length; and the testicle does not appear because moisture surrounds it on every side.
If there is moisture in the cloth itself, then the abscess is round with an ear-like part, and due to this, it is considered another type of egg.
And if moisture is between the skin of the testes and the white scrotum, then it falls under a different category.
When you wish to understand the colour of moisture, then test the colour of the moist abscess with a square-shaped probe which precedes its shape. According to that which emerges on the probe, judge about what is inside it.
When we come to treatment with the iron, you should then instruct the patient that they must be bled if possible and see their body as being full.
Then the patient should lie on their back over something slightly elevated and place many cloths beneath them.
Then sit on the patient's left side and instruct your assistant to sit at their right who will stretch out their penis towards one of two sides of the scrotal skin and towards the navel area.
Then take a very wide spatula and cut the scrotal skin from its middle lengthwise towards the comb; let this incision be made according to an even-distanced line that divides the scrotal skin until you reach as far as the white scrotum: then scrape it and observe it or watch out for this, so that you can cut it away: and make your scraping from the part which is suspended with the egg more; and carefully probe using this scraping according to what is possible for you.
Then pierce the water-filled scrotum with an ample hole and extract all of its water.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and stretch the scrotum upwards, and do not touch the skin covering the testes: and cut the scrotum however it is possible for you to cut it, either as a whole or in pieces, especially its thin side. But if you do not carefully examine it, there will be no safety in its incision, that water returns.
If however the egg moves outwards from its skin during your operation: then when you have finished the scrotum incision, bring it back. Then join the two lips of the testes' skin with a suture.
Then care for it like the other wounds, until it is healed.
If you find the egg corrupted by other blackness, then you must tie its vessels, which are its suspension, fearing a flow of blood.
Then cut off the testes with its suspension, and extract the egg: afterwards care for it with what we have said.
If however water has gathered in the two parts, then know that there are two branches: therefore cut off another part equally as you did with the other one.
And if it falls to you that there is one operation, then do this: afterwards wipe the blood away. And introduce into the incisions wool soaked in oil, or in rose-coloured oil: and place outside the wool that you have steeped in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testes, and navels of the abdomen: and place doubled cloths on top, which are compresses: and tie them from above with a bandage having six folds like this.
Two cloths should be taken and soaked, then filled with red lead powder according to this likeness: the ends which are pressed together will have a piece of cloth or wet wool; they will be bound with bandages on the arms from shoulder to armpit: one end is placed over the anus up to the back, and the second covers the side up to the breast area: two ends cover the thighs below, and the other two ends cover the groins. The whole binding should then be done tightly around the arms in the shoulder girdle region.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with the cautery instead of cutting it with an iron tool; take a thin cauterizing knife and cut the skin of the testicles with it, and heat it as we described earlier until some white substance containing water appears; then take the cautery in this shape.
The letter resembles Greek characters.
Then make a hole in the white substance with it (the cautery), which is hot until all moisture comes out.
Then stretch the white substance with hooks, scrape it with a thin hot cautery, and cut into it as much as you can until you have removed all of it.
And be careful not to touch the egg with fire (the cautery).
Also remember that this operation is safer, less likely to cause blood loss, and better than the one involving cutting (i.e., incision).
If a hot swelling or bleeding or some other complication occurs during the operation (i.e., incision) or cauterization, then it should be treated with appropriate therapy as mentioned above.
If however the patient is fearful and does not expect to survive this operation because of its horror: then perform the perforation as I have described it: and so it shall be.
When you have confirmed for yourself by what we said about the meanings that the branch is watery: then you must make the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then perforate the abscess on its lower part with a spatulate object that is wide: and make the perforation according to the length of the body, leaving an ample opening until all water runs out.
Then place on the spot a poultice, binding it in place, and let it remain until all residual water has run out: then treat the wound until it is healed.
If however there is any hindrance to the egress of water at the time you perforate it: and if this is only because a white membrane causes obstruction at the mouth of the wound, preventing the exit of water: then you must introduce into the wound either a feather or that instrument by means of which hydropic fluid is extracted, its tip having been first sharpened: or add this to your procedure upon the wound.
And you should be aware that water returns and collects after six months or thereabouts. When therefore it has collected, then repeat the operation in exactly the same way, and the patient will pass his days.

On the treatment of fleshy branches.

And sometimes it happens from the blockage of arteries, and their inflammation; as has already been recalled earlier: or from the inflammation of veins.

That which happens from the inflammation of veins does not discharge anything upon being compressed with the fingers.

Chapter 64.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids which is done with leeches.
A grape-like abscess wrapped up with bandages, resembling a swelling of the testicles; it becomes difficult for the patient in terms of movement and exercise, as well as walking.

Chapter 65.
On the cure for branches of the intestine.
The cause of these branches is from an incision that occurs over the navel on the abdomen in the groin area because of a rupture of the belly.
Why do the intestines herniate from that rupture down to one of the testes? And this rupture is either because of a hernia or its extension.
These two conditions occur due to many causes: either from blows, or jumping, or shouting, or lifting heavy objects, and similar things.
And the sign is, when it is due to excessive straining. It is a condition that happens gradually over a long period of time, and it does not happen suddenly; and it is an equal abscess pointing inwards (towards the intestines): because straining compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it is when it is due to a tearing of straining, because it causes an intense pain at first suddenly; and it is an outward-pointing abscess, appearing close to the skin: and this happens due to the exit of the intestine, and its course towards the exterior of the strain.
And sometimes it exits with the intestine as well: and this is called an intestinal rupture or a hernia; and sometimes it occurs with wind (gas).
And sometimes faeces runs into the intestine and is retained there; and it is harmful to the sick person: because it causes severe pain, especially when pressure is applied.
And there is fear (of harming the patient) when treating these types of ailments with iron instruments; therefore, one must take precautions to avoid such accidents.
And the method of operating is to instruct the patient to push the intestine back into their abdominal cavity with their own hand if they are able and willing to comply. Then have them lie back on their neck between your hands: and lift up their legs.
Then pull the skin that follows onto its upper part, and make an incision along the entire length of the scrotum's skin. Then attach hooks to the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them; and let the incision be made as large as possible so that an egg can pass through it.
Next, scrape the scrotal sac and what is under the skin of the testicles until the white albuginea is revealed, which should be hard on all sides; then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, between the white albuginea, which is under the skin of the penis, and the secondary albuginea, and separate the adhesions that are at the back of the penis.
Then move the right hand towards the inner skin of the testicles; and with this action stretch the white albuginea upwards with your left hand; raise the egg with the scrotal sac towards the side of the incision; instruct the assistant to stretch the egg upwards; and you loosen the adhesion that is behind, once stretching is complete.
And feel with your fingers that there isn't anything inside the intestine wrapped around the hard white albuginea. If however you find anything there; then push it towards the lower belly.
Then take a needle which has a thick thread attached to it, made of ten threads of silk; and insert the same into the last scrotal sac, that is, the tunica under the skin of the testicles which follows the incision; then cut off the ends of the protruding thread until four threads remain. Then arrange some of these over one another in the form of a cross; and tie them with that scrotal sac we spoke about, which is under the skin of the testicles along a tough ligament until no vessel, which nourishes it, can send anything to it: so that there is no risk of a hot abscess. And place another ligature outside the first one, about two fingers away from it. And after these two ligatures, leave out a piece of the sifach, which is under the scrotal skin, about the size of a finger; and cut away all that remains around [the testicles], and remove one [testicle].
Then cut in the lower part of the scrotal skin where blood and pus are flowing, as we explained earlier.
Then apply wool soaked in oil to the wound; and apply the ligature we described. And sometimes the white part of the scrotum that we mentioned is cauterized after its incision to prevent bleeding. For this happens often. And leave the ligature in place until it falls off on its own.
But if its falling off is delayed, then it should be bathed with warm water; and place on top of it what we described earlier until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the remaining treatment for wounds until it heals.

¶ Of hydrocele (watery tumor).

Chapter 67.
On ruptures that occur in the groins.
When a rupture occurs in the groins as we said and there is an obvious lump that does not descend into the scrotum or if it has descended then this is slight but returns at all times: when it becomes prolonged over time with the patient, a fissure opens up in sifach (groin) so that the intestines fall down into sifach.
And this happens because of the extension of sifach which is in the groins as we said. That is to say that because the tissue of sifach has become stretched it becomes soft so that the spot hardens (and develops a lump) and protrudes.
And its treatment is with cauterization as was mentioned in the previous chapter.
And it is sometimes treated with the use of an iron tool in this way: have the patient lie on their back and place your hands either side of them. Then cut the ruptures spot which has hardened and is protruding in one slice according to a width of three fingers.
