For a hernia is contracted, not an added protuberance of rupture.
And the cautery is better in this place, for it is closer to recovery.
Chapter 68.
On softening of the scrotum of the testicles.
The scrotal skin of some men is softened many times over due to which the flesh between them also softens and its appearance is marred.
Therefore it is necessary for him who is seeking its treatment to make the sick person lie down on his back and then cut away all the skin that has softened above the normal skin.
Then join the two lips with a suture; and if you wish, sew first the superfluity of the softened scrotal skin with three or four sutures and secure them.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat with wound treatment the remainder of its wounds until it heals and the threads fall out.
Chapter 69.
On emasculation.
Know that emasculation is prohibited by our law; and for this reason I could have omitted to mention it in my book; and its mention here is not necessary save on account of two points.
One of these is that it should be within the doctor's knowledge when he is asked about it; and that he may know how to treat him for whom it has occurred.
Another way is that we often require the castration of certain animals for our livestock, such as rams, goats, and cats, and similar among animals.
I say, therefore, that castration is done in two ways. Or by abrasion. Or by incision and cutting.
In that which is done by abrasion, the way of operating is this: we make the animal sit in warm water until its testicles soften, relax, and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer felt.
In castration by incision and cutting, you must hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then bind the suspensory ligament and cut above each testicle with one stroke until two testicles come forth; then incise these after you have scraped both and do not let anything of the spermatic fascia remain except the thin part that is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that done by abrasion because abrasion may leave some of the testicles, so the animal desires intercourse.
Then care for the wound until it heals.
Chapter 70.
On the treatment of hermaphrodites in two ways.
A hermaphrodite is from men in two ways. One of them is that it appears in the space that follows, or on the skin of the testicles, what is between the two testicles, shaped like a woman's vulva, with hair; and sometimes urine runs from what is on the skin of the testicles.
Among women there is one type and it has a woman's vulva above the comb just like small male testicles completely protruding to the outside; of which there is one like a man's penis and two like two testicles.
And the cure for all three types, two from men and one from women, is because you must cut away the flesh additions until their imprint disappears. Then cure them with treatment for other wounds.
On the second type of men from which urine comes out, that is on the skin of the testicles, there is no operation nor any cure for it at all.
Section 71.
On the excision of warts and protruding flesh on women's vulvae.
A wart may be added to a natural thing until it is disfigured and its appearance becomes ugly; and sometimes it grows on some women so much that it expands like a man's and reaches the point of intercourse.
You should therefore hold the superfluity of the wart with your hand or a hook and cut it; do not hesitate to cut deeply into its root so that there is no bleeding.
Then treat the treatment itself for wounds until it is healed.
Meat however arising is meat which is born in the opening of the womb until it fills itself, and perhaps comes out to the exterior like a tail; and because of this some ancients name the disease tailed. It is necessary therefore that we cut it like we cut warts, and treat it until it is healed.
On the treatment of alratitis which is an unpenetrated wound of a woman.
Chapter 72.
Alratitis is that there should be an unpenetrated wound of a woman or it should be a small hole. And it is either by nature or by chance/accident.
That which is by chance/accident happens due to an illness that has preceded it; and it itself occurs either from the meat born added or extremely fine or thick; and it occurs either in the depths of the womb or on its sides or at the top or bottom of it, and prevents coitus and impregnation and birth, and perhaps menstruation.
And its diagnosis is made by the gaze of an obstetrician if the obstruction is apparent nearby.
But if it is not apparent then seek it with your fingers or with a rod.
But if the obstruction is by extremely fine meat, and it is near the two lips then hasten and tear it apart.
And it must be that you place on the two lips some poultices.
Then place your two hands' index fingers together, and let the woman lie on her back with her legs spread. Then stretch the two lips with strength until it is torn open slightly, and uncover the incision.
Then take some wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the wound; have the woman sit every day so that the wound does not close up with scar tissue.
If the wound is thick and dense, you must cut it with a scalpel that is six inches wide, similar to a myrtle leaf.
If the incision is formed from flesh, then lift it up with hooks and cut it; and have you on hand some medicines to stem blood flow without clotting, such as acacia gum, dragon's blood, and oil mixed with egg white.
Then insert a lead pipe of appropriate width so that the wound does not close quickly; keep it in for several days; and then administer linen thread made from dry flax, after which cure the rest with other medicines until it is healed.
And when other fleshly growths occur in the womb, they should be cut out in this way: while there is no cancerous abscess, for an iron implement must not come into contact with a cancerous sore.
Chapter 73.
For the cure of hemorrhoids, and red pimples on female genitalia.
Hemorrhoids are indeed the opening of veins, from which a great deal of blood flows out steadily.
When hemorrhoids are inflamed, they become purple; and I have already mentioned their types in the division of species, and their signs: and here I will say which of them can be cured, and which cannot.
I therefore say that hemorrhoids which are located at the opening of the uterus, and are not apparent to the senses: then they cannot be cured with iron.
Those hemorrhoids which are located at the opening of the uterus and are apparent to the senses, are those that can be cured.
Therefore, it is necessary for you to introduce the woman into a cold house. Then stretch out the purple areas with forceps or with a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then sprinkle over them during the flow of blood a powder made from the excised hemorrhoids without any modification: such as acacia gum, dragon's blood, frankincense, and similar substances.
Then press her feet against the wall for one hour. After that, place her in cold water.
If the flow of blood persists: then have her sit in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate rinds, galls, and similar substances.
Then take barley flour, mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster from it for her back.
If the blood stops with this treatment, it is good; and if not, apply cupping glasses to her back and breasts without scarring.
Then take wool and soak it in the juice of tarathon, or the juice of sea-thorny head, or the juice of alant branches (that is, elecampane), with Pontic wine, and apply it as a poultice.
When the abscess subsides, then treat the place with ointments until it is healed.
The albarth, however, resembles the redhead of another bird, and it is of rough appearance.
Therefore, it is necessary that you cut off what appears from it, as I have said elsewhere evenly; and treat it until it heals.
Chapter 74.
On the perforation of the exit wound that occurs in the womb.
There occur in the womb many types of abscesses, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is herpes, aestiomemus, and fistulae, and haemorrhoids, and ragadiae, and thethel, and hot abscess.
And I have indeed already spoken of all these ailments and their types, and their signs, and their treatments in detail; it is necessary that I speak in this treatise of the hot abscess that occurs in the womb when it is one of those abscesses that collect pus, and you want to know how its opening with an iron is done.
Therefore, it is necessary that you pay attention. Then if the pain of the abscess in its onset is sharp with its throbbing, and burning, and fever; and you see it redden; if sensation falls on it, then do not rush into perforating it; and treat it with that which promotes maturation until such time as the sharpness of the symptoms subsides.
Then you should have the woman sit on a chair that has two slippery seats, and lean back, or be supported over her back, and draw up her feet to the lower part of her belly; and let her hips be spread out, and place her arms under her knees, and bind both with a suitable bandage for it.
Then the midwife should sit on the right side and use the tool to open the maternal opening.
And when you wish to use this tool, make sure to push with the rod into the depth of the woman's womb: so that what enters from the tool does not go beyond the depth of the womb and harm the patient.
But if the tool is longer than the depth, you must place some pads on top of the covering of the womb's depth: to prevent the whole tool from entering into the depth of the womb: and it is necessary that the helper places the handle, which runs through the tool, from the upper side: and hold the tool, and make the assistant turn the handle until the depth of the womb opens.
When therefore the emerging part appears, and is touched by hand, and is soft and gentle: then it is necessary to cut it near its head with a spatula an inch wide.
When the blood is drained, it is necessary to place on the wound lint soaked in rose oil: or green oil, in which there is some astringency: and put the lint, and extract the piece from the depth of the womb.
And place outside over the womb, and over the pubic hair clean wool soaked in water in which have boiled some apples.
Then it should be treated for two days with an ointment appropriate to the situation until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and wound are washed so that they are flushed with water and honey: then with water in which is boiled lily root or long aristolochia: then return to the ointment.
If a wound is hidden internally within the uterus, then one ought to refrain from treating it with iron instruments; indeed, treat it with what we have said in the division.
Chapter 70.
Doctrines of midwifery: how live infants who emerge with a distorted shape are treated.
It is necessary for the midwife to recognise first of all what constitutes normal delivery.
Therefore, the signs of it are that when you see a woman straining at the lower parts and wishing to sniff the air, and whatever is above her feels lighter and she hastens the baby's exit: then you know that this delivery follows the natural form, and its presentation is head first and the second stage with it: either suspended or hanging by the umbilical cord.
So when you see these signs, it is necessary to compress her abdomen so that the baby may descend swiftly.
For when it has descended head first, the second stage descends with it, and is cleansed of any superfluities by a good cleansing.
Anything other than this delivery is unnatural and to be deplored.
For sometimes the fetus emerges on its feet and its body comes out before its head and its feet and hands come together or one of its feet; or it comes out with its head and hands; or it comes out breech; and perhaps it turns onto its neck, along with similar unapproved forms.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and subtle in all these matters and beware of error and deception; and I am showing every form from them, along with how each condition presents itself, what it signifies, and how one should stand over it.
When the fetus exits head-first during its natural emergence and it becomes intense for the woman and childbirth becomes difficult for her, and you see her strength already weakened; then have her sit on a chair or have other women hold her: and apply vapor of wool with fenugreek decoction, and with moist oils.
Then the midwife should insert between her fingers a small spatula and tear with it the amniotic sac, and cut it open with her nail until all its moisture is gone; then press on the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then the woman should be enema'd with fenugreek glue and sesame oil.
Then instruct her to push after the enema; then make her sneeze with ground cumin, and hold her mouth and nose shut for an hour. The fetus will exit immediately.
If two hands of the fetus exit first, you should pull them back gently and slowly. If it does not comply upon entry, then have the woman sit on a chair with her feet elevated; shake the chair vigorously on the ground and make sure she is held so that she doesn't fall during shaking.
If the hands still do not exit and the fetus dies, then cut open her belly and extract the rest of it; tie one hand with cloth so that it's stretched out and pull the fetus from its hand with equal force on both sides, for it shall come out.
The exit of an embryo head-first. When the fetus exits feet-first, you should press its feet upwards; then turn it slowly and balance it afterwards.
Then take one foot of his/her leg and gradually stretch it out.
When the pain reaches the thighs, then tell him/her to press it and make him/her sneeze with peppercorns; if God wills it, he will come out.
And if it does not come out as we described, then gradually withdraw the fetus until you place him/her in their natural position. For it will come out easily itself at that point.
But if it does not come out as we have described: then take from the mucilage of the nightegale, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum; pound all together in a mortar with a good pounding: then anoint with it a woman's woolen pad and place it on her abdomen. Then make her sit in warm water until she reaches the hypochondrium.
When you see that her lower body has been warmed up then apply a poultice of myrrh to her and place it on. When she has held the poultice for one hour then make her sit upon a seat. Then make her sneeze: press her mouth and nose; and gently press the lower part of her abdomen with compression. For at that point, the fetus will come out immediately.
Exit of the fetus onto knees and hands. When it comes out in this form then deliver by gradually inserting your hands. Then the woman will lie on her back with her feet hanging down towards her lower body and she will be lying on the bed with her hands folded. Then press gradually on his abdomen. Then extract what is beneath their feet from the mattresses until it is ready in the lower part. If the fetus does not exit, then take their feet, and move them with a violent motion. Then press gradually on what is above the hypochondrium until the fetus ascends to the upper parts. Then insert your hands and equalise the fetus gradually; and instruct the woman to push until the fetus exits.
Exit of the fetus hanging horizontally from your hands. There will be damage to their hand as you reduce it. If they cannot reduce it, then elevate the woman and make her walk.
If she cannot walk, then make her lie on a bed; and shake her legs with a violent shaking motion.
Then administer the lotion you prepared from mucilages.
If she takes up childbirth, then make her sit on a chair. Then strive to reduce the hand and equalise the fetus according to its natural shape; and make the woman sneeze, and instruct her to push until it exits.
Exit of the fetus onto its neck, with hands spread out; and its face is towards its mother's back. It is necessary for the midwife to hold the hand of the fetus; then she should turn it gradually and equalise it gently.
When it is equalized, then place upon his wound what we have described from oil and mucilages.
Then instruct him to move gradually. Once you have done that, then make her sit on a chair, and instruct him to lean towards his right side. Then tell them to sneeze after you have made him sniff some snuff.
The prepared fetus comes out on its side. When it descends to the mouth of the womb in this manner with the caul, then cut the caul with your nail until all moisture is cleared away.
If it hangs over the face of the fetus and its neck, then cut it from its navel so that it is not retained and dies.
Then push the fetus towards the inside until you place it where it ought to be; and tell the woman to press herself several times for he will indeed come out by a gentle birth.
The birth of twins or of many children. You should know that twins often come to birth; sometimes three or four at a time. However this is an extraordinary thing, and rarely happens.
Five is beyond nature and they do not live at all; though the caul may contain them even when they increase, it still separates each one by its navelstring.
And sometimes they are born like one fetus is born naturally and easily; or they come by the ignoble birth as we have described.
If some of them are difficult during childbirth and reach that which we have described as unnatural presentations, then treat them as we have narrated: let there be a skilled midwife, and she should act with skill and slowness, and manage according to every presentation with what is prepared for her leading to health.
A reminder of that which is formed in the womb from embryos, and they abort.
Sometimes one is formed in the womb, and two, and three, and four, and five, and six, and seven, and more even ten: and it has been verified by me, that a woman aborting emitted seven, and another fifteen, all fully formed.
Which was done by measurement of the glorious sage.
All this, therefore, ought to be within the knowledge of the obstetrician. Perhaps she will come across one similar to it; therefore, oppose her with that which is required.
Chapter 76.
On the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus as I have said in the section on medicinal treatment, and it does not come out, and you are compelled to use manual manipulation, then you ought to consider carefully.
If therefore the woman is of sound constitution, and she has no accident from which one fears loss of life; then the woman ought to lie on her back on a bed, with her head bent downwards, and her legs raised: and an assistant should hold her at both sides, or she may be tied to the bed so that her body is not dragged during extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the womb bone with moist oils, along with mucilage of althea, fenugreek, and flaxseed.
Then the midwife should anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages; then she should insert it into the womb easily. Then she should seek with it the place where to hook the fetus' hooks, and convenient places for that.
Therefore consider then, if the fetus is prepared to descend head first: then hook the hooks on its eyes, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or below its chin, or on its fork (clavicles), or nearby places of its ribs, and below its flanks (hypocondria).
If however the descent is feet first, then on its pubic bone (symphysis), and on its mid-ribs (costae mediae).
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand; and place its curve between the fingers of her left hand: then insert with the hand easily, and hook the fetus in one of those places we have mentioned, until she reaches an empty space.
Then hook another hook on its opposite side, or a third if needed; so that there is an equal contraction, and it does not lean to one side.
Then it should be extended with an equal extension, and not just pulled straight; rather moved to every side so that its exit is eased; as happens when a tooth is removed.
And in what is between it (the fetus) there should be softening during the extension. If a part of it is retained, then the midwife must grease certain fingers with oil and insert them from that side so as to turn with them what is being held back.
If therefore the baby's part exits, then change the hooks to another place that is higher: do this until the whole baby comes out.
If therefore one hand comes out before the other and it is not possible to pull it back because of its constriction, then a cloth must be wrapped around it. Then pull it out until it comes out completely, cut it either at the ankle joint or at the elbow; and you should do so with the other hand and with both feet.
But if the baby's head is large or it suffers a constriction upon exit, or water has gathered in its head, then one must insert between the fingers a sharp-pointed spatula and split its head with this, extracting the water; or break its head open with an instrument called *Almisdac*, whose form comes together with those of other instruments in the chapter that follows.
Similarly do so if the baby is of large-headed nature; then it is necessary to cut its neck with a scissors; or extract it with an *Almisdac*, as we have said. Then pull out the bones with forceps.
If the head exits and becomes constricted at the collarbone, then it must be split open until the moisture that is in its chest spills out.
But if it does not get constricted nor yield to being pulled out, then cut the head in pieces as much as you can.
If there is an abscess in the lower abdomen or it has become edematous then you must pierce it until moisture runs from it.
If the fetus is in a head-down position then pulling it out is easy and guiding it towards the maternal pelvis is straightforward.
If however it is trapped around the abdomen or chest then you must extract it with a cloth wrapped around your hand and tear open its abdomen or chest until their contents run out.
If the other parts are removed but the head returns then the midwife should insert their left hand. If therefore the maternal pelvis is open then you should insert your hand deeply into it and search for its head with that same hand before grasping it firmly between your fingers until it reaches the maternal pelvis; after this introduce a hook or two hooks inside of it and pull it out with these tools.
If the maternal pelvis is cramped by hot inflammation then you should not strain yourself over it but rather administer evacuation of moist and fatty matter and apply poultices as well as sitting in waters that soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side then it may be turned; after this you should administer what we have said for live fetuses.
If this is not possible then it should be cut piecemeal and extracted.
And you must ensure that none of the membranes remain inside at all.
Once you have completed your procedure then you should administer what we have said for hot inflammation of the maternal pelvis.
If there is an occurrence of blood flow, then he should descend into styptic waters, and administer the remaining cure.
And I myself have seen a woman, in whose womb a dead fetus was; then she became pregnant on top of it with another child, afterwards the other fetus also died. So it happened to him after a long time that there was an abscess in his navel, and it became swollen until it opened up: and he began to discharge pus.
I was called upon to treat him, and I administered medicine to him for a long time: and the wound did not heal.
So I placed on him some of the plasters with a strong drawing power: and a bone came out from its place. Afterwards, some days passed, and another bone came out. I was therefore amazed that there is no bone inside the belly.
So I thought that they were from the bones of the dead fetus.
I therefore examined the wound, and extracted many bones while the woman was in her improved state of health: and now she has lived this way for a long time, and discharges very little pus from that place.
And I have not asserted this matter here as anything extraordinary, unless because there is knowledge in it, and assistance for that which the skilled physician administers with his hand from the treatment.
Chapter 77.
On the forms of instruments necessary in extracting a fetus.
The form of the vertigo instrument by which the womb is opened.
Here below is the form of the forceps, by which the handles are equalled out to which there are two pivots at both ends of two pieces of wood. However, this instrument for treating vertigo ought to be more slender and delicate; and so that it can be made either of ebony or boxwood.
And the width of each piece of wood should be about two fingers; and the edge of either one is about one finger, and the length of each is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions made from the same type of wood, which are already fixed to both, the length of which is half a palm plus a little more: and the width of each is about two fingers or slightly more.
And these two additions are inserted into the vagina so that it opens with them when you rotate both vertigo instruments.
Another form similar to this one, more slender and lighter.
It should be made of ebony or boxwood, shaped like a pair of forceps. However, at its end there are two additions as you can see above; and the length of each addition is about a palm; and the width of each one is about two fingers.
When you want to open the womb with this instrument, then have the woman sit on a bed with her feet hanging down and what is between her legs exposed. Then insert these two connected additions into the womb; as you hold the lower end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument as you do with forceps, evenly to the degree that you want from the opening of the mouth of the womb, until the midwife does what she wishes.
The other form of vertigo that the ancients described is the Form of Propelling by which the fetus is propelled.
The form of forceps, by which the large head is crushed, resembles a pair of tongs with teeth at the end, as you can see.
And sometimes it becomes elongated, like the forceps in this shape, as you can see, with teeth like a saw's, by which it is cut and crushed.
The shape of the hook with one barb.
The shape of the hook with two barbs.
The shape of the spatula having two ends for cutting off the head.
The form of two spatulas, also broad.
All these instruments are put into practice; and the more their types are multiplied, and the greater number that is prepared by the craftsman, the quicker he becomes in his operation, and the higher in repute among men on account of this quantity.
Therefore do not despise any one of these instruments which is prepared for you. The need for them is indeed inexcusable.
Chapter 78.
Concerning the extraction of secundines (the afterbirth).
When the secundines are retained after childbirth, you should instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then make them sneeze with a feather: and squeeze your hand over their mouth and nostrils. If therefore it comes out through this, that is good.
If not: then take a pot and pierce a hole in its lid: put into it herbs that open the womb's mouth such as calament, rue, dill, chamomile, sea holly, woody-smelling cinnamon, and centaury: take all these herbs or some of them, and submerge them with water, then place them over fire.
Then place a reed funnel over the pot's lid hole and the other end into the womb's mouth: hold it until the steam reaches inside the womb.
Then make them sneeze as we have said. Indeed it will come out easily.
If however it stays after this treatment and does not come out: then instruct the midwife to dip her hand in oil of turpentine or marshmallow mucilage. Then she should insert it into the womb and search for the Secondary there. When she finds it, then let her grab it and draw it out gradually.
If however it is attached deep inside the womb, then she should insert her hand as we described until when she finds the Secondary and pulls it out slowly along its length so that the womb does not collapse under strong traction; rather, it must be turned with ease to the right and left sides: afterwards the amount of traction may be increased. The blade itself then obeys and is preserved by being free from adherence.
If the womb is constricted as we have already mentioned, treat it with sneezing and an ointment of herbs from a pot.
But if it does not come out with all that we have mentioned, then be careful not to tire yourself by attempting the extraction repeatedly.
What comes out of it from the womb must be tied to the woman's hip. Then give her an enema with tetrapharmacum ointment. For it will cause her to decay within days and be dissolved, and it shall come out.
But when it rots, an evil smell is raised from it to her belly and head, which harms the sick woman. Therefore suitable fumigations should be administered to that part of her body.
Some ancient practitioners have experienced the fumigation of watercress and dried figs.
Make an instrument by which to fumigate the woman suffering from Secondary Retention and menses.
Make it from a glass similar to an alcama or from zere: and place the broad end over fire: and let the fumigation be positioned over coals: hold it there until that fumigation is consumed: and repeat with another.
Chapter 79.
On the cure of anus which is not perforated.
Many children are born with their anuses not perforated, which thin cauterisation has already closed up. It is necessary, therefore, for the midwife to pierce it with her finger; and if not, she should pierce it with a sharp spatulam, being careful of the arm so that it does not touch it.
Then place over it wool soaked in wine and oil. Next treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear it will become blocked, then place in the opening a lead pipe for many days and remove it when the child is able to walk.
And blockage of the anus also occurs from the healing of wounds or abscesses. It is necessary therefore that the healing be cut open, then it should be treated with what we have said about treatment and lead.
Chapter 80.
On the treatment of fistulas that occur in the lower part (of the body).
Fistulas that occur in the lower body are nodules and swellings that happen outside the anus or in one place; they can be a single fistula or multiple ones.
When the nodule becomes old, it opens up and there runs out of it a watery white liquid or thin pus.
And sometimes there are penetrating fistulas among them. Penetrating fistulas are recognised by what comes out in the stool and by flatulence when the sick person uses the bathroom; perhaps worms come out of them too.
And there are fistulas among them when they penetrate into the bladder and all the way to the tip of the penis. And some penetrate as far as the hip joint and even to the tailbone.
From where the penetrating fistula is recognized as far as the anus, even for one that does not penetrate, you should insert your index finger into the anus and also introduce a fine probe of copper or iron into the fistula; when there is no curvature.
But if there is a curvature, then introduce into it a fine lead probe or an horsehair pin until you feel the probe or the pin on your finger.
But if you do not feel it completely and no something comes out of the orifice through evacuation nor wind nor worms as I said; then know that it is non-penetrating: so hasten to its cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating as far as the bladder or the urinary passage then its sign is the egress of urine from it and the hindrance of this so that the place may be consolidated with medicines.
If however it is penetrating as far as the hip joint or the bone of the hip then its sign is that a probe reaches there if there is no curvature up to the bone and the discovery of pain around the part and that pus always flows out from it; and also that treatment in it fails and it cannot be consolidated with ointment.
And for all these penetrating fistulae there is no cure at all and their treatment is labour wasted on them by stupid doctors who deal with it.
In those which are not penetrating and also fresh recovery is hoped for with the knife as I tell you and I have already proved it; and it is so that the patient should lie between your hands on his back and you raise his legs above his hips and let his hips incline towards his belly. Then they should insert a lead or brass probe if there is any twisting within the fistula until you know where it ends by using the probe to test.
If however he feels weak around the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If therefore you feel that the probe has already penetrated what was detected beyond which you would feel between your finger and the fistula or flesh, then indeed you know for sure it is penetrating; thus do not labour over it as there is no cure for it as we said.
They say however that flesh can be cured and by medication which is hoped to help, it should be done with a subtle fire cautery according to the size of the fistula as mentioned before, and insert it while ignited into the fistula until you reach the anus. Then repeat that twice or three times until you know all those added filtered flesh like bird feathers are burnt.
Then dress it with butter-soaked lint until those burnt flesh come out; then treat it with flesh-generating and consolidating ointments.
If therefore it is cured, that's good.
And if not, then it will never be cured with any other treatment.
And if the probe comes out and has not reached your finger which is inside the anus, and there is a thick membrane of flesh or fistulous tissue between it and the probe; and you see that the fistula follows the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from where the fistula starts, and keep cutting with the probe which is in the fistula until your scalpel reaches the end of the probe, when the probe will be released and fall out.
Clean then those filtered flesh that resemble bird feather pipes so that nothing remains in them.
But if blood defeats you and resolves between yourself and the surgery so that you must cut those flesh out then it is better for what has happened and faster healing to cauterise with fire or sharp medicine.
For a fire-cauterion brings together good dispositions of burning those added flesh and cutting off blood and drying out moisture.
Then care for that by wrapping it in buttered linen bandages or crushed cabbage leaves until the wound gives out pus and those burnt flesh come away into the pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments which generate hard flesh; and there are medicines that have both stypticity and drying qualities.
For once they become restored then it is already healed; and do not fear repetition.
But if the fistula has already reached deep into the anus and stretches out from excess body then put your finger into the anus and probe with it.
If therefore you feel a membrane or flesh between your finger and it being close to perforation then there is no surgery upon that unless by the way of faith and hope.
Which is because it administers upon that one of three ways: either to cauterise it as we said or cut it away until you reach its floor so that it's possible for you to insert dressings and care from nearby. And do not proceed far with cutting so as to not slice the anus-holding muscle and it happens there is weakness of bowel movement without will. Then treat it with what we have said.
It will perhaps heal; either remove the scab itself or cleanse it with what we have said, after you cut yourself close to the vein, as mentioned, either with tweezers or another sharp instrument. Then cleanse those exposed flesh parts that are in the wound as far as you can with scrutiny.
Then attempt the restoration of the whole wound with the incision and the upper hole where it has healed; there remains an open fistula on the inside part of the anus which is lighter over the perineum.
And sometimes a fistula follows like this: and that is when you insert the probe into the fistula; it lies on the side of the anus close to the surface of the body with skin and the margin of the anus; then take a pointed-probe, which is like a leathern awl, i.e., skewer, according to this shape: And insert into it a thread wound of five threads, and around that. The more the thread cuts into the flesh, bind it well until those parts which are between the two ends of the thread fall off; then treat the wound until it heals.

Chapter 81.
Regarding the stitching of hemorrhoids from which blood flows due to their incision: and treat fissures.
Hemorrhoids occur in two ways. Or they are inside the anus, like bags of charcoal or plums; or else they themselves are like grapes. And from them there are small ones, large ones; blood always flows from them, sometimes it is one hemorrhoid that bleeds, and at other times multiple do. And they occur outside the anus and on its edge.
But those hemorrhoids that are outside the anus have little moisture, from which flows a small amount of yellow fluid or thin blood; and they match the colour of the body.
And the treatment for internal hemorrhoids is to instruct your patient to go on all fours; and press until it comes out of their anus so you can see its appearance. That's why also use hooks to lift them; or hold them with your nail. Then cut them at their base.
If they do not have hooks to hold them because of their moisture and softness, then take them with a rough cloth or with a cotton swab made from double-layered wool, and extract them with your fingers. Then cut them open, and sprinkle on them after cutting some hot powders to act as a local cauterization. Or cauterize them according to what was described earlier about the procedure of cauterization. Then treat them with the remaining treatment until they heal.
But if the anus does not obey by releasing it, then give the patient an enema with a small amount of the biting enema, so that it washes what is inside the anus, and quickly obeys the release when the patient expresses himself.
However, if the thorns are external to the anus, then the matter is simple and easy; you should take them with your nail or hook them with a hook and cut them. Then treat them with what we said until they heal.
And for those who cannot bear the incision with an iron [blade], then you must administer their suture in this manner. Take a rolled-up thread and insert it into the needle. Then extract the thorns upwards and make the thread penetrate with a needle into its root from the other side; and roll up the two ends of the thread on the lower part of the needle such that they are at right angles to it; then tighten the thorn strongly with this binding. Then knot the thread and extract the needle.
Do that with all those who are helping you and let one of them go; don't allow yourself to be too hasty so as not to make the blood flow excessively.
Then place over the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerote, and put it on the anus; instruct the patient to rest. Then let him go until he falls.
When the helpers fall down, take care of them with ointments and the rest of the treatment until they are healed.
On the treatment of fissures. Indeed, they happen frequently due to dehydration of faeces and natural contraction.
When they do not respond and medicine does not help, it is necessary to scrape them with the point of a spatula or your nail until they become moist and remove from them the upper layer that prevents their healing. Then treat them until they heal as is appropriate.
But if they do not heal then repeat the treatment with more force than before until they bleed a lot and then make them suppurate; afterwards treat them until they are healed.
On the treatment of inverted piles, non-inverted piles, dry helpers, and ants.
Head (section) 82.
Know that Almirmar is nothing but a round knot of the colour of the body resembling the head of a nail; it occurs throughout the whole body especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes and causes pain when walking.
It is necessary, therefore, to cut what is around the nail, and it should be held with forceps or a hook, and then removed from its root.
Then treat it, and if you wish to cauterize it according to what has been described in its chapter.
On the other hand, there is one small, rough, flattened, circular protuberance on the dry athélis, above the surface of the body. And these often occur on the hands of children.
The treatment is to extend the athélis and cut it; or tie it with a silk thread; or with a hair until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it with fire or sharp medicine as has been mentioned earlier; and now the athélis are removed, both inverted and not inverted, using an instrument that I will describe later.
A formica is also a small, flattened protuberance on the surface of the body that penetrates deeply; and when it encounters cold, it feels like an ant bite, and appears all over the body, especially on the hands.
The treatment is to take a tube made from a goose quill or a strong vulture feather; and place the tube over the formica until it encloses it on all sides. Then turn your hand with the tube until you cut what is around the formica; and place it deep into the flesh to remove the formica from its root.
And if you wish, do it with an air or iron tube in this shape.
Place the upper part of the cannula towards the earlobe, drawing it around and upwards so that its grasp is loosened from your finger and its rolling-up.
And if you wish to cauterise an ant, do it as has been mentioned earlier regarding the cauterisation of Athelil in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter 83.
On the shapes of instruments that are used for enemas in diseases affecting the rectum, as well as evacuating the belly and treating colic.
Sometimes an enema is made of silver, or gold-copper alloy (i.e., brass), or cast bronze, or sheet copper.
And sometimes it is made of these instruments small or large depending on those to whom they are administered.
Thus that which is administered for the cure of small children should be small; and so also their enemas if those children have weak or painful rectums.
Large enema.
Its tube, that is the upper part to which the bag (i.e., the bladder used as reservoir) is tied, should be wide like this: and have a mark so that the bag is tied on like you see, and its lower extremity that is inserted into the rectum should be deaf, i.e., not open, very soft, drawn out to a point; and let there be two holes in one side of it, and on the other side one hole.
And let the width of the hole be according to the thickness of rays (i.e., thin branches) and a little thicker; and let it have a bag in which medicines are put made from an animal's bladder, or from sheep paper that is like the shape of a small asofra whose root is one palm.
And it is pierced with multiple holes as it revolves, and there should be a space between each hole the thickness of a finger. Then insert into those holes a strong thread made of raw silk, which should be ten strands or thereabouts, and attach to it the bladder as with a sausage casing. When therefore the bladder is put on it, tie it at the end of the enema tubes between the two distinguishing marks with a strong ligature. Then enema with it.
And this is the form of the circular bladder depicted here, as well as its holes.
The quality of enema with it is such that you fill the bladder or wineskin with medicine which should be heated according to the extent the sick person can bear; and take care that it is not very hot nor very cold.
Then the sick person is laid on their back and their legs are raised upwards; and put under them an inthaan, that is to say, a skin on which people lie down, or a cloth.
Then anoint the end of the enema tube with oil or with egg white or with fenugreek mucus, or the like. Then insert it into the anus easily. Then the attendant takes the bladder with both hands at once and squeezes it vigorously until everything inside the enema tube is evacuated into the intestine.
Then withdraw the enema tube and raise the sick person's legs to the wall.
But if the medicine makes him want to defecate then he should hold on for as long as he is able. If he sleeps with her for a whole night, it is all the more beneficial.
If however you give him an enema during the illness of abdominal pain and his discharge hastens; then repeat the enema until he is cured.
If there are haemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then use a very gentle enema, soft, of the type that is used for children's enemas.
And this is the form of a gentle enema, which applies to cases where the skin is tight.
Chapter 84.
I have already said in the section on wound treatment that a book was appropriate, and I will say here the treatment of them with the hand, and with medicines as a supplement.
I say, therefore, that wounds are differentiated according to the instrument by which they are caused and the place where the wound occurs.
The things by which wounds are caused, such as a stone thrown or an incision with a sword or knife, or being struck with a spear, wooden stick, arrow; and many similar objects.
Wounds that occur according to the place on the body are, for example, a wound which falls on the head, or neck, chest, stomach, flanks; and similar parts of these members.
And I will describe the treatments of some wounds, so that I may establish this reasoning and rule for other types of wounds.
I shall begin with simple head wounds, since the description of compound injuries will be given at the start of chapter three.
I say therefore. When there is a simple head wound and no bone fracture occurs: consider this; if it was caused by the impact of a stone or something similar, and has already broken only the skin. If it is a large wound and you fear an adverse outcome with hot abscesses then proceed quickly to perform a cephalic phlebotomy without delay and extract from his blood according to the strength of the patient: unless there is some impediment to performing the phlebotomy, especially if the blood issuing from the wound is scant. And let the phlebotomy be performed on the side opposite to that of the injury.
And let the phlebotomy be performed on the side opposite to that of the injury. And caution the patient against overeating, drinking wine, or eating fatty foods.
If, however, something prevents the patient from taking liquid foods then reduce their solid food intake and avoid meats or wines altogether.
And if a hot abscess forms on the wound then apply to it lard that has been melted in rose oil alone or mixed with wine having an astringent effect.
But if you are assured that there will be no hot abscess then apply to the wound, while it is still fresh with its own blood and before it has been exposed to air, the powder which I shall describe shortly: but if the injury has already been exposed to the air then use certain ointments instead until healing occurs.
But if the wound is large or caused by a sword-cut or something similar and its lips do not come together naturally then use stitches to bring them together as I shall describe for wounds of the belly.
If however the wound has already separated from the bone, and it is hanging, and there is no way to retain it except with a small sling; then cut it off. Then treat the wound with medicines and restore hard flesh to it instead of skin; instruct the patient to be fed with foods that are coarse, such as cabbage heads and boiled wheat, until it is like porridge, along with similar fare.
If you find a case of skin-deep gangrene that does not adhere to bone; then cut out the entire gangrenous area and throw it away; afterward treat what remains.
If however an artery or a vein from which blood is flowing should be found among these wounds; and it cannot be severed by medicine alone, then make a ligature above the vessel. If therefore you find that it has not been severed; then sever it with a scalpel or ligate it. If need requires it when what we have said does not suffice; then cauterize until the blood stops flowing.
If however these wounds are small and simple; then their treatment is easy, light: so that you sprinkle this powder on them: for it solidifies them before the air changes them.
Recipe puluerem ex lapide talco, et sanguine uenarioso commisto parum cum salicacae, et commisce eam in mortario. Take one part frankincense and two parts dragon's blood with three parts quicklime; grind all thoroughly together in a mortar, then sieve it: fill the wound with this powder and dress it well so that it adheres firmly to the powder and clots with the blood.
Then secure it from above with small sticks and leave it, do not release it as long as it remains so that it does not come off. Make sure the medicine is not removed from it until it heals.
And sometimes lime alone does this, when you do not have frankincense at hand, and the blood of a dragon also performs the same operation in many large wounds, when it is appropriate to place it on them and secure them, and they are fresh wounds with their own blood which has not been altered by air.
But if air has already altered them in some way, especially when it is summertime, then this powder is not appropriate for the purpose: so treat it with what we have said, such that you place on it certain digestive ointments: or place poultices of barley flour made with water and honey until they discharge pus. Then treat them with the remaining treatment until they heal.
If however there is a fracture with the wound in the bone and it is small, then extract it with Gesht: and I will now describe the treatment of head fractures later.
On wounds of the neck.
But if a wound occurs on the neck, then there is no difference between it and a simple head wound with regard to treatment.
But if the nerve of the neck has already been cut, or an artery: then if it is a nerve, there is no skill in treating it except to abandon its cure with what causes severe contraction such as Zinnia and Zea and similar things. For they harm nerves and contract them.
And do not place anything cold on the wound at all: for the substance of nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is loftier than the members; and let it be cured by medicines that are soft, such as lime washed with water often poured in with oil, or with rose oil, and tutia, when it is also washed with sweet water: and the rest of the mineral stones, in this way, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then apply a suture; or draw together its lips with stitches. Then treat it until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep, or if there is an abscess in its lower part, where now pus has already gathered, then pierce it in a deeper place on it.
If an artery has already been cut in the wound and its flow of blood has followed, then separate it or tie it off, and bind it; or cauterize it if necessity calls for that.
But if the larynx has already been cut into, or all of it, and the alhaudegi are saved: then gather together the lips of the skin with a suture over the cane of the throat, and do not touch the throat, rather equalize it, and bring it back to its natural shape. Then bind it with a suitable restraint, and leave it for several days. Then treat it with ointments that have styptic and drying properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has already made an impression in bone, then probe it; then if there are fragments of bone in it, take out all that you can of them; and what is not prepared, leave it until the wound festers, and its discharge separates and lessens.
If the wound is fresh, then sprinkle over it the powder I described. If there appears to be bone in the wound and a fistula forms, then treat it with that which I mentioned in its own chapter.
On wounds on spathels.
If it is the strike of a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider this. If breath escapes from the wound when the patient gasps, then you know that the wound is fatal.
But if it is not deep and is fresh with its blood, then do not put on it at first any powder nor bind it up tightly so that the blood may not be retained in its depth and return to the heart and kill the patient; but apply to it an attracting ointment twice a day.
If you have no ointment at hand, then put on the orifice of the wound a warm poultice to draw from it what comes out of its moisture; and make the patient sleep upon the wound so that what collects in it may run out.
If three or more days have passed since the wound and no spasm nor heart tremors, tightness when breathing, or other signs of illness occur in the patient, then you know that the wound is healed. Treat it therefore with linen cloths and other treatments until it is cured.
If its healing is neglected and it always reopens, then you know that a fistula has formed. Take care of it yourself from its own chapter.
And if the wound is from an incised sword or knife and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with what was described earlier regarding sutures if it is large, or with powder if it is small.
But if it has already made an impression in the bone and has cut off pieces from it, then investigate the wound, and hurry to remove those pieces if they are separated. If however they are not separated, then leave them until the wound decays as their removal will be eased.
The judgement of the remaining accidental wounds in other members regarding treatment is the same as we have said.
On abdominal wounds, and on protruding intestines, and their protrusion:
The rupture that occurs in the abdomen is large, medium, or small.
From a large one, sometimes an intestine or several intestines protrude.
Why does their protrusion happen and their suture is more painful and difficult.
And the small rupture also occurs in another way. Therefore it follows that their reduction is more difficult if you do not hurry to push them back into place immediately because they become inflated, open up and thicken which makes getting them in again challenging.
For this reason the medium rupture is better than that of complete ruptures because with it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult as it is in these two types.
You should know that there are four methods for sewing veins.
Two common stitches are used for the abdomen and other bodily injuries; two other types of stitches are specific to other wounds. However, they are more specific when it comes to abdominal stitches; I will describe each one for you with an explanation and clarification.
I say then that when a wound is small and something comes out of the intestine, making it difficult to close, it must be done in one of two ways. Either because of the small size of the wound, as we said; or because there is an inflammation inside due to cold air.
When this is the case, you should warm it up by dipping a sponge or damp cloth into lukewarm water only. Or you could boil water with squills, rushes, spikes, and embrocation, using it to soak the intestine until the inflammation subsides.
Sometimes alcohol with astringent properties will do this, and it's better than water alone at reducing inflammation.
Once you see the inflammation has subsided, moisten the intestine with water that has been boiled with marshmallow root or apples. For it soothes the entrance with something soft.
But if its exit is obstructed after this treatment, then you must look for a small opening in the wound with an instrument that cuts haemorrhoids.
The first treatment is better when it's prepared beforehand compared to cutting; there's no force necessary for the cutting once the tool is needed and fails.
One part of it is the curved, pointed end and the other part is not sharp; its fine point should not be as delicate as a spatula, rather let it be somewhat blunter; and that which resembles chalk you see there.
Thus when a wound is enlarged and penetrates the intestine, then it should be reduced back into its natural form in its proper place according to its own nature if possible; for this is better.
When there is a wide rupture in the lower part of the belly: then you should make the patient lie on their back and place their legs higher than their head. And if it is on the upper part of the belly then place their head and chest higher than their lower parts.
Similarly if there is a rupture on one of the two sides of the belly then direct your mind and always consider that the side where it has occurred be elevated higher than the other.
And so you should administer this treatment for both large wounds and medium-sized ones.
For small wounds, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then ensure that there is present a diligent attendant who holds the entire rupture with his hand and brings its lips together. Then expose it to the one who follows with something after something else for a single suture.
And it is such that you take a needle or the number of needles according to the size of the wound. Then measure from the edge of the rupture a quantity equal to your ring finger (that is, the physician's) and fix one needle beyond that, then thread it through both ends of the skin with two ends of suture beneath the skin until you have made them penetrate on the other side; and now you have brought together two ends of the skin and two ends of the suture: there are thus four strands.
Then bind with double thread around the needle several times from both sides simultaneously until the two lips of the wound are brought together in a proper union.
Then discard the bulk of the finger as well, and fix another needle into them; afterwards, draw it taut with thread, just as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease doing this with as many needles as you need until you complete the suturing of the entire wound.
And let the needles be of medium thickness between coarse and fine; for thin needles pierce the flesh very swiftly, while thick ones are difficult to insert through the skin; hence it is necessary that they should be of medium thinness and thickness.
And one must not fix the needles too close to both edges of the skin so that flesh is not quickly cut through and the wound opens up before it has healed or stretched out with the suture so as not to prevent healing of the wound.
Then blunt the tips of the needles so as not to hurt the patient while they sleep; and place on either side of them small pads made from cloth which hold the ends of the needles; and leave these in place until you know that the wound is now healed.
And this particular type of suture with needles, thus described, is more suitable for small wounds. Since one needle or two or about that number is sufficient for the suture of these wounds.
The second type of suture is common to bring together the four edges - two skin edges and two edges of subcutaneous tissue with a needle which has thread wound around it uniformly in terms of thinness and thickness.
Then, when you have penetrated with the needle these four extremities, bring the needle back from the side where you started it so that the threaded filament falls down from the upper part of the wound: let there be a suture like the stitch used to string clothes together, and place between each suture the thickness of half a finger.
People refer to this as 'sewing'.
And with this stitch I sewed together the wounds of a man who had been stabbed in the belly with a knife and whose wound was gaping open wider than a palm; about two palms' worth of his intestines had come out through the middle, and there was a rupture right across the abdomen.
I brought those intestines back inside after they had been hanging outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
So the wound healed after about fifteen days; I treated it and he recovered, living many years afterward and going back to his old ways.
And the doctors had judged that it would not heal.
It was truly miraculous because I did not treat him with any ointment. Because I was in a place where there were no ointments to be found nor any medicines. So I put an old linen cloth on the wound twice a day and kept washing it with honey water until it was healed.
I will now describe to you two types of stitches that I have used successfully in my practice.
And this is how you start with an acute suture: the needle should go in from the outside to the inside.
When the needle therefore has penetrated into the skin and having entered the muscle according to the length and width of the whole body: release the end on that side where you have inserted the needle, and make it penetrate from the inside to outside at the other end at Mirach.
Once you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate according to this into the same Mirach end from outside to inside: and release the Sifac end which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate into the other end from inside to outside: and make your penetration as you do when penetrating into the Sifac Mirach end, which is in its part, until it penetrates the whole thing.
Then begin again from this same side, and guide it with the end which is from Sifac on the other side, and extract the needle from the skin through which it has penetrated.
Then return it to that skin and guide the Sifac end which is on the other side with this Mirach end: and extract it from the skin of its part, and make one after another, and one after the other: and do so until you have extracted all of it externally according to that example.
The essence of this suture is that the sutures of hides are like those of skins: so that you guide Sifac one after another from one side with an end of the skin: and release it one after another from the other side, until you have completed it.
The second type of suture proper, which Galen calls.
His discourse about this is that you guide it according to the similarity of how some physicians use it, so that they bring together all parts to their appropriate counterpart by nature: and apply the Sifac end to its other end, and the Mirach end to its other end. And it happens just as I tell you.
You must fix the needle at the tip of Mirach close to you from the outside part and make it penetrate inward only; and release the two ends of Sifac. Then rotate the needle, and make it penetrate from the outside to the inside into the two ends of Sifac, that is, on both sides.
Then rotate both of them as well, and make both of them penetrate from the inside to the outside into the other two tips of Mirach which are on the opposite side.
And this method of stitching is better than the common and easy stitch, and it is a kind of stitching that has been mentioned before.
This, therefore, is also Galen's saying.
And some have said, The abdominal wound is stitched with two needles. Which is to insert into both of them one thread; and start with the insertion of the needle from what is near you, and make it penetrate up to the other side according to the stitching of those who are equal in skin.
And you should know that when there is a tear in the middle of the abdomen; then its stitching is more difficult than other parts of the abdomen.
The judgment of the wound's healing is the judgment of other wounds.
Which is because when you acquire a fresh wound with its blood, before it changes itself by air; and you bring back the intestine, follow it, and make it fit; then place it over red powder.
If the air has already altered the wound: then place over it certain ointments, which are available to you, until they produce pus; and let the filaments fall out, and let the sinews become consolidated, and the bone. Then treat it like the healing of other wounds until it is healed.
But if these medicines are not available to you, then place over it immediately, from the moment that it begins to discharge pus, an old linen cloth; and change it twice a day as I have taught you until it is healed.
For you do not need any other medicine for many treatments when the wounds are simple ones.
But if you fear that the wound may communicate with any of the principal members during their dislocation, then it is necessary to immerse soft wool in oil of a moderate warmth or in rose oil; and place them around those places which are between the groins and under the armpits.
If he feels pain or gripes, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens), then administer an enema to him with astringent wine, black, tepid; especially if the putrefaction has already reached the intestine and is about to break out penetrating right up to his hollow belly.
And know that the part from the large intestine is easier to heal; and that which comes out of the finer intestines is more difficult to heal.
The intestine which is called the jejunum does not admit of healing from a wound which occurs in it at all; and this is due to its great multitude of veins, and the size of them, and because of the fineness of its body, and the proximity of its nature to that of nerves.
If, however, what proceeds from the wound is zirbum, and you follow it while fresh: return it according to your own method of reducing intestines evenly.
But if some time has passed, and it has already become green or black: then you must tie it with a thread above the place that has darkened from it, so that there is no bleeding. For in zirbum there are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligament, and place the two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it: so as to relieve yourself of drawing out its attraction, and extracting it in case of a zirbum, and emitting pus of the wound.
Remembrance of the wound that happens in the intestine.
When disruption happens in the intestine and is small, then it may be possible for it to be restored in some people; because I have seen one who was wounded in their belly with a lance blow, and there was a wound from the right of the stomach, and the wound had become old; and it became a fistula, and there came out from it discharge, and flatulence.
So I treated him until I was not confident of his healing; nor did I cease to treat him until he had healed and the place had consolidated.
After I saw the place now consolidated, I feared for the patient that there would be an unfortunate accident in their belly; and no misfortune happened to them from it. And he remained in better condition, eating and drinking, and having sex, and entered the bathhouse, and exercised at his meals.
And now some experimentalists have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be sutured in this manner. It happens that you take ants of large heads. Then gather the two lips of the wound, and place one ant with an open mouth on top of the two lips of the wound. When it therefore bites down on them and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and does not let go.
Then place another ant near the first one, and keep doing so with an ant after each ant according to the size of the wound.
Then draw it together, and sew up the wound. For those heads remain attached inside the intestine until the intestine has healed and there is no longer any harm to the patient.
And now it is possible that the intestine be sutured even with fine thread: which has been removed from the gut of an animal and attached to it after being passed through a needle.
It is necessary that you take the end of this thread from the gut, and it be thoroughly cleaned of flesh. Then bind to its end a thin linen thread wrapped around it. Then pass that thread through a needle, and sew with it the intestine. Then draw it together at the wound.
And this method of suturing with ants and gut is only by way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound which is large and wide, especially if it is on one of the thinner intestines, then there is no cure but subtle treatment, nor is its healing at all.
On the cure of boils and fistulas.
Know that any wound or abscess when it becomes old and ages, and turns into an ulcer, and does not consolidate, and discharges pus constantly, and is not cut off: then it is universally called a fistula on whatever part of the body it may be, and we call it a boil.
A fistula is indeed correctly a hard, white nodule with filtering, without pain, which has the concavity of a bird's feather; and on account of this some call it Risati, that is, feather.
And at certain times it becomes moist, discharging pus. And perhaps the moisture is split up at some times. And sometimes this moisture is plentiful or scarce; it is thick and thin.
And I have already mentioned in the division, then when I said wounds, that any ulcer which does not heal and flesh is not born in it, it is not except for one of nine causes.
Of which one is either because of the scarcity of blood in the body or due to its ill-nature; or because between it and above its lips there is hard flesh, preventing the birth of flesh; or because it has an abundance of filth and dirtiness; or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter sent to it is of poor quality: or because the medicine used for its cure is unsuitable; or because the corruption falling on that region is a type of pestilence, or due to some property in that region, as happens at Caesaraugusta: where recovery from illnesses takes place with difficulty, and healing of boils by nature is delayed there; or because there is one bone, or number of bones, in it.
And now I have shown you all these causes and signs of the conditions, and their treatments along with the medicines in the section: and here it is necessary for me to make you aware of the treatment of fistula and zukën, which are carried out by means of manual operations.
Therefore, you should know that fistula occurs in all body parts. For among them are fistulas that reach major veins and arteries, or nerves, or the spine; or [those that reach] the intestine, or the bladder, or one of the ribs; or one of the vertebrae of the backbone, or one of the compound joints such as those of hand and foot; and among them are some which go towards the proximity of a main limb, and similar areas.
The fistula from among them that is like this is one of the difficult illnesses to heal, and it does not accept treatment, and dealing with it is labour-intensive and requires expertise.
But if there is any fistula whose depth does not reach these places that I have mentioned: and you are hopeful, and confident in its treatment, and want to attain knowledge of it, then take a probe made from brass or iron, if the fistula runs straight, and use it to trace [the path of] the fistula.
But if there is curvature in the fistula, then investigate it with a fine lead probe. Because the softness of the lead enables it to be bent and conform to the curvature.
But if the fistula has many openings, you cannot trace it with a probe. Therefore, you must inject liquid into one of its openings from among the openings.
For the moisture which the enema brings, enters via one orifice and runs out through others.
Then carefully inquire as to how you can tell whether it is bone or nerve, and if the cavity of the fistula is deep or shallow; or whether there is one fistula with multiple openings that covers all of this, using your utmost strength: through questioning the patient and finding pain upon compression with your hand on the site, and similar signs and causes and symptoms.
When you have thus stood at your true station, then go to the cure with confidence; and consider then whether the fistula is a shallow one that appears close by, or in a safe place far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the places I mentioned: then cut open the fistula as previously described, remove whatever may be draining from it and corrupted flesh which forms at its opening out of flesh or membrane and similar things; treat this until it is healed.
But if the fistula is deep and straight, then you must cut it open as deeply as possible. Then clean it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply linen wraps soaked in sharp medicines; insert these into the fistula cavity which you have opened with your knife: do this several times until the sharp medicine has eaten away all that remains of corruption within the fistula's cavity.
Then treat it with salves which generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it does not heal by this means, then cauterise it as we have described.
If the cause of the fistula is bone, and you ascertain this, then expose it and cut into it as I have described to you; if nothing prevents you from among veins, nerves or main members, as we said.
If you find bone and there is some decay or blackness within it, then scrape it until all the decay is removed. Then treat it with something that promotes flesh growth until it heals.
But if it does not heal and still discharges pus as before, then you should know that you have not succeeded in removing all the decay.
Therefore expose it again, and make a thorough investigation of its condition and cleaning with all your strength.
Then restore it. If it heals, that is good; but if not, then know that the decay lies deep within the body, beyond your reach. It is not within your skill to cure it, other than to leave it to nature.
But if the decayed bone is small and it is possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps that suit it.
If, however, there are many bones, then make a thorough extraction of them all, and do not leave any behind with your strength.
If any of the moist tissues offer resistance and there is no fear or mistake in cutting them open: then cut into those moist tissues and flesh if they are present there, and free the bones.
If you do not have the means to remove bones immediately, then place on the wound something that will cause it to rot; and leave it until what is around those bones rots away from flesh and moisture.
And guard the wound so that it does not heal. And constrict the incision you made during your procedure. In my opinion, place on it, if you fear it, a poultice of sulphur pounded with oil and moistened; or a poultice steeped in Egyptian ointment, or any one of the green ointments.
For when those moist parts decay, and the bones are separated from them, the removal of the former and the drawing together of the latter will be facilitated.
But if it is one large bone, such as a leg or hip bone, and what is corrupted of it is a large part; then scrape it with the ultimate razor until that blackness and corruption are removed. Then restore the wound.
But if what is corrupted of it is a large part and the corruption has already reached the marrow, then it must be sawn through and cut away entirely down to the place where the corruption has reached; then take care of it until it heals.
And I will tell you, Zuken, what happened to a man in his leg; so that you may take it as an example and help for your procedure.
There was a young man around thirty years of age, who experienced pain in his leg from a cause that originated inside the body; until the pus reached the leg and formed an abscess - and there was no external reason for it.
Thus was extended over time with him the error of the physicians until the abscess opened and many substances had mixed with it. And his cure was postponed until the leg became fistulated and many holes were made in it: all of which discharged pus and body fluids.
Thus he was treated by a multitude of physicians for about two years and there wasn't among them one skilled in the art of surgery until I was called upon and saw his leg and the substances that were flowing from those holes at great speed: also all the man's body had already wasted away and his colour was yellow.
I therefore inserted a probe into one of the holes, and the probe reached the bone.
Then I examined all the holes, and found that they interconnected with each other from every part of the leg.
Therefore I sawed and cut away the tissue over one of those holes until I uncovered a part of the bone; and found it to be corrupted: for it was already corroded and blackened and putrefied and perforated right through to the marrow.
I therefore sawed away what had been uncovered by me and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I judged that there was nothing in the bone but that corruption which I had cut away and sawn off. And by this point, I had already eradicated it.
Then I applied a long-term healing remedy to the wound as a means of restoration. And it has not healed (consolidated).
I then tried and placed a splint on the second bone above the first one; and I found that it was also contaminated with decay, so I removed what appeared to me from this contamination as well.
I then tried to restore it. And it was not restored.
I placed a splint on it again and did not stop cutting the bone piecemeal in an effort to restore it; but it was not restored until I cut out about one palm's width of bone with its marrow.
I then treated it with medicines and it healed quickly; this repetition of the operation and incision would not have been necessary had it not been for the condition of weakness and frailty of the patient, and his lack of endurance as well as my own fear over him of death: since he was fainting every hour from an excess of evacuation.
He has thus been completely cured; hard flesh was formed instead of the bone and a good condition established in his body, so that he regained strength and returned to his normal state, conversing with people again: there is no sign whatsoever of injury or difficulty while walking.
But if a protruding bone has already been broken at the body of it; then you must saw it off like this: take a ligament and tighten it to the end of the prominent bone, while someone else stretches it upward, and place another thicker woollen bandage on top of the first one. Then bind it to the flesh above the bone; and stretch its end so as to pull the flesh downward while you cover the area of the flesh that you wish to saw with a splint so as not to hurt it.
Place a wooden stick or board under the lower bone: for when you do this, it is not prohibited that it cuts through the corrupted flesh.
The lock must be placed over the infected area for a short time to prevent infection from entering into the hollow of the bone; this will not appear as an obvious wound and forces the use of another lock instead.
If the bone is infected and not protruding, but it continues into a connected portion; the infection being within its center or one of its sides, then you must remove all flesh from every side (of the injury). Then place a wooden stick below it.
After that, lock it from the first side where there is infection until the lock continues to the other side; and let the lock be slightly longer than the area of infection as we have said.
If the infection is at the joint, cut away that infected part of it; then lock down the bone from where it continues on its other side.
However, if the infection is between two joints there is no remedy other than to scrape it out.
If the infection is at the comb of the hand or foot, then the situation becomes very difficult to treat. But it is necessary that you inquire how exactly the infection presents itself to you. And you should scrape it out and clean it according to any arrangement that is possible for you; with whatever remedy appears right to you as long as this does not go against the vein or nerve.
You should be aware that there are many different types of scalpels and locks for cutting these bones; according to the location, preparation, thickness, fineness, size, smallness, hardness, and rarity of each bone. Therefore, it is necessary that you prepare every kind of instrument suitable for that particular operation.
