And know that these operations indicate to you the type of instrument needed, which you require when you have a lengthy study. And knowledge of the techniques of this art is a guide to the nature of illnesses; for then you will discover for yourself which instruments are suitable for every illness.
And I am providing you with a list of instruments at the end of this chapter, which serve as examples on which you practice, and as tests by which you may experiment with others.
Shape of another saw.
Shape of the scissors with joined blades. Let the head of these scissors be made according to the shape of a star-shaped nail; and let its points be like those of the eyelash-curling iron.
And it is not suitable unless the blades are rubbed with them when they become blunt or a large and thick bone is to be cut.
Shape of the larger scissors.
The form of a wide rope ligature.
Form of a curved clamp.
The form of another thin rope ligature.
Form of the other curved clamp with a hooked end.
Form of a small pair of thin forceps.
This resembles a lever; its end is like a saw: it appears in many places because of bones.
Form of the clamp which suits its purpose for perforated bones.
Its end is triangular; it has sharp ends.
It is made of Indian steel. And similarly it is necessary that all of the instruments we have mentioned be made of Indian steel, whether they are clamps, knives or saws.
On amputating limbs, and sawing bones.
Limbs become putrid either from an external or internal cause.
Therefore when you have treated that putridity by medicinal cure and it does not bring about healing; and you see the putridity spreading into the limb which is unaffected: then it is necessary to amputate that limb as far as the extent of the putridity, so that the patient may escape death or a serious ailment.
The sign that this has occurred is when the limb turns black and remains so until it is thought to have been burned by fire or to have gone bad after turning black; until that rot is cut away right up to what follows that limb and spreads throughout the entire body.
Therefore make the first cut appropriately, and likewise if the cause of the rot is from the sting of certain venomous creatures such as the sea scorpion, viper, adder, and similar things.
If therefore the rot or sting is on the tip of a finger then cut off the finger and do not give space to the rot so that it may spread to the rest of the hand.
And likewise if it happens to the hand then cut it off from its root at the wrist and do not give space to the rot so that it takes hold of the forearm.
And if it invades further then cut off the arm at the elbow joint.
But if the rot spreads beyond that point then there is no hope for the patient's life. And administering anything further in treatment at this stage is pointless.
And likewise for the foot; when rot invades a toe then cut it off at its first joint and if it takes hold of the whole of the foot then amputate the entire foot. If however it ascends to the knee then cut off the leg at its joint with the knee.
If however the rot has already reached above the knee then it is not a matter of skill but rather resignation and leaving the patient to death.
And the method for cutting a limb or binding it is to tie the tendon below where you wish to cut and then tighten another tendon above that place. And the attendant should stretch one tendon downwards, and the other upwards. And you separate the flesh that is between two tendons with a broad chisel until all the meat is exposed. Then it should be cut or bound.
It is necessary that you place a linen cloth on all sides and not touch the healthy area with the saw; for if an unhealthy part is struck, there will be added injury and hot abscess.
But if internal bleeding occurs while you are operating, then quickly cauterize the place or put some styptics on it. Then return to your dressing until it is completed.
Next bind the wounded limb with appropriate bandages and treat it until it is healed.
And I tell you that this same accident happened to a man in his foot.
Which is because a blackening occurred in his foot, like a burn resembling fire combustion; and that corruption first began in his toe until it started to affect the whole foot.
So the man decided to take action for himself after he saw that the corruption was spreading into his limb with increasing severity; which he found from its pain and burning sensation, and it had occurred at the joint, but now he is healed.
After a lengthy period had passed, he experienced the same decay in his index finger of his hand.
He came to me, and I attempted to treat the excess flesh with medicine that I applied to his hand after cleansing his body; however, the superfluity was not rectified, and it began to spread to another finger until decay set into his hand.
He called on me to cut off his hand, but I refused, hoping for a recovery from the excess flesh and fearing death during the amputation of his hand itself. Since the man's strength was above adversity.
After losing hope in me, he returned to his land and hastened back to me, having cut off his entire hand; thus, he was healed.
I have related this account not only as an aid for treating those who suffer from this illness but also to signify and describe what may be done in such cases.
On the treatment of fistulas and the nature of enemas with medicines.
When a swelling occurs in some fleshy parts and the condition persists until it forms an accumulation of pus; then it breaks or is pierced, discharging its contents and leaving an empty space like a vessel with only thin skin covering it: and this does not extend to complete decay nor affects bone, nerve or ligament. It should be called a Sinus rather than a Fistula; however, if the condition persists until decay reaches one of these structures then it is named a Fistula or zuken.
And its treatment is to cut that skin entirely as it peels off; especially when it has been thinned out and made like cloth; and be confident that it will not stick with the site because of its decomposition.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere and it does not follow from that last corruption; and if there is thickness in the skin from flesh, then treat it with an enema; and it is necessary to pay attention to the cavity.
Then if it is large and the pus that comes out of it has a foul smell; then treat it with an enema using Egyptian medicine made up of equal parts vinegar, oil, and ziniar, combined in a vase: boil them over a fire until the remedy reddens and becomes as thick as honey.
Then take as much of it as you need; dissolve it with water and honey, and administer an enema to the cavity using that mixture; squeeze its opening, and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then extract it by pressing; do this for as many days as necessary until the cavity is cleansed and the stench removed.
And sometimes it is treated with an enema of ash water when this remedy is not available to you; and be sure to use the ashes of grape vines or the ashes of holm-oak wood, throwing them into the water. Then mix it well and administer an enema with that water until you are confident that the cavity is now cleansed.
But if the patient cannot tolerate Egyptian medicine or ash water, then treat them with an enema of just water and honey to which some ground ziniar has been added. The enema itself with honey and wine mixed in it because of the property of honey is cleansing and washing out; and wine makes it adhere to the sinus, especially when there is excess astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you have done this a few times, and are confident that the corruption of the sinus has now been removed, then use an enema with what makes flesh grow there.
And it is necessary that you take some dyaphinitis plaster: and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine: or use an enema with those medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have described in the treatise on ointments.
But if the sinus orifice is narrow and does not admit the enema's cannula: then stretch it a little with an iron instrument: or place over it a linen cloth wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in balsam-ointment, until it is stretched.
And similarly if it is also a wide orifice, then draw together its lips with a suture: and leave of it the amount into which the enema will go without any extra length.
And similarly if its orifice from which the pus issues is elevated above the rest: then cut it at a lower spot on it so that the pus runs out downwards; for when pus is confined deep within the sinus, it prevents flesh from forming there.
If you cannot cut the sinus as low down on it as you wish: then try to prepare the member with a preparation that will make the pus run out of it easily as is prepared for you. Try to ensure that no vestige of pus remains within it at all: and take healing medicines which are put on the wound, suitable for what you wish. And extend them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that it accommodates the entire cavity. Then make an incision with scissors along the center of the cavity's hole, somewhat beyond the cavity's opening. Then apply a fresh poultice of soft ointment to another cloth, according to the size of the hole; and place it on top at a distance from the cavity's opening so as to ease the flow of pus from it: and do not remove the large cloth unless after some days have passed.
The one that is on the cavity's opening, you will remove every hour to allow what has accumulated in it of pus to drain out; and so that you may know whether what flows from it is more or less, cooked or raw.
And with this again consider the place of the cavity itself, whether its owner feels pain in it or not; and whether there is abscess in it or not.
When therefore your treatment is extended like this and you see the material that comes out over the cavity's opening, then you know that the cavity has already healed or is close to healing; so then dissolve it in the morning or on the third day: and when you heal the wound, then change the other cloth and renew the ointment.
But if a refined material comes out of the cavity after a long time has passed, then do not despair over its healing for it is near; since there are men who delay the growth of flesh in their wounds and some act contrary to that.
But if raw pus comes out of the cavity after many days have passed, then you know that the cavity is not healing.
If the adherence of the sinus is delayed and its condition prolongs, then add in desiccation medicated substances, but physicians should ensure that these medications are moist by substance yet dry by virtue; such as dyafinicon unguent when added to it an excess of zegi.
And for the more advanced treatment, take dyafinicon unguent and dissolve it with rose oil, then moisten an equal amount of old wine by substance over it.
Then prepare it with that and use it.
And it performs a similar action to this when you do not have dyafinicon unguent at hand: honey, when cooked until thickened, can be used instead. Or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense; grind everything together and powder it over honey while it is over a flame. Then spread over it a cloth and bind it to the sinus; coat with the honey you cooked until thickened and powder spices over it; place over the cloth and bind again.
And sometimes it is administered with long aristolochia, heaven lily roots, and true lily; the herb's flour and centaurea are also used. Use these simple substances or combine them; grind them together and sieve; powder over the honey I described earlier and use it.
If the condition of the sinus prolongs and does not heal with this treatment, then know that there is deep corruption; or it has already impacted the bones, hard tissues, or ligaments; in such cases treat as for fistulas, as previously mentioned.
On the treatment of panaritium and abraded nails, as well as incising and cutting a finger.
A panaritium is an abscess that forms under the nail of the thumb or toe, but may also occur on other fingers.
If it is left untreated and the abscess becomes hot to the touch or filled with pus until the root of the nail is destroyed—or even the entire nail itself—and perhaps reaching the bone until its smell is foul; then the finger's tip will swell, and its color darken.
So then when you treat it, along with what we mentioned earlier regarding the types of panaritium, and your treatment does not help; then you must cut away all remaining superfluous material left from the nail using an iron tool. Then cauterize the wound afterwards. For cautery is indeed very beneficial.
But if the bone is healthy and both nail and flesh are also so, yet its supporting nails now press against this interior flesh, pricking it and causing pain; then place a slender probe under the offending nail's edge that pierces the flesh, pushing it upwards: then cut away that flesh with ease, and apply what remains of any cauterizing and corrosive medicinal substances until all is removed. Afterwards treat it with ointments until it heals.
But if the rot has already set into bone then it is necessary to cut away and remove that bone; for it will never heal so long as the corrupted bone remains.
If you see decay and deterioration progressing into the finger, then cut it off at one of the joints, according to what has been mentioned above. Then treat it until it heals.
But if the nail suffers abrasion or a blow, and severe pain occurs within it, then you must first use phlebotomies on the patient. Then cut the nail with an acute spatula, making a transverse incision from top to bottom.
And be careful not to let the incision reach the flesh underneath the nail, for you will cause severe pain in the patient and it may result in an addition of flesh by nature at that place.
Then treat this place with what soothes the pain.
However, the extra finger that is born in some people's hands may be fleshy throughout; and perhaps there are bones in some of them, or even a nail; and some of these fingers are born at the root of the joint, while their nature is native to some joints of the finger. Therefore the one that is born on some joints of the finger does not move; and the one which is born at the joint of the finger may well be movable.
The sectioning process for the fleshy one among these is easy. This means that you should cut it at its root with a wide spatula.
However, the treatment of that one whose nature is native to some joints of the finger is difficult. Therefore abstain from cutting it.
However, if the tumor is born on a finger at the end of the joints, you must first cut his flesh with a round incision down to the bone. Then saw through the bone with one of those saws suitable for this task. After that, treat it until it is healed.
However, the stiffening of the fingers against each other often happens either from birth, that is, they are born with it, or as the result of wound-healing, or by firebrand, and similar things.
Therefore you must cut away that stiffening until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them strips of flesh, or cloths soaked in rose oil: so that they do not stiffen quickly, and separate them; or put between them a thin sheet of lead until the site is healed as it should be.
And similarly if stiffening happens to some fingers with the palm of the hand, then cut away that stiffening as is prepared and appropriate for the shape of the limb.
On the topic of incision for a hernia.
There are twisted veins, thick and full of melancholy superfluities that happen in many parts of the body; and their outcome is often in the legs, especially the legs of those who walk long distances and labour and carry heavy loads.
Therefore you must administer the expulsion of black bile from the body by strong pounding several times.
Then apply leeches to the patient's basilic vein.
The treatment of these conditions with an iron instrument is according to two methods. One of them is that they are incised, and black blood is extracted. And the other method is that the vein is raised, and the whole thing is removed.
Its incision is according to this method. Soak the patient's leg first with hot water until the thick feculent blood is loosened. Then bind the patient's leg from the upper part of their hip to the lower part of their knee with a bandage. Then make an incision on one spot, or two, or three places with a wide cut.
Then raise it, that is, extract the blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and you must have leeches on the patient first. Then make an incision to the nail down below until you see enough blood coming out and as much as the patient can tolerate.
Afterwards bind it and instruct the patient to avoid foods that produce black bile; and repeat the evacuation and leeching when the veins are full and harmful to the patient.
Its elevation is according to this method. Shave the patient's leg if there is much hair on it. Then immerse it in a bath or apply hot water to their leg until they turn red and the veins swell; or have them exercise vigorously if there is no bath present, until their limb becomes warm.
Then cut the skin along the vein's length, either at its end near the knee or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks and scrape the vein from all sides until it becomes visible; you will know when it appears because it looks reddish-black. So when it has been removed from the skin you see that it is white like a cord.
Then insert the rod beneath it until, when lifted up, it emerges from the skin; hang it with a blunt hook gently.
Then make another cut close to that cut, about three fingers away. Then scrape the skin from the upper part of the vein until it appears. Then lift it up with rods as you did before, and hang it with another hook just like at the beginning.
Then make another cut or several cuts if you need to. Lift it up again and make the final incision near the heel.
Then extract it to the cut that is above it.
And do this until you have extracted it from the top cut or highest of all cuts; when it is fully out then make an incision in it.
But if it does not obey you by coming out and lifting up, then insert a needle with strong thread doubled over and tie it, and pull it out; or insert beneath it a rod: turn your hand in all directions until it comes out. And beware, lest it be cut. For if it is cut, it becomes difficult for you to raise it greatly, and it enters the patient from that harm.
So when you have extracted it all: then place over the wound a piece of wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil; and take care of the patient until they are healed.
But if the artery is contracted, having a twist with an inward bend to its parts, and it is not as we have said straight: then you must cut over it on every side of its twists, and at the places of that bend.
Then lift it with hooks until you have raised it completely: and beware at your incision over it lest you cut the vein or wound it; for it will be difficult for you to raise or extract it. Therefore, preserve your prudence.
The manner of the cutting whereby an artery is healed.
The manner of blind stitching.
It does not have a curved edge like other stitches, nor is it sharp-pointed so as to wound the vein; and it has a thick, undulating shape that is gentle; for if it were thin, its thinness would cut through the vein: rather, let there be superfluous thickness to it, as we have said.
On the extraction of the femoral vein.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Alexandria and Arabia; and in dry, hot lands of little moisture, that is, where neither grass nor trees are born.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
Its generation indeed comes from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, just as happens inside the bodies of snakes and roundworms, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the onset of this varicose vein is a severe inflammation that occurs in the leg. Then the place should be cauterized. Then the vein begins to emerge from that place of cauterization, like the root of a plant or an animal.
When its tip appears, you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing from one to two drachms, and tie that in place; then leave the lead suspended from the leg.
Whenever anything comes out of it to the outside, wrap that in the lead and tie it up. But if it extends too far, cut off a piece of it, and wrap up the rest. And do not cut it off at the root until it has come out completely. For if you cut it off, it contracts and reenters the flesh; this causes an abscess to occur at that place, and rot, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it should be handled gently and extracted little by little until its very end comes out, so that nothing of it remains inside the body; indeed this vein extends outside some people's bodies to a length of five or ten palms. He has now arrived because a man left with twenty palms.
However, if it happens to you during the hour of your medication: then insert a rod into its hole and perforate it with a long hole, with the body, until all that is in it of matter is evacuated; and apply putrefaction of the place with medicine for several days. Then cure it with the treatment for boils.
And this vein indeed sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the ankle joint, or on the foot itself: and there are multiple openings to it, and a branch comes out of every opening.
Therefore treat it as we have said in the division, and with what has been mentioned before.
On cutting over worms generated under the skin, which is called Bovine Disease.
This disease is called Bovine Disease in a certain region among us: because it often happens to cows, and there is one small worm that is born between the skin and flesh.
And it enters the whole body, ascending and descending: and is felt during its walk from limb to limb, until it bursts wherever it bursts on the skin, and exits.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain juices. As happens with worms and snakes, that is, earthworms, and ascarids in the belly.
And it is feared for its harmfulness. Because when it enters the body and is raised towards the head, and reaches that place, then perhaps it opens there, comes out, and destroys the eye; and this happens many times.
So if you want to cure him and extract it, then this can only be done when he walks around, and its presence is revealed to the senses.
It's necessary, therefore, to tie up what is above him and what is below with a strong binding ligature. Then cut over it and extract it.
But if it is deeply embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then place a cauterizing iron on the spot until it dies.
And greater than that which is feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If however you see that it has already reached the head near the eye, then bind beneath it the eyebrow with a good ligature. Then cut over it and extract it.
And the patient must use cleansing of his body with medicines that dissolve bad putrid humours.
And he should beware of foods that cause putrefaction.
On the operation for the illness of Nakir.
This illness is called Nakir in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs; then it changes from limb to limb.
I have indeed seen it myself, just as I am telling you. I was called to attend to an ailing woman in certain villages. And I found her arm with a small swelling on the vein of the bicep.
And after I remained for an hour, I saw one swelling move with a whip, like a worm, ascending to her shoulder faster than could be believed; so that it was as if live silver were running from place to place.
So the pain retreated from that spot and fixed itself on her shoulder.
Then I sat for an hour, and it rushed into the rest of her body until it reached her other arm.
Then she told me that the pain was revolving throughout her entire body, just as I had observed.
I was amazed at the speed of its transformation from limb to limb. I had never seen this illness with my eyes in such a manner before. However I have seen many who experience pain that shifts from limb to limb; and I have not witnessed it with my eyes as I did with this woman.
And I do not think it is anything other than the fact that she was among those from dry villages where the veins were often found to be exposed; and thus this sensation of windiness appeared when it shifted.
And it is necessary that such sensations not appear according to this reasoning among those who are luxurious and whose bodies are moist with concealed veins.
When you wish to treat it and the patient feels that pain, then if it is visible to your eye as we said, then squeeze above it, and beneath it quickly; and cut over it until that trapped wind comes out; and cauterize the place.
If however you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it with body shaking and with that which expels wind, and makes it fart with foul-smelling pills, and pills made from serpent's flesh, and similar things.
On the removal of arrows.
Arrows vary according to their kinds, and according to the places in which they fall out of the body.
Their variation is because among them are those which have large and small heads, concave and convex. And among them are those which have three edges. And among them are those which have barbs. And among them are those which have stars.
But those that fall into the limbs do so in two ways. For either the limbs are from the main concave members such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lungs, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and things similar to them. When therefore it falls into one of these members and the signs of death which I tell you about later appear to you, then you ought to avoid extracting that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in many cases. And when these bad signs are not evident to you, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the limb, then extract it and treat the wound.
For when an arrow strikes the brain and pierces the bone, exiting towards the membrane that covers the brain, the following signs will occur: severe headache, scotomas, vertigo, redness in the eyes, swelling, reddening of the tongue, spasms, mental confusion, and vomiting bile. And perhaps blood will exit through the nose or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And from the place of the wound, a white moisture like pus will exit. And it exits with a moistness similar to that of flesh.
If these signs appear to you, then refrain from treating it, and extract the arrow if it has not exited.
The signs of an arrow having struck the heart are that it is near the left nipple, and with it comes a feeling as if it were fixed upon something hard, not empty. And perhaps the movement of the arrow is similar to the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound. Afterwards, it is followed by coldness of the limbs, cold sweat, and fainting. Therefore, you should know that death has surely descended upon them.
And the sign of an arrow having struck the lung is the exit of foamy blood from the wound, and the veins that follow down the neck will swell, and the patient's complexion will change. He breathes a great, deep sigh and seeks to draw in cold air.
If the arrow falls upon the diaphragm which is in the chest, then it is near the small ribs; and there will be a great panting with severe pain, together with a sigh; and all the limbs of the shoulders move.
And if an arrow falls upon the stomach, then perhaps something undigested may come out of the wound.
And if an arrow falls into the belly and pierces it, then something of faeces will come out of the wound or the zirb (a looped gut) or a ruptured intestine. There is therefore no hope of cure nor of extracting the arrow.
If an arrow falls into the bladder and urine comes out, and something goes from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger below (in the lower parts of the body), then you know that it is a mortal wound.
The remaining parts such as the face, neck, throat, jaw, aid (hyoid bone), spinal vertebrae, wishbone, hip, leg and similar limbs shall be treated according to many different methods; when an arrow has not struck an artery or nerve: and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I tell you that I have seen these arrows so that you may judge by it concerning your treatment.
Which is to say that an arrow fell into the tear duct of a man's eye at the root of his nose. I therefore extracted it from him on the other side beneath the fat of his ear; and he was cured without any harm to his eye.
I extracted another arrow from the eye fat of a Jew under his lower eyelid; and the arrow was already hidden, and I only found its small end with which it was attached to its wooden shaft: and this was a large arrow from Turkish bows, without ears on its iron tip.
The Jew therefore healed up, and there was no ill accident befall his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And this was an Arabian arrow, which has ears on its iron tip. So I secured it above the wound with two sutures, although by then the arrow was already submerged in his throat. I therefore operated on him with great care until I extracted it; and the Christian healed up.
And I extracted another arrow from the man's belly; we thought that he would die of it.
So after thirty days had passed without any change in his condition, I made a suture over the arrow and then devised a plan to extract it; and the man healed up completely with no ill accident befalling him thereafter.
And I saw a man with an arrow that had pierced his back; the wound over it was healed up. So seven years later, the arrow came out at the root of his hip bone.
I saw a woman with an arrow that had pierced her belly; the wound over it was healed up and remained so, nor did she suffer any ill effect from its presence: no change came to her condition, and during all her natural functions the woman found no harm in it.
I saw another man who had an arrow lodged in his face; the wound had healed but remained, causing him little pain; and there were many similar cases like this.
And I extracted an arrow from the nose of a man who was one of the king's nobles; it had struck him right between his eyes and then veered off slightly to the right, becoming entirely hidden within.
I was called upon to treat him three days after the arrow struck. The wound from the arrow had become very constricted.
I therefore tested it with a fine probe but could not feel the arrow; however, he himself did find the puncture and pain beneath his right ear. I hoped that this was simply due to the tip of the arrow having pierced him there.
I therefore plastered the area with an ointment possessing both drawing and digestive qualities, trusting that it would form a pustule, revealing to me some sign of the arrow so I could cut above it; however, this did not happen at all.
I thus tended to him with the plaster for many days; yet still no incident occurred that indicated the arrow had followed some course beneath his skin and meanwhile the wound healed over completely: he remained weakened by the extraction, until one day he felt something inside his nose – it was the arrow. And so he narrated this to me.
And so he narrated this to me. I therefore applied a sharp corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it opened up; and when I probed with my probe again, I felt the fine tip of the arrow adhering to wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound with that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared visibly to us; and it passed by me with him for about four months.
Then after the wound was enlarged and it became possible for me to introduce forceps into it, and move them without their obeying at the outlet: then I did not cease from sharpening and devising ways over it with devices until one day I began on it a certain device made of forceps according to what will come in form at the end of this chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restorable. Therefore I restored it, the wound became consolidated, and the patient was healed with a complete recovery; nor did anything at all harm him.
And I tell you the nature of the extraction of some arrows: so that you may put it as a rule and a sign over what I have not said.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not covered along with the narration: nor does a book comprehend it, and a prudent craftsman compares little over much: and what is present over what is absent: and he discovers new procedures, and new instruments in dealing with foreign matters that happen to this art.
I say therefore that arrows do not come out of the members in which they are fixed except according to two methods. Either by attracting them from the place through which they entered. Or by opposing from the other side.
And that which exits where it entered, or is an arrow proceeding through a fleshy area; and it is drawn out, and exits.
If it does not obey and exit from the hour in which it falls, then you should leave it for a few days until the flesh around it becomes rotten; and its attraction and its exit will ease.
And similarly if it is stuck in bone and does not obey you to exit, then leave it for a few days; and repeat with attraction and movement every day until it exits.
If it does not obey you to exit after those days, then you must bore around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine trepan, until the arrows are enlarged. Then attract it, and extract it.
If, however, the arrow is already concealed within the body and hidden from sight, then you must search for it with a probe. If you feel it, then draw it out with certain instruments suitable for its extraction.
But if you cannot [draw it out], because of the tightness of the wound and because of the length of the arrow inside deep down; and there is no bone or nerve or vein in that place: then make an incision over it until the wound is enlarged, making it possible to extract the arrow.
If there are ears by which it is held fast, then free the flesh that has become attached to them from all sides with every means at your disposal; and if it cannot be extracted due to the tearing of the flesh in splitting those ears or twisting them: continue until it comes out.
And when you are extracting the arrow from whatever location it is in, use hand movements and twisting with the scissors on all sides until you cause it to come out: using ultimate skillfulness so that the arrow does not break during this process making its extraction more difficult for you.
If you cannot extract it from the first moment onwards, then leave it there for several days until those things around it decay. Then try again; it will be easier due to the loosening from [the decayed flesh]. But if you encounter trouble because of a profuse flow of blood, then do what has been described in its own chapter regarding treatment: and guard your strength against cutting into any veins or nerves or tendons.
Use every means at your disposal for the extraction of the arrow: and let that be done with ease or tranquility; slowness and strength, as I have described to you.
You must ensure that when you are removing an arrow [sagittam], the patient is positioned according to the angle at which the arrow entered their body. As it is more beneficial. If this is not possible for you, then use whichever of the positions that is feasible.
An arrow which exits from the opposite side of the body; or if part of it has already come through to the outside; or if one end of the arrow is causing a lump under the skin on the upper side, and you can see this protruding: cut over this area with a knife, and make an incision large enough for you to grasp in forceps.
Then pull it; it will be easy now to remove.
If the arrow is stuck to bone, then rotate your hand following the curve of the bone until the arrowhead cuts into it and enlarges its hole; then pull on it.
And if not, leave the arrow for several days; then try again until it comes out.
If there is any wood from the arrow shaft still present inside the wound, then push it out with this.
If the shaft has broken and you wish to push it out with an implement, then insert a concave tool inside the wound so that its cavity fits over the tail of the arrow; then use this to push on it.
If the arrow is concave, however, then use an instrument which inserts inside it to push on this. The arrow will be pushed out by the implement.
If the arrow is poisoned, then you must scoop out all of the concave flesh where the venom is; if this is possible for you to do. Then treat it with what is appropriate for that.
If the arrow has fallen into the chest, or abdomen, or bladder, or side; and is close enough to be reached with a hook, and it's possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and avoid cutting any veins or nerves, and extract it.
Then sew the wound, if you see fit, and treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
The ends should be like the beak of a bird, which are made sharp like iron; when they grasp the arrow, they do not release it.
And many kinds are made from them, large and small, and medium, according to the size of the arrow, its slenderness, and the width and narrowness of the wound.
Form of the concave driver.
And the form of the deaf driver.
This is the form of the deaf end like a wedge, to ease its entry into the concave arrow, and drive it with that.
On the cutting of veins.
There are thirty veins that are regularly opened for bloodletting.
Among these, there are sixteen in the head: two pulsatile veins located behind the ears called 'Herbae', and two arteries visible at the temples; also, there are two veins found in the lacrimal ducts of each eye known as 'Aspicientes'; additionally, there is a vein situated on the midline forehead, and one placed at the tip of the nose. And two 'Guidegis', which are found in the neck. Also, there are two veins that are present on the upper lip of the mouth. And another pair of veins located below on the lower lip. These, then, are the vessels known as 'Algecherichs'. And there exist two veins situated beneath the tongue.
The veins typically used for bloodletting in the arms consist of five vessels on each arm.
One is the Cephalic vein, positioned on the outside of the forearm; it's commonly referred to as the 'head' or 'capital' vein by laypeople. And then there is the Fusca, or the dark/brownish one. The next is the Median vein, composed of branches from both the Basilica and Cephalic veins; it's commonly known as the 'body' or 'trunk' vein by laypeople. Following is the Basilica, found on the inside of the arm; it's also known as the 'Titillicar'. Laypeople call this the 'belly' or 'abdominal' vein. And the tendon of the arm, which is located above the wrist: it is the one that is bled and appears over the thumb with a clear manifestation.
And the Salvatella: it is the vein that lies between the ring finger and little finger, and has two branches.
In the leg and foot there are three veins. One of them is the one that lies beneath the thumb's curve on the side of the forearm. And the second is the Saphenous vein, which lies at the heel on the inner side. And the Nesse Vein, which is located at the heel on the lateral side.
In the other leg there are three veins similar to these.
Regarding venesection of two veins behind the ears.
The venesection of two veins behind the ears is helpful for old catarrh, headaches, facial swelling and long-standing head ulcers.
The method of venesection should be as I describe it. The patient's head should be shaved, and their occipital region rubbed at the site of the two veins with a rough cloth using vigorous friction. Then the patient should tightly bind their neck with a cord until the two veins become visible. Their locations are behind the ears, in the two hollows of the head. Feel for them with your finger, and where you detect their pulse beneath your fingertip, there make a mark with an ink-soaked swab.
Then take the lancet, which is called the 'Incidens.' Then introduce it beneath the vein into the skin until you reach the tip of the lancet. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein along with the skin using the Incidens. And let the length of the incision be equal to that of two joined fingers, or around this amount: and make as much blood come out as you wish.
Then compress it with small bandages, and leave it until it is healed.
And already at the beginning of this book was described the incision of such things, as well as their cauterization.
Indeed the puncturing of two arteries in the temples is beneficial for an old headache; an evil sore, and frequent eye-ache; as well as a superfluous flow of sharp matter into the eyes.
And here is how you puncture them, as I describe it to you. The patient must hold their neck very tense until two veins become visibly apparent and their pulsation is felt beneath your finger. Then mark these with the encaustic pen.
Then lift the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger, introduce the Incidens underneath and then raise the vein upwards. And cut it like you did with the other two veins; and make as much blood run out as is necessary for you.
Then release the patient's constriction and place your finger over the vein for one hour. After that, place cotton and poultices over it, and tightly bind it with a strong compression; leave it until it heals.
And now there has already been a reminder of those things, their incision, and their extraction at the beginning of this book.
However, the indication for the venesection of the frontal vein is after the venesection of the cephalic vein, for old facial diseases; and for headaches, swelling; and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its venesection is as I tell you. The patient should tighten their neck with a cord until the vein appears. Then take the instrument that is called the probe.
Place the sharp point that is at the head of the probe over the vein itself and strike it with a comb or another object like it; let the blood flow out according to the amount you desire. Then release the patient's puncture wound and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad lancet. However, it should not be sharp-pointed like the other lancets, but rather broad at its point. And you should lance it across the vein because the bone is close by; and perhaps a thin lancet will break inside if you do not.
The use of the two veins which are in the two lacrimal glands of the two eyes is for eye ailments caused by scabies and inflammation; also for swelling in the eye and illnesses affecting the face.
And the way of performing venesection on them is that the sick person should press their neck with a bandage. Then venesect these [veins]: and you be standing over his head.
And let the venesection be done transversely along its length with a small scalpel, which is broad to some extent because there is not much flesh at this place. For if the scalpel's end is thin it may well break.
Then make [the blood] run out as much as you need to. And place on them a compress, and bind them for one night, then release them.
The veins of the nose that are to be venesected, are relevant to acute fever and severe pain, and illnesses affecting the face like migraine and inflammation occurring on the face; especially when they are longstanding.
And the way of performing venesection is that the sick person should press their neck with a bandage. Then hold his nose with your left hand and take a fine scalpel that is long; place it in the middle of the rabbit's snout between its nostrils. Since the vein is not apparent to the senses there, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And you must press your hand firmly with the lancet for a moment; and make the blood flow according to your need. Then bind it for one night. For it is quickly restored.
Now two advantages of Guidogi's venesection are relief for dyspnea and the onset of leprosy, as well as melancholy diseases, impetigo, bad ulcers, scabies, that is, itching eruptions.
And the manner of these venesections is that the patient should be bound under both arms at his neck with a bandage; and the operator should stand over the patient's head, while the patient sits on a chair.
Then lance the vein with an extensive venesection for a moment. Then extract an appropriate quantity of blood; or according to what you see is required in the case. Then do likewise with the other vein.
After that, loosen the bandage and bind the two veins moderately tight; so as not to suffocate the patient. And let it remain until morning. For the wound is healed.
On venesection of the veins, by Algeberich. Now the advantages of Algeberich's venesections are that they contribute to salivation in the mouth and the corruption of gums; bad ulcers; and cracks on the lips, as well as ulcers which are within the nose or around it.
And the technique of bloodletting is as follows: make the patient sit before you and tighten their neck with your hand around the vein. Then turn the patient's lip upwards and look at two veins, one of which is on their right lip and the other to its left; they are both identifiable by dark coloration. These veins are true because around them are smaller black ones. Make an oblique cut into both of them.
If you are in doubt as to which one is which then look for the larger and more pronounced of them.
Do the same for the two veins on the upper lip, and indeed it is customary to do so; make a cut into both of those which are in the lower lip.
On bloodletting from the vein beneath the tongue after bleeding at the temple. Bleeding from the two veins beneath the tongue is beneficial for swellings of the throat and for inflammations affecting the uvula and mouth.
The technique for bleeding these is as follows: make the patient sit between your hands facing the sun; lift their tongue and look beneath it on one side for a vein that is dark in colour, then do likewise for the other side.
Bloodlet from both of them; be careful not to hurt your hand while doing so. For beneath them lie arteries, and it may be that blood will gush forth from these.
The three veins that are commonly used for bloodletting in the elbow are those which, when lanced, result in a flow of blood from all people.
And these veins are bled according to two methods. Or by pricking with a broad myrtle-stick lancet; or to lance and open up the vein gently with an ivory tube; or to dry-cup with a lancet, this is called alnesil. And this is its form.
And from these there are broad and narrow types depending on the width of the veins and their tightness. The form of a myrtle lancet is broad as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave, full, prominent, apparent, thick veins, and those that contain thick, cloudy blood.
And this lancet is less broad at its end and finer.
And it is suitable for the bloodletting of fine veins which contain thin yellow blood.
The basilic vein, which is one of these three bleeding veins, provides the greatest benefit. Because it removes blood from illnesses below the throat and neck, following onto the chest and belly; and it is suitable for the surgeon to bleed this with care, being above fear of it because beneath it is an artery. If he errs and adds to the incision during bloodletting, he will sever that artery, and there will be a flow of blood. Therefore, it is necessary not to bloodlet her with the sharp bloodletter; rather, she should be cut by a surgeon using an alenesil.
If the basilica does not appear with a clear manifestation, then you must move away from it, change to another place, or seek one of its branches; alternatively, bloodlet at the place of the arm's string (i.e., tendon). For it is clear, and cut it with the anesil bloodletter, as we have said.
But if you wish to bloodlet it in the same place, then before the arm's constriction, you should touch the place until you know the pulse's place. Then mark the place with a cauterizer, afterwards bind up the arm and cut the vein with a concave incision using an alenesil bloodletter, as we have said. And take care that the strike does not fall through from the place of the artery.
And when you see that the place you marked with the cauterizer swells up as you bind it, then that swelling is of the artery; therefore, avoid it.
But if you see the blood during the bloodletting rise like a child's urine and it is thin, red blood, then know that it is from arterial blood. Then hurry and put your finger on that long space; afterwards take away your finger. If the blood is checked (for it often happens), then bind up the arm and let it go; and caution the patient against moving it, and be above his fear.
And it should not move for a few days until it is healed.
But if the bleeding is not stopped, and it defeats you; and you do not have medicine with you at that moment, then cut into the artery, should it appear necessary to you. For its ends draw together, and the bleeding is stopped.
Or take the bark of the pistachio tree, and grind it; take half of this amount, and press it on to the place where the vein has been tied appropriately with a bandage and some lint until the next day.
Then if the bleeding is stopped, it is good. And if not: then treat it with that which was mentioned previously from the place where the lint used to stop the bleeding is removed; and its bleeding will not be difficult in most cases, due to the small location of the wound, and the possibility of tying off the artery using a bandage from the arm.
The benefit of the ligation of the jugular vein is that it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; because it is composed of a branch of the axillary artery, and a branch of the superficial temporal artery, as we have said.
And it is fitting for the patient that he should be above any fear arising from this ligation, for beneath it lies a nerve. If therefore he adds to the ligation by cutting into it, and finds a nerve, an accident of numbness occurs for him, and its healing becomes difficult, perhaps even impossible.
And this nerve often appears to the senses. But if it is hidden in some people and is delicate, and does not show itself, then you must place your fleam so that you may bleed it with Al-Zehra's incision, and divert the nerve with your strength.
If the vein is between two nerves, then cut the vein lengthwise.
Concerning the cephalic vein. However, the bleeding of the cephalic vein is beneficial because it draws blood from the head and directs it towards eye ailments.
And in this vein specifically, if you wish, it is necessary to bleed it by pricking with a fleam of olive wood or myrtle wood, for it is safer than all the veins because there is no artery nor nerve beneath it.
However, it is appropriate for you to divert the forearm a little with your fleam and seek out the softer spot; and if it does not appear upon first percussion, it causes no harm to repeat the bleeding several times over it.
However, perhaps an abscess forms in some people when it is not bled upon first percussion, but this abscess does no harm to them.
Concerning the utility of bleeding, and precautions to be observed therein. The quality of bleeding, and its accidents that arise from it, whose correction you ought to consider.
First then of this you ought to know, that bleeding is not administered unless for the preservation of health and its continuation, and caution against the onset of sickness.
And for phlebotomy to take place on one of the three veins that are in the elbow region, namely the Cephalic, the Throbotic, and the Basilic vein. And for phlebotomy to be carried out at the beginning of spring before a fast and during summer: likewise in autumn, and winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
And let the phlebotomy be performed on the first day or third after three hours have passed.
However, phlebotomy administered for illness has no set hour; rather it should be done when necessity and convenience demand it at any time of day or night.
And it is not appropriate to perform phlebotomy on children until they reach the age of fourteen. Nor should a pregnant woman undergo phlebotomy unless she is between her fourth to seventh month of pregnancy. Nor should elderly people who have already passed their sixtieth year undergo phlebotomy.
Therefore, if one wishes to undergo bloodletting for any reason, it is necessary that they cleanse their intestines beforehand with a mild enema, should there be an excessive amount of stool retained: so as not to draw from the veins during phlebotomy any noxious putrid superfluities from the gut which could harm vital organs.
Nor should a patient who is suffering from nausea due to overeating undergo phlebotomy; nor while drunk or recovering from drunkenness until these effects have subsided. Nor should a patient who is suffering from dropsy undergo phlebotomy.
And cauterization should be avoided after colic pain, vomiting, abdominal pain, sexual intercourse, labor, exercise, lack of sleep, fasting and anything that dissolves the virtue from physical or animal matters.
Then consider the subtleties of humors before that, if the blood is thick with food and drink, and medicines: if it is possible for him to do so.
Then he should enter the bath, if nothing prevents it; or he should engage in some exercise to refine his blood.
And perform his phlebotomy on the chest, around the third day, as we have said; and try to ensure that his chest is evacuated of all evil animal accidents that day: such as distress, anger, and fear; and from all physical accidents, such as labor, pain, excessive sexual intercourse, and similar things.
And let him have present at his residence things with which custom has established the use of humans due to their pleasant aroma and fragrance, and cheerfulness, and similar qualities as much as possible.
Then the phlebotomist should sit on a cushion that is higher than the patient's; afterward, he should draw out his arm and rub it twice or thrice with his own hand. Then he should tighten the ligature with a strap and wind it around twice.
And the binding should be moderate, for when it is uneven or excessively tight, it then obstructs the flow of blood; and when it is too loose, it also obstructs the flow of blood.
Then after the binding, it is necessary for the patient to rub his hands together until the veins fill and become apparent.
Then the phlebotomist should clean the phlebotomy wound with a little old oil.
Then they should place their left index finger on the vein itself below the spot where they wish to perform the phlebotomy, just a little: so that the vein does not escape and the strike is broken. For there is a vein that you will find, like a fleeing rope at the time of phlebotomy: and among them is one which is full of wind; and when you place the lancet on it, it contracts under the lancet, and deceives the phlebotomist, and does not open the vein: and if it opens it, it is tight; therefore for this reason the phlebotomist must be firm, and be careful in all these matters.
Then put down the lancet. If therefore the vein opens with one thrust, then that is good. And if not, then repeat that thrust elsewhere under that spot a little, or above it quickly: if the area does not become hard.
If the area becomes hard, or the patient becomes weaker, then leave him for a day, or two days: and do not bind tightly with a bandage. For perhaps it will draw the hot swelling to itself. Nor should one enter the bath.
Then repeat the phlebotomy if he likes it.
If the opening is small, or the blood runs thinly: and you fear that not enough blood will come out: then repeat the lancet in the same hole according to its direction, and add a little to your action.
Do it with haste before the site becomes infected, for the site often becomes infected after a minor surgical procedure.
If you see that the site has already become infected, then do not operate on it at all because nothing will excuse you in this case. And place over the site something made from oil faeces, because it alleviates the flow of blood. And this is better than plain oil itself, and also better than all other oils.
Similarly apply oil faeces for every venesection you do to prevent the flow of blood; and they make a mixture of it with alfarachio tyrian myrrh and saffron when either one is placed on the site.
For thick blood thins and resolves when it is made thicker.
If a large infection occurs at the site of venesection, which often happens especially when one has not been bled or there is an opening into a small vein, then hurry and place over the site a sponge soaked in warm water and salt; press it for an hour because it will resolve.
And you must do this after the blood has stopped flowing out of that same vein or from another one.
If there remains in the site after a few days some blackness or greenness then this does not harm; and if you wish apply over it some aloe or liquid myrrh, or something made from mint juice like peppermint or similar.
Infections and swellings often happen after venesection on the Basilica vein.
Place your hand over it, then if you find softness under your compression on it, then that prominence is bad. Therefore, cause it to be placed over it something of what we have said. For perhaps blood will flow from it of the artery.
But make a plaster for it with that in which there is astringency, so that the place may harden. Then take care of it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you draw from everyone of blood according to the quantity of their strength. And what appears on top of the blood, conquering it in color.
For if the blood is black, then let it go out until it becomes red; and similarly if you see it thick, then let it go until it becomes thin; and likewise if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for him who is full and strong and requires the extraction of blood to increase the vein's venesection and let it be wide; and on that which is weak, let there be the opposite of this.
And it is necessary that you draw it from the veins and let there be a narrow hole; and what is better for venesection is to make a cross-section, not a puncture.
And this method of venesection is a safeguard against an overflow of blood and from cutting the nerve; and it is more praiseworthy and safer than venesection by breadth or length.
For the person who is accustomed to fainting during bloodletting, it is necessary that they eat before the procedure a small amount of bread soaked in pomegranate juice, or heated secaniabin; and draw out for them some blood in three or four portions.
And if they are of a cold constitution, then they should take before the bloodletting bread soaked in grape syrup, or in spiced honey syrup, or in perfumed aromatic syrup.
If a fainting incident occurs during the bloodletting and it is due to excessive loss of blood, then they must take as a drink meat broth and delicate perfumed syrup: and use an aromatic compound with aloes, and bind their chest with it; and employ the other methods that we mentioned in the section on fainting in chapter 3.
Whoever wishes to recover their arm strength and release their blood a second time: it is necessary for them that the bloodletting be done over a large area of skin, and their constitution is weak; they must release the blood gradually according to their condition on consecutive days.
Whoever wishes to recover their arm strength and release their blood again but whose body is strong should do so after seven or nine hours from the first bloodletting.
Whoever wishes to draw the blood from their body towards the opposite side of where they lean must recover on day two or three.
When there is much heated blood in someone's body and it causes a fever to develop, it must be let out all at once with a large quantity being drawn from them; the wound must be enlarged until they faint; afterwards you must observe all conditions of bloodletting, and place your hand on their pulse near where the blood flows so that death does not occur in place of fainting. Indeed it often happens that when the bloodletter is ignorant and carelessness occurs.
And when you want to release the arm and let out blood for a second time after the opening of the vein is already closed and the outflow of blood becomes difficult, it is not appropriate that pressure be applied to it with force, but rather it should be extended quickly while curved. For this is a very bad thing indeed; or rather let it be, until the bloodletter comes again.
Or remove with the sharpness of the surgical tool that which has coagulated from blood at the opening of the vein and place over it some salt dissolved in water. And place over it some of the unguent called alfaroch or sage-based and apply gentle compression until the blood flows out.
But if the vein has already developed an abscess, then let it be and do not lance it until the abscess subsides.
But if necessity calls for letting out blood a second time and there is no avoiding it, then either lance it over that place or else do so on the other arm at another vein.
The bloodletter's cord is tied to the arm at the points of Fuscus and Basilicus when they are not found and remain hidden because it is compounded from them.
And the nature of his bloodletting is such that he should immerse his hand in warm water until it becomes red and the vein appears clearly.
Then let him be bound over it with a bandage having a moderate tightness. Next lance it a little at a time across the width of it and not lengthwise; let your opening be broad and make your lancing where you join the hand somewhat.
If the flow of blood is obstructed, then dip your hand back into warm water and release the blood to run into the water until you have achieved what you need to do.
If however you were to be working during the summer months, then you would be exempt from repeating the action of dipping your hand into warm water. And indeed it is mostly recommended to release the flow of blood into warm water during wintertime.
And the bloodletting from this vein is safer than any other, for there are no pulsating veins or nerves beneath it.
Bloodletting from the right hand's "Salvatella" vein is beneficial for ailments of the liver.
The technique of the bloodletting is such that you should tie a piece of mahason cloth around the arm with a ligament or your own hand, after having dipped it into warm water, until the vein fills and becomes very apparent to touch. Then proceed with bloodletting by piercing across [the vein]. And if you cut it fully through, that will not harm the patient at all.
Preserve the [bloodletting] instrument in a safe place; do not finish with your hand. Under it, after all, lie the nerves of the fingers laid bare of flesh.
Then dip your hand back into warm water again and let the blood run out into it. For if you do not dip your hand back into warm water, the blood will clot at the opening of the vein and be obstructed from flowing out.
When you have removed enough blood, then apply oil and salt over the vein so that it does not quickly harden.
Similarly, you should do likewise with every narrow vessel.
The indication for this type of bloodletting is from the left hand since it corresponds to ailments of the spleen and proceed with its bloodletting as you did with the second one similarly.
The indication for the saphenous vein's bloodletting is ailments of the lower part of the body such as womb disorders, retained periods, kidney problems, old hip and leg ulcers, and similar conditions.
And the procedure for its bloodletting is that the patient should put their foot into hot water and rub it until the veins stand out and then bind above the ankle with a leather strap. The veins' location is indeed at the heel appearing towards the big toe and many branches spread from it on the foot's surface.
So bleed it at its wider branch or at the heel where they gather because this is better and safer.
If you bleed it on the foot's surface then beware of nerves that are beneath it over the foot's surface.
And make your bloodletting where you are bleeding it transversely as if you wish to cut it and let the lancet be a Nesil one.
But if the flow of blood is impeded then repeat putting their foot into hot water and let the blood run into it until it is evacuated.
If the phlebotomist makes an error and misses the vein on the first attempt, then repeat the phlebotomy slightly higher up, for it is a safe place, and do not fear any harm from it, as you are avoiding the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly with the other foot's saphenous vein.
The location of the sciatic veins, as we have said, is at the heel on the side of the hock.
And the benefit of this phlebotomy is relief from hip pain when it occurs because of hot blood.
And the procedure for this phlebotomy is as follows: the patient should enter a bath and hurry; press their legs from the side of the hip down to above the heel with four fingers using a thin, long ligature; it will not be visible unless because of that.
When it becomes visible, then phlebotomize it however you find possible, either according to its direction or transversely; and this is better, or by making an incision or a cut. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most people.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most people. If you do not find it and it does not appear at all to the sense: then phlebotomize one of its branches, and that is the one which appears on the back of the foot towards the anklebone; and avoid the nerves, and let as much blood flow as you wish.
Then release the ligature, and place skin over the site of the phlebotomy, and bind the site. For it heals quickly.
The ventosa, and its qualities regarding usage.
Ventosas are made from horns, brass and glass.
Indeed the ventosa has two modes of application. One method is with scarification and bloodletting; the other is ventosa application without scarification.
This latter ventosa treatment, which is without scarification, has two further methods. One method is with fire... Or without it.
There are fourteen places on the body where ventosas may be applied with scarification and bloodletting. One is the ventosa for the elbow, and it is at the back of the head; another is the one for the nape, and it is at the centre of the neck; those for temples are beneath the jawbone on either side of the mouth; there is also a ventosa for two flat bones, and this is placed below the mandible in the lower jaw region. Additionally, there is a ventosa for the tip of the tailbone and another for two hollows, which are at the centre of both arms; further, there is one for two legs, and another for two kneecaps.
The ventosa does not remove blood from the many small vessels scattered within flesh and thus its power is not diminished as with phlebotomy. A ventosa application should not be used for any sickness caused by over-fullness until the whole body is evacuated.
If necessity requires the use of cupping for illness or due to custom, we perform it at any time: at the beginning of the month, at its end, and in the middle as well.
That is because there are people who, when their blood multiplies within them to the point where they require its removal through cupping, find heaviness in their head and dizziness.
And there are those among them who experience fullness and redness on their face, head, and neck. And there are those who experience itching on their face and forehead, darkness, and dullness in their eyes.
And among them are those who scratch the place where they were cupped. And there are those whose laughter increases. And among them are those who taste blood in their mouth; either an ulcer appears on their tongue, or they spit out blood. And there are those whose sleep increases. And there are those who see blood and redness, as well as dead people, injuries, and things similar to them in their sleep.
When we see any of these symptoms, especially if it is on the thirteenth day of the month: we order cupping to be performed then after two or three hours have passed from that time.
The reason for cupping on the nape is that it helps with heaviness in the head and what flows towards the eyes. But it should be done after emptying the entire body.
This cupping glass is positioned for a cephalic venesection.
And it is not permissible for someone of a cold constitution or with a cough to use this. For it causes them great harm.
And for this reason it is not appropriate for the elderly to use them or those with cold ailments in their head.
But whoever is diligent about it will have forgetfulness come over him.
And because of this, you should instruct the cupper to briefly remove their hand and the cup downwards, fearing that forgetfulness might be generated.
Cupping or al-kihil is in the place of venesection for the haunches and thighs, and it benefits those with dyspnea, stridor, wheezing, coughing, and fullness.
The al-kihil cup should be pressed briefly because if it is placed below, it causes weakness of the heart and stomach.
Cupping with the al-haqdhim benefits chance headaches and pain, ophthalmia, migraine, toothaches, and tooth roots; it is in place of venesection on the temples.
You should instruct the cupper not to press their hand down with scarring; lest they lacerate an artery and cause bleeding.
Cupping underneath the chin benefits mouth ulcers and gum disease, as well as similar ailments of the mouth; it is a substitute for venesection on both lips.
Wind treatment with spoons contributes to the relief that comes from repulsion and warmth.
Wind treatment of the interior parts with two bellows contributes to the benefit of bloodletting from three veins - Basilica, Fusca and Cephalic; since it attracts blood from all those minute veins that are in the flesh; and those small veins draw from other thicker veins until the drawing reaches the three veins.
You should instruct the wind treatment practitioner not to go too deep with the scarring; since it is a place stripped of flesh and beneath are nerves and arteries.
Wind treatment with the spoon that is winded over the anal fissure contributes to haemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
You should instruct the wind treatment practitioner to use a large spoon and one made of copper; since the location requires strong suction and perhaps the delicate spoons will break; and that scarring is to be done with deep scars.
Wind treatment of the legs reduces them noticeably; since it draws blood from the entire body and contributes to old pains in the kidneys, uterus and bladder; provokes menstruation, contributes to nausea and dizziness; and takes the place of bloodletting of the two saphenous veins and alhorcobeni.
However, it greatly weakens the body and causes many people to faint.
The benefit of the alhorcobeni wind treatment is almost equal to that of the two legs wind treatment.
The quality of the wind treatment placement is that you should place the spoon first while empty and suck with a moderate suction; and do not prolong the position of the spoons but rather put them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humours come to the place in an equal arrival; and do not stop repeating this and continuing until you see the place already reddening and swelling, and there appears a redness of blood; then therefore scarify and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider the arrangement of bodies.
A person with delicate flesh, whose sores are few in number, should be operated on only once; no more, so that the place may not become ulcerated.
Also instruct the Suctionist to slightly enlarge the incision and make it deeper; and to moderate the strength of his suction according to the patient's ability to endure, speed and gentle motion.
If there is great thickness of blood, then you should operate on him twice. In the first stage, a way is opened for the delicate subtraction of blood and its thinning; but in the second examination, there is precise extraction of thick blood.
But if the blood is very muddy, then let there be a third operation to get at the last of it.
Finally, when we wish to draw blood gradually or sparingly, one operation is sufficient for us. But if we want to extract much blood, then we operate with many incisions.
And if we judge that the blood is thick, then it must be operated on with deep incisions; and the limit of equality in incision depth is equal to skin thickness.
What should be administered from the oils at the placement of the suction-cups, and what the Suctionist must avoid.
In the case of someone whose skin is thick, hard and dark with their pores being tight, it is necessary that the places for cupping be anointed with opening, softening and dissolving oils such as alkei oil or viola oil in the summer time, sweet almond oil, or squash seed oil.
And if it is in winter time, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or sarbatine oil; and similar ones.
If however the superfluity is thick and cold then use oil of tamarisk, or balm oil, or poplar oil, or dill oil; and similar ones.
If however the windpipe is long and of tender flesh then it ought to be prevented from oil, and it ought such places of those windpipes to be washed after being oiled with rosewater, or with cold water, or with cucumber juice, or with purslane water; and similar ones.
He however whose blood is of much moisture should wash blisters with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac; and similar ones.
Whose however superfluities are thick should wash his blisters with old wine, or with tansy water, or with a dill decoction, or chamomile; and similar ones.
And it ought to be cautioned against being winded in a bath, or in the steam room of a bath; rather it ought to be administered after exiting the bath for an hour, or two hours.
And one ought not sleep after being winded.
What is it by which the windpipe or the wound ought to be regulated before being winded?
And after it one ought first to consider: then if the windpipe or wound is choleric and there conquer his blood sharpness and inflammation; then it ought to take refrigerants, such as pomegranate molasses: also okra with vinegar and lettuce, and rocket, and juniper: and similar ones.
And give him poultry as food, and boiled mutton, and foods seasoned with vinegar: and things similar to these.
And for one whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take as a drink syrup of honey, or of alum, or else alsecaniabin made from seeds; and let him take medium wine which is neither old nor new; and he must be instructed to use little food. And give him as food poultry, and goose; and sparrows, that is, small birds; and he ought to eat young pigeons.
And it is necessary that on the day of ventousation and bloodletting, drink be more than food; and it is necessary that in some people there be given as a drink alfaroch treacle or a medicine of moss, that is, nutmeg, or alesselithe before ventouses; either before or after bloodletting: to strengthen the main members and thin out the blood. And it is not necessary that they take it heated as a drink.
On ventouses without scarifications. Now ventouses which are without scarifications are those that are placed over the liver, and spleen, and podagra, and over the belly, navel, areas of the kidney, and hip bone; since these members do not tolerate scarifications upon them.
And one does not seek from them anything other than the drawing of blood away from a member to another; as we place a ventouse over the nipples in illness where there is a flow of nasal blood. And we use them to relieve from one member the cold congealed ventosity and place it on another member; as we place a ventouse over the belly or navel. It dilates the limb, heats it up, and removes the pain because it dissolves that viscosity.
It is also placed on the kidneys when there is gravel or stone in them. Perhaps by the power of its own drawing quality it opens up the gravel or extracts the stone from its place.
And it does similarly when applied to the liver or spleen in order to dissolve any viscosity present in either organ.
And these leeches should be applied when fasting, with the necessary incisions only being made. And sometimes they are used along with fire.
And sometimes they are applied full of warm water when there is a case of pleurisy; the leech should be filled with warm water only or water in which suitable herbs have been boiled.
The application of leeches using fire: take some hot coals from the fire, wrap them in a cloth, then apply this to the affected part; afterwards place the leech on top and make the necessary incisions.
The size of the opening should be that of two open fingers, and its depth half a palm; on its side where it is roughly at its middle there will be a small hole sized to fit a needle through: this must be made from bronze or brass, with smooth rounded edges, equal-sided and polished so as not to damage the limb when applied over it: there should be in its middle a transverse bar of either bronze or iron whereon a candle is placed and lit.
Now there is made this large leech, greater than we described above and somewhat less so for the smaller one, according to illnesses and the age of the user.
For the flatulence of children and weak people is different from that of men and those with large bodies.
The quality of placing this flatulent condition with fire on the limb is such that you should light a wick with fire from decent linen or a small candle made of wax; and place it over the middle of a transverse rod which is in the center of the flatulence, so that there may be burning fire around about the lower part of the flatulent condition, so that the body of the patient will not be burnt.
Then it should be placed on the limb; and a finger should sit over the hole that I mentioned, until when we hold the flatulent condition: while we need to, we remove the finger, and vapour will come out over this hole, and the flatulence shall be relieved immediately.
Then light the wick in its own way, and repeat this if you need to.
Now the cupping that is applied in the illness of pleurisy with water does not have a transverse rod or a hole; and it is only applied when it is filled with water, and placed on the limb.
And this is its form.
And the larger this cupping glass is, so as to be able to take in much water, it is better.
§ On Bloodletting by Cupping.
Bloodletting by cupping is not administered to many [people], unless it is on limbs where the placement of cupping glasses is impossible due to their delicacy, such as the lip and gums, and things similar. Either on a part of the body stripped of flesh, such as a finger, and nose, and similar to these.
And the quality of using them is: that you come from leeches which are in fresh water, free of decay.
Then they should be left for a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry and nothing remains in their stomachs. Then the body should be evacuated first with bloodletting, and with emetics. Then wash the patient's limb until it reddens. Afterwards, place them on top of it.
When they become full and fall off, and there is still possible suction of the site with an emetic, then it will be more effective for healing; if not, then wash the site with vinegar. Then with much water, rub it and squeeze it out.
