If the flow of blood continues after the removal of the leeches and it is dripping: then soak a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top until the oozing stops.
If the blood multiplies, then sprinkle over it egg white powder, or gallnut powder, and similar astringents to it: until the bleeding stops.
Or place on the site halves of shelled beans: and leave them until the beans stick to the site, for the blood will be stopped.
And it is necessary if repeating leeches is needed, when possible, change them.
If leeches are prevented from attaching, then wipe clean the site with fresh blood. Or fix a needle to the sites until something comes out of the blood. Then apply leeches, for they themselves will attach immediately when they sense anything from the blood.
When you want them to fall off, sprinkle over them something powdered from aloe, or salt, or ash; and they will fall immediately.
This chapter is also often necessary in the art of medicine, and it deals with restoration after fractures and dislocations caused by accidents to bones.
Know, O Sons, that those who are ignorant of medicine and the people claim to know about this chapter but have never examined an ancient book nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason, this method of science is deprived in our land. And I have never found any good in it whatsoever and did not gain from it what I gained except through the length of my reading of ancient books and study towards an understanding of them until I extracted its knowledge from them.
Then I applied experiment and study throughout my long life.
And now I have described to you from that book into this chapter all the knowledge I contain: and on what my experience has passed, after having abridged it for you, and released it from the occupation of lengthiness, and shortened it by means of a summary abbreviation, and showed it at the end of variation: and formed in it many forms out of the shapes of instruments which are administered into it, being composed of addition and subtraction, as I did in two preceding chapters.
Chapter Two.
It is necessary that we say in the prologue of this chapter an outline and summary of topics for which understanding first compels the surgeon to stand on their truth: namely so that you and those who are willing know or learn this noble art and teach others.
I say therefore that when fracture or dislocation or torsion or fall occurs to someone then you must first hurry to bleed and loosen them or do both at once if nothing prevents it such as weakness of strength or if the one whom it happens to is a child or decrepit old man or if the time is extremely hot or very cold.
Then he should be content with his food, cool vegetables, and the meat of birds and kids; avoid wine, fatty meats, and foods that fill the veins with blood. Until you are safe from hot inflammation and no longer fear the discharge of matter to the limb then let the sick person return to their original diet, with which they are accustomed.
When the broken bone begins to heal, it is necessary that the patient be fed nourishing food with much nutriment, heavy, thick and viscous; such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, heads, feet, and stomachs of fowl, eggs, and fresh fish: also coarse wine and similar things. For with this regimen the clotting of the fracture occurs more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in the first stage of old age and decrepit, it is not possible for them to be strengthened and consolidated according to their nature always, because of the desiccation of their limbs: but those parts which remain of them at the end [of life] become hardened and strengthen like bones of young children.
However nature applies to the broken bone from all parts some substance similar to glue which has thickness, by which it adheres and binds itself together until they are cemented to each other, and bound firmly together, until it returns finally to its former strength and firmness, until nothing hinders it from performing its functions: and for this reason it is necessary that you feed the patient nourishing food in which there is weight, viscosity, and thickness as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of limbs; for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head-bone; and a fracture of the chest-bone is different from a fracture of the back bone: likewise all other limbs differ one from another. And I have come with a recollection of every kind of fracture set forth in its own Chapter distinct from others. And the types of bone fractures also differ within themselves.
Since it becomes an arched fracture, besides which accidents occur in the stars. And sometimes it becomes a fracture along the length of the bone; and its fractures are splintered, that is, stars, and extensions resulting from this, as in a reed when it's cut with a knife for writing. And they do not extend; and it is a fracture with wound and separation at the head, and there is a small crack-like fracture.
And for each type there is a specific skill or tool required for its restoration; this will be discussed later, divided by the areas of the body.
And from where the bone fracture is known, its curvature and prominence are seen; its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
When there is no apparent curvature in its place, nor a sound upon your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, and the patient does not feel much pain; then there is no fracture here, but rather possibly a curvature or small crack - therefore one should not move it with the hand and compression at all, but instead put over it some medicines which will be recalled later, appropriate to the place; then bind it tightly with fine bandages.
And you should know that when the place is arched and split in two, besides splintering, yet all parts are removed from their counterparts; then it's necessary for you to begin your work on its straightening at once before a hot inflammation develops.
But if a hot inflammation does develop, then leave it for several days until the inflammation subsides. Then it is equally possible to adjust it according to whichever method, depending on its softness and ingenuity.
And you should know that its restoration and adjustment are easier than with bone, where there occur fractures.
Then tighten it according to how you will remember it.
If there are fractures in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this broken limb from two sides, if it is a hand or foot; or with one hand, if it is a small limb; or with two cords, or with a cord and a hand.
And your position should be such that you place the limb over an equal spot, according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the broken limb are extended; then try to bring back those additions with all your ingenuity and in any way possible to their respective places: and beware, based on your strength, lest it happen on the weak through your operation some pain, nor injury: and try, according to your strength, to fit one of the two bones with its partner, according to better shapes.
And it is necessary at that hour, that you try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it, and adjust it according to the amount of your strength.
And beware excessive stretching, and strong pressure, as many fools do. For they often cause to happen through their operation a hot abscess, or contraction in the limb: as we have already seen it from their operations many times.
Then fasten after adjustment, and proper operation, and the setting of that limb, and rest, and tranquility: and advise the weak person about it. And make it so that he does not move in the hour of his watches, and of his sleep at his turning, and his agitation, and at his excretion, and all his movements with his final strength. And to avoid having the position of the limb in a position that results in pain. That is, because when he feels pain or injury in the positioning of the limb, then it must be changed to another position where he does not feel pain.
And take care with him so that his position is equal and straight; so that the limb does not become twisted when restored.
And the quality of the fracture of the broken limb is as I describe it.
You should know that broken limbs differ according to their size, magnitude, and shape.
So those that are small among them, such as the arm, finger, and jawbone, and things similar to these, then there must be a soft, thin cloth wrapping. But those that are large among them, such as the thigh and leg, it is necessary that there be a broad, hard wrapping around them.
Because a broad bandage connects the large limb and binds it from all sides of the fracture equally, so that no part is left uncovered.
Then, after your evacuation from the area around the fracture, start to apply a suitable ointment with soft lint on the place of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the place of the fracture itself with three or four turns, according to what the limb deserves; and squeeze your hand a little bit with the bandage.
Go with him to the upper part above the site of the fracture, and bind it less tightly than you bound the fractured site. Extend yourself with the roll above the site of the fracture for some time, and gradually loosen the binding until you get hold of something large from the healthy place.
Then take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured site with bindings. Afterward, go with the binding to the lower part below the fracture. And your action should be in tightening the binding and loosening it as we said about the first upper binding.
Then place between the bindings some soft linen or cloth to even out any curvature of the fracture if there is such a curvature; and if not, do not put anything into it.
Then wrap another bandage over the top of that. Afterward, apply over these bindings warm compresses that are slightly off-centre if there is no swelling nor hot abscess at the site.
But if there is swelling or an abscess, then place on it what will remove the abscess and reduce the swelling; and leave it on for several days. Then bind compresses over them at that point.
And the compresses should be made from the hollowed halves of wide reeds that have been skillfully stripped and shaped; or they may be fashioned instead from wood used to make wickerwork such as pine or willow, which is also known as palm branches, or rushes; and every compress should be the same size with this shape and form.
However, it is necessary that the star-shaped instrument which is placed over the wound be somewhat thicker and wider than the other instruments; and the length of the instrument should be according to the size and smallness of the limb.
Then tighten over the star-shaped instrument with another cord, in the same way as the first tightening. Then bind on top with threads off-center, as we said about the tightening; and it should be that your binding is over the site of the fracture more; and the further the binding is from the fracture, the less tight it should be.
And it is necessary that the threads be of moderate thickness and fineness, and they should be made from soft linen.
Because if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the work of fools who use their own twisted linen cords, it is a great mistake; for the binding slips with them beyond repair.
And fine threads also are not suitable; for you will not achieve with them the tightening that you wish to have.
And it should not be less than a finger's breadth between one star-shaped instrument and another.
If the patient is injured by the ends of the instruments after the binding in healthy places, then place under them some soft tow or crimson wool until nothing is damaged by it.
When there is a fracture with a wound, and it happens that there is a break in the skin, I shall come back to this point specifically in Chapter 2.
And you should know that not every broken limb needs to be bound with star-shaped instruments from the first day. For it is because the limb when it is large, you must not place stars on it until after five days, or seven, or more, according to your judgment regarding avoidance of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters with which the ancients restored those that are placed on a fracture and dislocation.
The method of a common shortened plaster, restoring fractures, suitable for many constitutions, especially women and children: since it is one which neither excessive heat nor cold overcome.
And it is, as you take from the dust of the mill, and it is the flour which hangs on the walls of the mill by the movement of the millstone: and make it like that, except that you sieve it with a strainer along with the white of an egg: and put its preparation neither thick nor thin. Then use it.
The method of another plaster that restores fracture, and dislocation, and twisting. Take alum, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugwort: and juice of all, of myrrh each 10 ounces, and aloe both 5 ounces, and aletheel 20 ounces; pound everything, and sieve them, and mix with water of altheel: or with white of an egg if the patient's constitution is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, restores broken bones quickly, and suits many people.
The method of another plaster which also suits fractures and twists. Take mugwort, mallow, and white poplar, of each 10 ounces of myrrh, 5 ounces of aloe, 6 ounces of acacia, and 20 ounces of Armenian boulder. All these should be crushed thoroughly, then sifted, mixed with water or egg white, and administered.
Method for a plaster to be applied to joints, and bones separated from their places: this soothes the accidental pain of them, bone dislocation, and fracture. Let newly shorn wool be taken, soaked in vinegar and boiled oil, then placed on the affected area.
This plaster has no restorative power but is good for soothing hot abscesses; it specifically expels pain.
Method of a plaster that restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the mulberry fig tree and wild poppies; grind both together to form a plaster from each.
Method of an abbreviated plaster for the restoration of a broken bone; use when you wish to resolve any remaining abscess residue. Take white poplar root, chamomile, violet flowers, and herb flour, all in equal parts; grind them together then mix with wine unless the limb is warm.
If it is warm then mix instead with moist coriander water or just water, and use this.
Method of another stronger plaster for resolving harder abscesses when restoring a limb than the first method mentioned previously. Take white poplar root, flax seed, paeony, fenugreek, ginseng, violet flowers and chamomile, all in equal parts; grind them together then mix with willow water or fresh water or wine. The whole of it according to the warmth of the member, and the calm of its heat.
Hippocrates however did not say in his book that anything should be placed on the broken member when restoring it, except for a poultice made of clay and oil; nothing else; and he described it as being midway between coarseness and fineness.
Galen thought that things should be placed on the broken member when restoring it which dry out heat, such as myrrh, aloe and frankincense, and similar things to those we have described.
Recipe for an ointment which helps with injury and pain. Take equal parts of mallow, chickpeas, human hair cuttings or bird feathers, althea root and salt; pound them up, sieve them through a fine mesh, and make an ointment from them.
The amount of time for which the binding should stay in place is such that you should then remove it only after considering whether there's no pain or itching and the broken bone has not moved from its position; if these things are so, don't remove it for many days. But if there's severe itching or disturbing pain or inflammation at the site, then hurry and remove it within the hour; take off the ointment from them; get a soft cloth or a soft sea sponge, dampen it with water, immerse that in warm water, and wash the place with it until the itching stops and the pain eases.
But if there's severe itching or disturbing pain or inflammation at the site, then hurry and remove it within the hour; take off the ointment from them; get a soft cloth or a soft sea sponge, dampen it with water, immerse that in warm water, and wash the place with it until the itching stops and the pain eases. Then let the member rest for an hour. After that place over it a wadded piece of felt soaked in vinegar and oil or rosewater oil; bind this on top of it for one night until you're sure about the hot abscess, and the inflammation of the member has gone down, and its pain is removed.
Then repeat this to a fine structure and apply a small plaster; do not tighten it with your first binding, but make the finer ones until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling and all other symptoms have already disappeared completely and there is need for a plaster and binding, then repeat as was done initially.
If nothing happens to the limb among those we have said, then do not remove it unless after three days or four or five or seven. And when it is released after twenty days, according as you see the release of the limb, as we have said until such time that fracture adheres and flesh coagulates over it: then add also to your binding more than your first binding in entirety; and increase also in quantity of food by the amount previously prescribed.
If however you see the place of fracture already dried and become more attenuated than it should be then know that nutrition is prevented from reaching it. Scrub it with warm water near to every place where you release it and let this be done for all three days; also relieve the binding somewhat.
By this method therefore nutrition flows to the limb and it is healed swiftly.
But that which foolish physicians do to restore a limb with another bone when the first is not restored as it should be and is restored crookedly, this error on their part and great fear; if it were right the Ancients would have said so in their books and acted accordingly.
And I have not seen any traces of them anywhere at all, and it is better that it should not be done.
# On accidental injuries to the head.
The types of fractures are diverse, and their shapes are many; and its causes are distinctive.
For there is a fracture which occurs from being struck with a sword: and the blow takes away the entire bone until it reaches the marrow, which lies under the bone, as an axe does to wood; and for that reason this type of fracture is called axial.
Or the sword strikes some part of a bone and cuts its surface but does not penetrate the cut to its end; and this type of fracture is called *Kalaan*, that is, complete levation.
And it is the wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is comminution or crushing; and its cause is being struck by a stone, or falling onto a rock, or something similar.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which approaches the membrane that lies under the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone; and it is a wound of these two fractures, either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is concealed within the bone as fine as a hair; and it is a small crack, and for that reason this type of fracture is called *capillaris*.
And there is a fracture which occurs from a fall or being struck by a stone, or something similar, that causes a piece of the bone to enter inwards towards the internals, and creates a concavity; as happens with air bladders, when they are struck, and part of them enters inward.
Indeed, it happens mostly on the moist bones' heads, such as those of children's.
And all these types of fractures occur separately, and I shall come with a reminder of that whole thing in its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures through an examination on it and by investigating them with probes and removing the corrupted flesh that is upon them.
The hairy type is recognized by this fact; it is detected on the bone and cleaned off, then wiped with caustic medicine over it because indeed a fracture shows up black.
However, when treating a fracture, first consider the patient's condition; if you see among his symptoms any that clearly indicate fearfulness, such as vomiting and diarrhea, fainting, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, and chills, high fever, bulging eyes with redness in them, or similar terrifying conditions found in many disorders, by all means it will not heal. And if you see non-terrifying symptoms and hope for recovery, then begin the treatment process.
That is, if an injured person comes to you when he was first wounded and the injury happened during winter days, then it's necessary that you pay attention to removing bone pieces before fourteen days have passed since his wounding according to all disorder scenarios.
And if the injury occurred during summer days, then you should hasten to remove bone pieces before seven days have passed lest soft tissues underneath them become corrupted and those frightening symptoms we mentioned occur.
If the bone fracture has already reached membranes touching brain matter and is accompanied by fragmentation and tearing of flesh, then you must cut away any broken or torn pieces as I shall describe to you.
And it is necessary that you shave the head of the wounded person; and expose the bone according to whichever way is possible for you; and following the shape of the wound, and what is light on the patient.
If it happens to you that during the exposure of the bone there is a flow of blood or a hot abscess: then oppose this with what is appropriate. And you should fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil; and let the wound rest until the abscess is settled, and be sure of no flow of blood. Then begin treating the bone, and its removal.
And this is done in one of two ways.
The first way is to cut the bone with an Incision Knife of fine steel; and this is its form.
Apply another knife, slightly wider than the Incision Knife, after the former; and this is its form.
Then apply yet another knife, wider still than the previous one.
The second way is to have a set of different knives, some wider than others and some shorter; they should be very sharp at their tips and made of iron or steel: you can ease the striking on the knife to avoid shaking the head and harming it.
If however the bone is strong and hard: then you should bore around it before applying any of the knives with a Trephine, which is what they are called because it does not pierce through to that which is behind the bone, since its tip is round like a pin and a small circle prevents submerging and piercing the depth of the bone.
You should take a great number of drills from these, each one appropriate to the thickness of the bone, until you have for every part of the skull a suitable drill, whose acutely pointed end is in length and breadth proportioned according to the thickness of that part of the skull.
These are three types of drills or trepans.
However, the quality of boring round the circumference of a broken bone is to place the drill upon the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then shift the drill to another spot, and place within every aperture a roller equal in thickness to a finger-joint or thereabouts.
Next cut with a knife what is between every aperture in the bone; and make it thinner than you can over that part from one end to another as lightly as possible, as we have said, until you remove the bone either by hand or with some other of those instruments which you have made ready for this purpose such as pincers or delicate scissors.
And take care with all caution that the drill or knife touches no part of the brain.
When therefore you have bored round the bone and separated it from the brain without injuring it so that it remains continuous with it, and drawn it out, it is necessary to smooth and trim evenly with another instrument like a knife what part of it remains in the bone. However, it should be thinner and more delicate than other knives.
But if anything of the little bones or membranes remains then take them up easily with what you have among your instruments for this purpose. Then treat the wound afterwards with dressings and ointments as we have described.
And another method of operation is easy, completely remote from danger: this was described by Galen, and he praised it highly; and these are his words. It is necessary for you first of all to begin by exposing that portion of the bone where the fracture is more violent and obvious: until when you have exposed it, and placed under it this end of the Lenticular knife, which is of this shape.
And the Lenticular part, being smooth, cuts nothing; and its pointed part on either side of it tapering to a point as you see: so that the Lenticular part shall be applied against this saw with its cutting edge acute towards the bone.
Then strike upon the knife from one side with a small mallet until you cut through the bone completely as it turns; and you may be sure that there will happen to it no accident which you fear at all.
And if the surgeon were not more stupid than other men, and less acquainted with it, nor sleeping, then should there remain any part of the membrane attached to the bone in certain places, he would cut it away from that with the end of this same Lenticular knife, and free it from that easily. It is freed from it without injury and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the membrane, and what is torn away from the bone is its surface, while irregularities and fine splinters remain in it; then you must scrape off that irregularity, and root out those splinters with fine scrapers of which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so as to be able to apply from them more suitable and proper ones in every place, according as the operation leads you, and the fractured bone and its shape. And it is necessary that you hold your razor at an angle wider than that which first cuts the bone. Then use the finer one after it.
And it is necessary that you hold your razor at an angle wider than that which first cuts the bone. Then use the finer one after it. And do not stop doing this continuously until you reach the point of administering the thinnest and finest [one].
In each of the remaining small hairline cracks and fine fractures, it is necessary that you administer care according to what leads to its correction.
And it is a matter that is not hidden from anyone who has any interest in this art, and has stood over what we have written, and shown in large fractures.
But if a piece of skin is found to be retained when you remove the bone: then it is necessary that you take a piece of linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another piece of cloth that is doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth; and make it as light as you can so as not to burden the wound.
Then apply above it a wide bandage: and do not tighten it unless to the extent that it holds the wound; and leave it in place for one day or two days until you are certain about the warm abscess. Then remove the bandage and apply afterward medicines with which excessive dryness is superfluous; such as lily roots, herb flour, incense powder, aristolochia, and similar things to them: and it is useful that you make from these medicines a powder that is powdered onto the wound like they are dry.
And universally administer to it all medicine that has the property of cleansing and does not sting. And ensure that according to your skill the wound is clear during its healing process; there should be no greasy stains nor any dirt within it.
And ensure that according to your skill the wound is clear during its healing process; there should be no greasy stains nor any dirt within it. Nor should you allow pus to gather there at all; for when pus gathers over the brain's membrane it corrupts and rots it, and a great deal of harm befalls the sick person afterwards: this can already happen with skull sores if the bone is cleansed but care of it is neglected, leaving blackness on its surface.
So consider then that if any of the conditions we have mentioned befall a sick person you may know for certain it is extremely harmful.
And if blackness does not occur unless caused by the medicine applied to it and this is in the power of that medicine to produce such blackening: then you should take one part honey, three parts rose oil, and dip a linen cloth into them. Then apply this to the sore; afterwards treat it with appropriate healing spices until it is cured.
On resetting a broken nose:
You should know that only the two upper parts of a nose break together or one of them: since it is made up of two bones; for its lower part is cartilage and so breaks neither but suffers instead abrasion, twisting, and creases.
If one of the two parts breaks you should insert a little finger into the nose's hole and with your index finger and thumb equally align the broken part until the nose returns to its normal shape: this must be done skilfully yet gently. And beware, lest through your operation that pain befall the sick person.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and does not reach it with your finger: then you should make a splint for it using a small piece of wood.
But if the fracture is in two parts, then make a splint for it like that one and hurry with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after seven or ten days when the swelling is warm.
Next, insert a linen thread into the end of the nose, if the fracture is in one part; or insert two threads if the fracture is in two parts; and let the thread be of such thickness as to fill the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said to pour butter over the threads and change them every day. That however does not seem right to me; rather it should be that the linen thread is dipped in egg white mixed with flour from a mill. Then leave the threads until the bone is firm and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose instead of the linen thread after it is wrapped in soft cloth; and this holds the broken nose more firmly so that the sick person is not prevented from breathing.
And this thing is not necessary. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, use the threads.
If a hot abscess occurs on the nose again while you are operating, apply to the nose a plaster with a kerchief or with a cotton wool pad soaked in rose oil, or with a small amount of dyakylon.
If a hot abscess does not occur, then you should apply plasters made with sesame flour and powdered incense mixed with egg white. Next, place on it a soft rag made of tow, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
If the bones of the nose are broken in a minor fracture or shattered, then you should cut it open above this and remove them with the appropriate instruments. Then suture the incision and take care of it so that it heals and becomes firm with appropriate plasters or ointments.
If there is a wound between the nose bones, then you should treat it with linen cloths and insert into it a leaden cannula until it heals.
On the restoration of the lower jaw when it is shattered.
When the lower jaw is shattered and its fracture is not accompanied by a wound, look then to see if it is fractured on the outside only, and has not been broken into two parts, and treat it for the inside (of the mouth): then its treatment is easy.
If its fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left index finger into the injured person's jawbone; similarly, if the fracture is in the left jawbone, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and push with it the swelling of the fracture inward from outside with ease by your other hand from the inner part of the displaced bone, and align it.
If the jawbone fracture has already separated into two, then you must administer an extension from both sides, following the straight line, until it is possible to achieve alignment.
If there is already loosening or breaking of the teeth, then bind those you hope will remain with gold wire or silver wire, or silk thread. Then place a poultice over the broken jawbone. Then place over it a doubled cloth, and put on the cloth a large flat splint, or an equal piece of leather, matching the length of the jawbone.
Then bind it from above with bandages as prepared for you, and apply the splint appropriately until it is not destroyed; and instruct the patient to remain calm and still; and give them soft food that can be sucked or sipped.
However, if you think the shape is changing somehow then hasten to remove it on day three. Then correct the deformity with poultices of bran flour and white of egg or sesame meal after you remove the poultice; and put a soft wool pad over the poultice.
While the poultice remains attached to it, and there is no alteration in the position of the bone, leave it be without removing until it heals; or bind the fracture again. Many times this fracture is bound for three weeks.
If meanwhile a hot abscess appears then administer that which we have spoken of many times for its reduction until the abscess is removed.
And if there is a fracture with a wound, consider it. Now if the fragment has already separated from the bone, or is very small: then you should be careful here to remove those fragments of splinters along with what suits you best for their removal using the instruments.
If however, the wound opening is narrow, then widen it with a lancet according to what you judge necessary.
Then when you have removed the splinters and none remain, either close up the wound if it is wide; or if not, put on it a plaster appropriate for such a wound, and apply flesh-forming ointment until it heals.
On the restoration of a fork when broken.
The fork is broken most frequently from the front part of the arm bone, and its fracture occurs according to one of three ways.
Or it breaks and separates into two parts without any splinters appearing; this is easier to restore.
Or such that there are splinters in the bone; this is more difficult to restore.
Or such that it has fractured with an accompanying wound.
The procedure in such a case when there is fracture without an accompanying wound is to have two assistants present: one should hold the support that follows a broken fork, and the other should extend the neck towards whichever side.
Then equalise the fracture with your fingers until it is of a form that suits its shape; and there should be no prominence nor depression within it.
If you need more extension, then it is necessary that you place beneath the patient's ticklish area a sphere made of cloth or wool; and let its size be according to your needs; extend and raise the fork, and shorten the sphere with your hand until the fracture is reduced as required.
If you cannot pull the end of the fork outwards because it has gone too deep, then the patient must lie on their back with a pillow under their shoulder and the assistant should press down on the patient's shoulder until the head of the fork is raised up; then straighten the fracture and adjust it with your fingers.
If you feel a splinter in the fork and it moves around, then you must cut open that spot and remove the splinter with ease.
If it is retained within the bone, then use your knife which you have prepared for this purpose to make an incision over it after you have placed beneath the fork a protector made of wood or iron; and this is the instrument for safeguarding the nerves. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon which has no hollow.
Its width should be according to the size of the bone you need it for, and its slenderness. Its length should also be according to what is possible for the operation.
And it has two ends as you see; one end is wide and the other slender.
If the wound you made when extracting the splinter of bone is wide and you fear it from an abscess with pus, then sew together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the rupture is small or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and soft dressings according to the size of the wound opening.
If a hot abscess occurs, then dip a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it on top of the abscess.
However, if there is a bone fracture without any wound or break, you should place a plaster made of flour and egg white on the bone. And put soft oakum on top of it. Then place a ball under the armpit if necessary. Take a bloodletting cup, which is long and has a width of one palm or thereabouts. Then place a doubled soft pad on top of the oakum and plaster. Take a disk of thin wood whose width is three fingers and its length likewise. Then wrap it in cloth. Wrap the cloth with the disk at the bloodletting cup site over the fracture and joint. Then place the bloodletting cup over the fracture as it was rolled up and wrap it around his neck, under the healthy armpit, and under the injured armpit, pulling it taut across all parts so that you can see how the bone is held by the plaster: and be careful not to cover or obscure any part of the broken bone with the disk. And for this reason, it is necessary that you examine the patient every day. As many times as the ligament is softened, and you see that the dislocation has already been removed; then correct it, and tighten the ligament.
Then put the patient's leg on top of his other leg, and place under his testicle near his scrotum a small pillow at night, so that it elevates his testicle from its side. Therefore, the fracture of the furcula is elevated by the elevation of the shoulder.
Or bandage his arm to his neck; and do not loosen the bandage unless an accident occurs at this place due to itching or from a pustule, until twelve days have passed.
Then renew the plaster, if you see its face becoming visible. And add some more bandage material to the tightness of it, and leave it there until it is restored and coagulated.
Indeed, the fracture of the furcula is tightly bound and takes a long time to heal, typically twenty-eight days; although sometimes it can be less for some people.
On the restoration of the fracture of the spatula...
It is rare for the spatula to break; but when it does happen, the break occurs over a large area of it; and only the edges will be broken.
So when a break occurs to it, and the spatula is broken through its middle; but you would only know about this by feeling for it; then prepare accordingly based on how bad the fracture is. And restore it to its natural shape in every way possible with your skill.
Then place over the wound mill dust with egg white, soft lint, and a small pad of doubled cloth on top. Then place over it a wide plaster made from thin wood, matching the size of the entire spatula or slightly larger.
If there is any depression under the plaster in certain areas of the spatula, then fill that depression evenly with soft lint until the plaster lies level.
Then bind it firmly with long bandages of linen, applying them tightly to secure the plaster in place so that it cannot be moved.
Check the bindings every day. Whenever the bandages become loose, tighten them and adjust the plaster if it has shifted from its position; let the patient lie on their healthy side.
The wound will heal within twenty or twenty-five days.
Once this number of days is complete, untie the bandages; you will then be secure. For it is made up of bones whose fracture or dislocation is not feared.
If, however, a piece of bone protrudes through the skin and causes irritation, then cut it off above that point and remove it. And do what we said for the fracture of the collarbone resulting from an abscess with inflammation, if anything similar should happen.
# On Restoring the Fracture of the Chest Bone #
The chest sometimes breaks in the middle, and this rarely happens. Its limbs indeed break and shatter most often because of its accidents.
When his middle breaks, which leans towards the lower parts, and it causes him severe pain, with difficulty breathing and coughing; he may also vomit blood, and a concavity is apparent in the broken bone, and this is not hidden from sense perception. The patient should be laid on their back, with pillows placed between their shoulder blades to support them.
Then press his shoulders and bring the ribs together from both sides with your hands; then realign it carefully in every way possible until its shape returns as it should be.
Afterwards apply a plaster and linen bandage to him; then place on top of this a willow or hazel board, and similar ones as light as possible after you have wrapped them in cloth. Then bandage it tightly above the broken bone so that it is not moved; and run the bandage around its back several times according to the roundness of its shape, tightening it with an appropriate bandage.
Check the bandage every hour and whenever it loosens, tighten it; if need arises due to itching or corrosion occurring at that site, or pain, or abscess: then you should quickly loosen the bandage and remove the plaster, then rectify what has occurred according to how his treatment goes.
If you see its face swelling then replace the plaster, and attach to it a bandage until he is healed.
The restoration of ribs, when they are broken.
You know that a rib fracture does not occur except in the thick places which follow the spine and from their ends on the front side. And they do not suffer abrasion except because they are cartilaginous. The knowledge of this is not hidden from the sense of touch during examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in this way: equally treat the fracture with your fingers according to what is possible until the shape becomes equal to what it should be. Then apply plasters and strap the broken bone with splints if needed.
And if a rib fracture is sloping towards the interior then the patient suffers intense pain and stabbing sensations as with the stabbing sensation that occurs in someone who has pleurisy because the bone is piercing the lung and also he suffers difficulty breathing, coughing and vomiting of much blood; and this is difficult to treat.
And the ancients have devised many devices for this and some of them say that you should give foods to the patient which generate inflammation and wind so that the stomach becomes inflamed and extended and pushes the fracture outwards.
We however avoid that there is a swift cause for the formation of a hot abscess if it does not happen. But if it has already happened then you should add to it.
And some of them said: place a windy substance on the spot then suck it with strength and this is similar to reasoning. However, it is feared that it may draw superfluous humors to the site due to its weakness.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the site be covered with wool which has already been soaked in warm oil; and let pads be placed between the ribs, until it is full, so that there may be an equal binding when you wrap it according to its roundness.
After that, the patient should be treated with treatment for pleurisy from food and medicine.
But if something extremely serious happens to the patient that they cannot bear, and the splint bites into their wound with an injurious prick, and we fear for the patient; then it is necessary that we split open over the site, and uncover the broken rib. Then we should place over it the tool that keeps a wound closed, as mentioned previously; and we should cut through bone with ease, and also make an incision in it.
Then we should bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large with a suture; and treat it with plasters until it heals.
But if a hot abscess should occur meanwhile, then hurry and pour in pads soaked in rose oil; and place it over the site; and the patient should be treated with what is suitable for an abscess.
In the meantime, they will also lie on their side, which is where they sleep, until it heals.
On restoration of vertebrae in the back or neck.
The bones of the neck sometimes suffer fractures, and they rarely sustain attrition; likewise, vertebrae in the back also suffer similarly. When this happens to someone and you want to know whether they will recover or not: then consider carefully. If you see their hands already softened and stiffened and deadened: and they cannot move them or stretch them out or clench them; and when you try to clench them with your fingers and prick them with a needle, they feel nothing and experience no pain there: then know that it does not heal and is fatal in most cases.
But if they can move them and feel the clenching with your fingers and pricking with a needle in them; then know that their neck bone is intact, and that the patient will recover with treatment.
If this should happen with a spinal injury like his and you want to know if he will recover too or not; then look at his feet: if you see that they are softened and the same happens in them as happened in the hands; then when he lies on his back, gas escapes without control; and when he is turned onto his stomach, faeces escape uncontrollably; and when he is turned onto his back again, he wants to urinate but cannot: then know that it is fatal. Therefore do not trouble yourself with treating him.
If nothing of this happens to him, the case is less serious.
And the treatment for what happens as a result is that you try to soothe a warm abscess; so place on the affected vertebra just rose oil alone; or with roasted lamb meat.
Apply this several times a day until the warm abscess is soothed; then place over it one of the hardening, drying plasters and bind it tight with a bandage: and tell the patient to rest and keep calm. He should not sleep on the side where he finds the pain until it is healed.
If this happens with the grinding of a shattered bone, or something which has already separated from it, then you must cut through the skin and remove it. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with stitches. Then take care of it yourself with some ointment until it is healed.
But if the last bone of the spinal column is broken and it is the end of the tailbone, then you must put your left thumb into the anus and equally align the fractured bone with the other hand as best you can according to this alignment.
Then put something on top of it and take it off again; treat the wound with the treatment that came before until it is healed.
On restoration of the hymen.
Chapter 11.
The bones of the hymen rarely break. If they do break, then their fracture is not a breaking but rather a splintering at the ends; and they split lengthwise, and curve inwardly; and it happens that there is pain and pricking in the affected area, and the leg which breaks becomes numb.
Its restoration is indeed to spread your hand over it until you feel the fracture in the manner of its shape.
If the fracture is only at its ends, then align that fracture as it is prepared for you with an alignment until its shape resembles its natural form.
If, however, the fracture is along its length or has already shifted inward, then have the patient lie on their stomach until you prepare to set that fracture. When you have therefore equalized it, place a plaster cast upon it; then place upon it a splint of wood or leather, and bind the binding firmly over which you fear neither displacement of the fracture nor removal of the splint.
And equalize the concavity of these with that which fills it until the binding takes hold according to equality; and direct the patient to sleep on his back or on his healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs to him, then cease from its extension and its setting until the hot abscess subsides; and place upon it what lies above it according as before, then return to its setting; and bind it as is required.
If however there is a break or splintering of the bone, extract it.
On setting fractures for assistants.
An assistant is one who is between the elbow and the head of the spatula.
If it breaks therefore, its setting is according to one of two ways.
One of which is to take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this shape that follows.
And there shall be bound at the two ends two bindings.
Then hang it from an elevated place; and let the patient sit on a seat. He should then throw his broken arm over a wooden splint until he reaches his little finger, in the middle of which is attached the curved part of the wooden splint.
He should then hang something heavy above it so as to stretch out the attendant toward his lower parts. Then the doctor should align the fracture with his hands at the same time until the fracture returns to its correct place.
And there is another way in which the patient lies on his back over his neck; and hang his hand from his neck with a bandage. Then order two attendants to hold it; one of them should hold what is above the fracture with his hands and the other below it while each stretches in their own direction.
And if you wish the stretching to be stronger, then bind below the fracture with a bandage and above it with another bandage; and let each of the two attendants stretch in his own direction.
So if the fracture is near the shoulder then a bandage should be placed under the little finger and another one below the fracture toward the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow then a bandage should be placed over that place and over the elbow itself.
Then align the fracture gently without difficulty until it is aligned correctly and well set; then bind it if there is no hot inflammation.
But if there is hot inflammation then release the binding until the seventh day. And place over it a compress of wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the inflammation calms down; then bind it by placing a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a bandage made from new cloth over the plaster. Then bring your arm over to support it, and place your open hand on your shoulder.
Place a cloth and tighten it over the supporting arm, so that the arm remains in place; if nothing prevents you from doing this, nor is anything changed about the broken bone above.
If you fear that something has changed about the broken bone above, then use splints; and it is necessary to place over the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints. Place your finger between all the splints, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an additional three fingers on each side.
Then tighten over the splints with the binding I mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is necessary for your binding to be tighter over the place of the fracture. And as much as the fracture extends lengthwise, let the binding be looser.
If therefore you see the splints and binding set as we have said, when you restore the limb immediately do this; but if you fear a hot abscess, then remove the binding and splints up to the seventh day, as I mentioned.
Then inspect the ligature over all three days, so that there is no itching or swelling at the site nor food prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive binding. Therefore correct any of these things according to what I have said.
If it is a serious wound and there is no risk of an adverse event occurring, then do not remove the dressing for at least three days or more.
And if the patient is sleeping on their back with their hands over their stomach, place under them an even pad full of wool to support them.
And take care at all hours of the night or day that the position of a broken limb is not altered nor its dressing loosened. Do this, then, with all your skill.
And feed the patient according to the instructions given, offering them soft food at first until their mouth begins to heal, after which you should thicken their diet.
For the usual time for a splint or bandage is forty days; at this point it should be removed, and the patient bathed and treated with ointments to correct any deformity.
But if the fracture is severe or complicated by injury to the bone, do not remove its splint and bandage for fifty days, even up to two months.
On restoring a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the humerus (upper) and ulna (lower).
One of these is small, being that which follows the thumb. The other one is large, being the one positioned below the small bone on the lower part of the arm.
Perhaps only the larger bone is broken; or perhaps just the smaller one. And sometimes both are fractured at once.
When therefore the little jawbone is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower jawbone is broken, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when both bones break at the same time.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the little jawbone above, then it befits the physician when restoring it to place a small extension with ease until he makes it level.
If however the large jawbone is the one that is broken, then it is necessary to place a much stronger extension.
And it is necessary to place the shape of a hand when restoring it, and its extended extension above a pillow. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers above; and let it be lower than all the other fingers, or the rest; and the patient sitting square on himself, and let a pillow be before him, or in his line, at a height so that the patient does not incur labour.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from its lower part, either by his hand or with a bandage. And another attendant extends likewise from above.
Then let the physician make level the bone until he restores it to its best shape as far as possible.
If there is splintering in the bone fracture, then try to bring all the pieces back into their place by your strength.
If however it appears to you that there is a separated splinter and it pierces the skin, and you have no hope of restoring it; then find its place, and remove it as we said in what was previously mentioned.
If there is a fracture with a wound, I have already made an individual chapter on it, so take the treatment from there.
If at the beginning of your restoration a hot abscess occurs, then line the cloth with a plaster made of rose oil, white wax, and medium thickness/consistency, and bind the cloth on it with a fine bandage until the abscess is dried up; then remove the plaster and apply an ointment prepared from flour and egg white. Then put on the splint, which should be slightly wider and stronger than the fracture itself.
And be aware that the number of splints required for the arm is six in most cases, whether the fracture occurs in one bone or two bones simultaneously.
Then apply a stronger and tighter bandage over the fracture site, somewhat more so above or below the fracture; or apply a slightly softer bandage with that of your previous recollection at the beginning of this chapter.
The cloths that wrap around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or rough. The thread used for binding should be pure linen of medium thickness between fine and coarse, as we described earlier.
And examine the limb and the joint after one day. If something requiring correction occurs, such as itching of the limb, then immerse the limb in warm water until the itch subsides and release the bandage without tensioning until it quiets down; then repeat the binding process.
If the bandage has already loosened and the bone has moved out of place or similar issues have occurred, then correct it with all your might and examine it again.
If nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excess tightness of the bandage, then you must loosen it a little and leave it for several days until the nourishment reaches it, then tighten it again.
If nothing of what we have said happens to the patient, then it is not necessary to remove the bandage until after twenty days or so.
Then the patient's hand should be hung from their neck. And their neck should be kept level and protected by its own strength from active movements. Make them sleep on their back.
You should know that this fracture heals within thirty days or so; maybe it will heal in twenty-eight days. All of this depends on the patient's constitution and strength.
On the healing of fractures in the hand.
Know that fractures in the bones of the hand and knuckles of the fingers rarely occur. And it does not happen unless there is repeated friction or pressure on the bones.
When there is a fracture or pressure on the heel bone, then you must have the patient seated in a square seat and place another equal-sized seat before them. Then place their hand on top of it, with the palm extended.
Then have an assistant stretch out the broken bones, and let the doctor align them well until they are joined in a good union.
Then it is necessary that he place a plaster and lint, lest there occur a hot abscess. Then place a tile on top according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in a linen cloth.
But if the fracture is towards the lower part, around the sole of the foot, then make a similar sphere out of cloth, and instruct the patient to press their broken foot against it; afterwards, bind it with a long cloth.
And let the tile be made of leather, which has some give to it so that the skin may adhere to the sole of the foot; and bind it as necessary.
But if the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary to place a tile above and another below the foot; and let the hand be open and straight. Then apply a bandage as the hand is turned; and extend it between the fingers with a splint.
If there is a fracture of any of the finger joints, then if it is the thumb, treat as necessary. Then bind it with the foot; and if it is crooked, place a straight tile under it of moderate size, so that the fracture may be elevated and not disturbed.
But if the fracture is of the other fingers, such as the middle finger or index finger, or little finger and ring finger, then they shall be treated; and bound with the healthy finger that follows it, or all bound together in sequence, which is better. Or place above it a small tile, as we have said for the thumb.
Consider well your restoration and, after the appearance of the hot abscess, oppose it with what is necessary when something happens to it, as described in this account.
On the restoration of a broken hip bone.
The hip bone breaks often enough that it needs no explanation; it turns to the inside and back.
The bone is restored by binding the ligament above the break and another below it. The patient should be laid face down.
Next the attendant should stretch each binding to its limit on either side; this is when the break occurs at the centre of the bone.
But if the break is close to the hip's root, let one binding be attached at the hip's edge so that the stretch goes upwards; and another below the break.
Similarly, if the break is close to the knee, let one binding be attached near the knee so that the stretch goes downwards.
Then the doctor should align the bone with both hands until it matches its original shape; and join the bone with a good joint.
Then you should apply a plaster cast and bandage if the limb does not develop an abscess. But if it does develop an abscess, leave it alone for a few days until the hot abscess settles, then return to your treatment.
The bandaging should be done as follows: You should wrap above the fracture with a thick dressing, that is, a bandage; and double it twice or three times: and there should remain some excess of it. Then wrap the leg until you reach the heel at the base of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the hip and leg near the knee towards the underside, and let the ends of the thread come out from the upper side of both parts. Then roll up onto the leg and hip what remains of the other dressing.
Then place over the hip at the site of its fracture some splints, and put one of these splints over the leg bone.
Then fill the gaps between the leg and hip with soft cloths until it is even.
Then begin with a strong binding from the middle over the site of the fracture with three or four turns. And the farther you extend the binding from the site of the fracture, let your binding be tighter and softer, and gentler.
Then proceed to the two ends of the thread that you inserted between hip and leg. Bind therefore with each three splints which are above. Then go with the two ends of the thread to the underside until you reach the sole of the foot. And with each bind also the ends of the splints on the other side, so that the binding is not displaced from its place.
Then leave the binding in place until it is healed, so that there will be no corrosion or inflammation of the limb nor anything similar to these things. If anything happens to it. Purify it with its remedy, and rectify whatever happens to it entirely, as we have said many times.
But if there is a splinter of bone inside the bone which pricks [the wound], then you should equally remove it, as far as this is possible for you. And if not, bind firmly over it, and remove it; then treat the person whose condition has been recalled until they are healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is compressed; besides which splints are applied to it, as we have said for the shoulder and arm.
But when such fractures of the hip bone are healed, lameness does not happen to the patient. And if it is healed by itself apart from having the other leg applied to it, then it is necessary for that person whose hip it is always to limp.
And you should know that the hip is compressed within fifty days or thereabouts, whether it increases a little or diminishes a little; all this depends on the various temperaments and other conditions.
On the healing of the fracture of the kneecap.
You should know that the kneecap rarely gets fractured but often gets worn down.
If the kneecap is fractured then it will only be either a crack or hole in its parts. And this happens with injury as well as without it. He stands upon it completely aware.
Its restoration is such that what was broken of its parts must be made level with the fingers until it is gathered together and joined as much as possible by leveling and ease, and shaping.
Then apply a plaster of sesame flour or wheatmeal with egg white and place over it a round splint if required; bind this above with suitable bandages.
Next you should consider all the treatments we described for other fractures, such as hot poultices and similar remedies; so that you may oppose every hazard with what is appropriate to it until it heals.
On restoring the fracture of the leg.
You should know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick and called the shinbone; while the other is thin and named the calf bone.
Just as with the two bones of the arm, it can happen to both types that any kind of fracture occurs; and for this reason its restoration is equal to that of the arm's, and the treatment is one and the same.
If both bones break at once, the leg turns to all sides.
And if the thinner bone breaks, the leg turns towards the front.
If the thicker bone breaks you will find it below; and this is one of those that do not escape your notice.
Next give traction and levelling; then bind the splints equally.
However, if the leg fracture is severe with many splintered bones, your reduction and realignment must be less extensive and gentler.
And indeed there is something in the operation of the leg that is superior to the arm; and it is such that when you align the stars and complete your entire operation, then take two wooden splints from pine wood, which is used in making shingles placed under roofing tiles: they are made from palm spatulas; choose those that have a small thickness but are not too narrow and let their length be according to the leg's length, from knee down.
Then wrap each splint with two layers of cloth, according to its length. And place one end of the leg on one splint and the other end on the other splint; let them extend from knee to beneath the foot.
Then bind the two splints together at three points, at each end and in the middle. For when binding is prohibited, the leg will bend to the right and left and remain in a good position.
Now provide a wooden cradle according to the length of the leg and place it within, so that it keeps the leg from moving.
It is especially important to do this for the kind of fracture accompanied by an open wound.
Then monitor the leg each day, attending to it with great care if pus, swelling or other complications develop. If any of these complications occur, then oppose it with what is required until healing occurs, if God wills it.
On fractures of the bones of the foot and toes.
Indeed the heel bone does not fracture completely.
Fractures of the bones of the foot do occur at times and also fractures of the toes occur rarely; and they only happen to them if there is friction in many places.
If a fracture of the bones of the foot occurs and you see those bones being raised above one another then let the patient put his/her foot on prepared ground as if walking.
Then stand up and put your foot over that which is raised from those bones; afterwards press or stamp on it; and level it until it returns to its place.
Then apply a plaster, then a piece of cloth above it; and put under the sole of the foot a small board which has two flat ends.
Then bind it with suitable bandaging after you have wrapped it up in cloth and the other things required.
When therefore three or four days pass by then undo the bandage. For you will find that the bones have returned to their level state or they may be fractured or dislocated. Therefore the treatment of them is as you see it.
But if some toes are fractured then restore them and level them as I have described to you regarding the restoration of fingers on the hand. Then put a splint under the toe according to its length and let it be somewhat wider than that; then put underneath this board which I have described to you and bind it with suitable bandaging.
If the bone that is broken has two, three or more pieces, place over each finger a splint of appropriate size, wrapped in linen cloth; secure the board to the underside of the foot; and have the patient stand on one end of the board outside the belly of the foot so as to provide good retention.
You should also not forget to take into consideration all that I have told you about the other types of fractures from among the mishaps we discussed; and you must treat each mishap with its appropriate remedy.
Fracture of the woman's pubic bone, woman's pelvic bone, and man's thighbone.
When the woman's pubic bone is broken, have her sit on a square-shaped object. Then lean her towards one side of her back, and have someone hold her from behind.
The midwife should fill the woman's pubic area with cotton until it is full; this will make her pubic region swell into a sphere-like shape.
Next, the woman should be shaken gently, and her back raised slowly. The cotton will come out until there is a sphere-like shape in front of the woman's pubic bone; this indicates that the broken bone has returned to its place.
Next, place a small cushion on her back. When she needs to urinate again, remove the cotton gently, and then let her urinate; return it as you initially placed it; afterward, have her go back to her cushion.
He should do this for seven days, or thereabouts, because that is how long it takes for the bone to heal itself.
And if you wish, take a goat's bladder and tie over its opening a reed tube, then insert the entire bladder into his rectum. Then blow into the tube with all your might until the bladder is fully inflated inside his rectum, for this will reduce the fracture.
Then it should be filled with fat and left there for several days, as we have described, until the patient is healed.
And when the bone of a man or woman's hip is broken, administer this treatment and apply what we have described for healing the bone of the pelvis.
And the rectum is not injured in such breaks nearby, which happen rarely, especially to anyone who has any concern or use for it.
And understand this my book well, for the very break itself shows you in many cases the straight path to its healing and knitting together: so understand it.
But if the man's penis is broken, take a goose's gullet and insert the penis inside it. Then place over it a wrapping of cloth and bind it up, then let it remain for three days or thereabouts; and he will be healed if God so wills.
On the healing of broken bones where there is also a wound.
It is especially suitable for him who has sustained a broken bone with a wound, if it be large, like the bone of the hip or thigh or similar, that you make haste and immediately bloodlet him; provided the indications for bloodletting are present as we have set out above.
If the wound is bleeding then you should hurry to cut off that part of the flesh in which you see blood issuing forth and sprinkle on it crushed zege powder if no other remedy is available.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not delay it if suppuration does not occur.
If however suppuration does occur that is hot then leave the restoration until day nine until the suppuration has settled down. And do not come near it on the third or fourth days for you would make bad things happen to it.
If however the broken bone is found to be protruding through the skin then it should be gently pushed back in and made level with your hands using little force and extension.
If however it is not possible to prepare its extension or make the bone level with your hands then use this instrument to push it back in. And this instrument is made of iron seven or eight fingers in length and its width is according to the size of the wound. And therefore it is necessary that the physician take three or four of them according to how many are needed for this treatment in every type of fracture.
And it should be round with a small thickness so as not to double up when pressed upon during the operation and have sharp ends that are tapered at its edges. And it should be thick in the middle tapering off towards its ends.
This is how this instrument looks.
In this operation, it is necessary that its curved pointed end be placed over the protruding bone end; and push it with a single motion until such time as the bone returns to an equal state.
Therefore, try to align the ends of the fracture with each other.
If its fractured end is slender and does not receive the tool with a good grip, then it is necessary to cut off that bone end until the tool has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce them as we have described altogether, then cut it with that which suits it from the Cutting Instruments which we mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
Then scrape off what remains on the bone of roughnesses and small splinters.
When the bone is reduced and the patient finds severe pain afterwards, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
Therefore do it. For you confer upon the patient great aid when your restoration of the bone is completed.
Then dip a cloth in black astringent wine. And especially if it is summer. And do not place a warm compress on the wound, nor anything containing oil; so that it does not cause rot and decay within.
Then give the patient aster leaves at the end of each hour from your bone restoration. Release the wound that has been exposed: so that you make an incision with scissors into the involutions according to the size of the wound.
And take every precaution so that you do not tighten the wounds when there is a fracture. For many times have foolish physicians done this: and they caused to happen upon their patients either death or decay or gangrene, that is pus. And let your tightening be soft, gentle, unlike the tightening of other fractures.
But if the wound is bad or it's a large wound and you fear some of the bad complications we've described will happen to it, and find pain in the suspended area: then it's not right that you set it with splints. And make them involutions of hard cloths at the place of the splints, and tighten it with them.
But when it is after one day or two days and you see the wound starting to generate pus: then remove from over it the cloth that you set upon it with wine. Then apply lint cloths and ointments that we are accustomed to using for healing wounds: such as the four-ingredient ointment, and those like it.
And it is fitting that you loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every day in the morning and evening until it heals and becomes whole again.
And the limb must be prepared so that pus may flow from it with ease to the lowest part of the wound.
If many days have passed since the wound and it has not healed nor discharged its pus then know that there are small splinters of bone in it. Therefore, it is necessary to examine a wound with care. How many of these splinters are separated, remove them, and extract it. And those which are not separated from them, and pierce the member, and cause pain to happen, try to cut into them, and remove them in every way that is possible for you.
If a wound is inflicted with poison, or ekileten, that is, istiomenus, or some other kind of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary to oppose each one of them with what suits it from the cure, whose preceding narrative has preceded in its chapter.
And from that which my speech ought to stand in, and to which you make your mind present when a bone is broken and protrudes above the member, such as the thighbone, and the helper-bone, and similar ones of those large members, it is, do not presume its attraction, for many times death happens from it; rather, leave it alone until it decays.
Indeed, it may fall off by itself after twenty days or thirty.
Then therefore treat the wounds if it seems right to you; and if not, leave it be.
Regarding the treatment of nodation in the aftermath of a fracture.
Many times, this nodation happens in the aftermath of healing from a fracture. Especially when it is near the joints; and the figure of the member is marred by it. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, consider this then, if the wound is fresh: apply to it medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, sardonicum, gum acacia, and similar substances.
So take some or all of these substances: aloe, egg white, or vinegar; and mix them with astringent wine, or with albuminous egg white, or with vinegar: then apply this mixture to the wound, packing it with lint, and bind it firmly in place.
Leave the binding on, and do not remove it for many days. Then remove it, and repeat with another binding until the wound is healed.
Or bind over it a moderately-sized piece of lead foil. For lead has the property of removing any fearsome matter from the limbs.
But if the wound has already turned hard like a stone, and is bound up, and there's an urgent need to remove it: then shave over it from its upper part, and cut away any raised parts; or scrape them off with some razors until they are removed: and treat that area until it heals.
Concerning treatment when the limb is restored but remains thin, not according to its natural state.
When the broken bone is restored but the limb remains thin and weak, it happens only due to many causes. One of which is the excessive amount of broken ligaments and their binding not as it should be; or due to an excess of bandaging that prevents nourishment from reaching the limb; or too much ointment applied at the wrong time; or because of a lack of blood in the patient's body, and its weakness.
And its treatment is to feed the patient and make their body tender until blood multiplies within them. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and happiness to them.
Then place pitch on the limb so that it attracts pus towards itself in great quantity. And treat it regularly with warm water until the pus flows out and it returns to its natural shape.
On treating broken bones when they have healed crookedly.
When it happens that a limb which has already healed, after its cure, displays crookedness and prominence of the broken bone or knotting; and is disfigured by it although the limb does not lose any power to perform its natural movements: then you should not accept the opinion of those who think that the limb ought to be broken anew.
And already there have been many foolish physicians and healers who carried this out in our country.
This operation is indeed highly reprehensible, leading to death or great fear so that the patient becomes moribund.
But if the limp and knot are recent, then it is necessary that they should be treated with warm water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as marshmallow leaves and root, as well as honeysuckle, and similar. And make for them a plaster with softening ointment like well-made dichalcon.
Either take a mucilage of althea root and mix it with chicken fat, or with oil of sesamum: and plaster it on.
Either take fat figs and mix them with pigeon dung, and similar substances which are called medicines that reduce inflammation.
And sometimes it is loosened by persistent, gentle rubbing of long standing, which is done with moist hands. And apply movement to the limb in all directions every hour.
But if the curvature has already developed and become severe, and turned into a stone, and it is necessary to treat it with iron; then it must be cut above that point, and the bone's continuity should be loosened, and the superfluous part of the bend or the bone itself should be cut away with fine knives: and ease in this should be administered with care and attention.
