A dislocation is the separation of any joint from its socket. It is prohibited from moving due to damage to the shape of the limb. And it causes severe pain and injuries for the afflicted person.
So when dislocation occurs in someone, then it is necessary to hasten their restoration without any delay.
For if it is delayed, the site may become inflamed, and reduction of the dislocation might be affected.
Therefore it is not appropriate for it to be moved or extended during the hour of its inflammation. For it often causes spasms to occur and painful injuries.
But when this happens, you must hurry to bleed the patient. Then let it be dismissed until the inflammation quiets down.
Afterwards, the limb should be anointed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced gently.
And every limb shall be healed with him whose memory comes back to its place.
I have already arranged the chapters on dislocation, also according to what preceded it regarding fractures, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
§ On the treatment of the dislocation of the lower jaw.
Both jaws rarely dislocate, unless in rare cases.
And the dislocation of both happens according to one of two ways. Either they are removed from their places with a small force, and become slightly softened. Or they dislocate with a complete dislocation until they soften against the patient's chest; and when their saliva runs down and they cannot hold it back or close their mouth, and their tongue rolls out with speech.
But when its dislocation is small, it returns to place by itself in many instances without treatment.
If it is indeed a complete and full dislocation, then it must be reduced promptly, and not delayed at all; the assistant should hold the patient's head, while the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw between their teeth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or they should insert both thumbs together if the dislocation is on both sides. And his other fingers must remain outside, bracing them evenly.
Tell the patient to clean their jaws and start chewing on both sides equally. The physician braces the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to reduce, especially if both jaws are dislocated: administer steam with hot water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy; do not delay reducing both at all, as we have said.
Once they return to their place and align, and the patient's mouth is closed without slackening; then place some cloth pads on them that have been made with wax ointment and rose oil.
Afterwards, they should be bound gently with soft bandages.
The patient should sleep on their back; their head should be supported between two pillows so that they do not move it from side to side; nor should they try or be forced to chew anything; rather, their food should be given in the form of a soft liquid until such time as pain diminishes and the jaw is loosened: then they may eat what they want, which must be served gently.
He should not strain his operated mouth during eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is healed and strengthened.
If the reduction of the two dislocated jaws becomes difficult at some time and they do not return to their places, then fevers often occur with persistent pain; perhaps the patient's bowels will open and perhaps he will vomit pure bile.
So when you see this happening, then know that it is a serious case; and often the patient dies within ten days.
On the reduction of dislocations of the collarbone and shoulder blade.
The collarbone cannot be dislocated from its inner side due to its attachment to the chest. And it may sometimes become dislocated outwards, which is obvious to the senses.
Its restoration involves having the patient lie on his back with arms extended. Then squeeze it tightly with your hand in a strong grip; for it will return to its place.
After that, apply an ointment and bandage on top of it, and bind it firmly.
The parts that follow the elbow and are connected to it do not become dislocated unless rarely. If it becomes dislocated on some day, then it must be reduced and put back into place according to what we have said and what is prepared for you.
After that, apply an ointment, padding, and a tight bandage on top of it. Advise the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until they are healed.
And when this same treatment reduces the extremity of the humerus, it is also separated from its place.
The reduction of shoulder dislocation.
You should be aware that the humerus cannot dislocate unless it is in one of three ways.
One way is for it to dislocate towards the side of the pectoralis muscle at the bottom. The second way is for it to dislocate towards the side of the chest. And perhaps it dislocates at the upper end of the humerus, which occurs rarely.
It does not dislocate backwards due to the scapula. And it does not dislocate forwards due to the nerves.
Indeed, it most often dislocates and exits towards the bottom at the side of the pectoralis muscle; especially among those with little flesh. Since it exits quickly from them, so too does it enter quickly.
Among those with much flesh, the opposite is true; that is to say it exits with difficulty and enters with difficulty.
Perhaps there are people who experience a blow or fall and the humerus becomes dislocated with a hot swelling; it is thought that this occurs because of a previous dislocation.
Therefore you must test it until you are certain of this, then proceed to treat it.
And dislocation is recognized when it faces downwards towards the little finger; and you should distinguish between a dislocated arm and a healthy one. For you find among them an obvious difference, and you find that the head of the arm is hollow, and near the little finger, by the touch of the arm's head as if it were an egg: and the sick person cannot lower their hand to their ear nor move it in all its motions.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards: then you find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And indeed, the reduction of this kind of dislocation is easy when it is recent or if the patient is a child.
And its reduction method is for an assistant to lift their hand upwards. Then you place your thumbs under the little finger of the patient, and lift the joint with strength upwards to its place: and the assistant lifts their hand and stretches it upwards. Then they should put it down; for indeed, it returns quickly.
But if it does not return as we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary for the patient to bathe in warm water, and an embrocation that softens and soothes should be administered, such as when roots of althea, fenugreek, and meadowsweet are boiled in water, and it is administered.
Then the patient should lie on their back: and under their little finger is placed a sphere of wool that has an equal balance between softness and firmness.
Then the physician should place their heel on the ball and lift the patient's shoulder with strength, drawing the sick person's hand downward.
And if you wish, return him to this position: and it is necessary that you have present a man who is taller than the patient, and who stands by the side; and he should insert his shoulder under the patient's collarbone: then lift their collarbone upward until the patient is suspended in midair. And another attendant should pull the sick person's hand downward towards their abdomen. But if the patient is light, then something else should be hung from them to weigh them down; for otherwise the dislocation will return immediately.
It is also returned to another position: and this requires that a long wooden pole be fixed into the ground, its head being round in shape like a pestle handle, neither thick nor thin. Then the patient should be placed upon it after soft cloths are put on top of the wooden pole's head; and the sick person is made to stand along the length of the wood.
Then his hand should be pulled downward on one side, and his body extended with strength from the other; for the joint returns swiftly to its place.
If returning the joint is difficult by all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it requires a wooden pole whose length be equal to two arms' lengths, its width one of four fingers, and its thickness that of two; with a round head so as to ease its entry into the patient's collarbone, in this shape.
The cloths, soft ones, should then be bound over the head in a round manner so as not to injure the weak wood.
Thereafter place under the ticklish area of the patient so that the hand or arm is stretched out over the wood down to the lower part; and bind the wood onto the helper, arm and tip of the hand.
Then place the arm on the step of the ladder from side to side and stretch out the hand down towards the lower part; let the rest of the body be suspended on the other side. For indeed, it immediately enters into the joint.
When therefore its entry is completed in whatever way possible; then you should place under the ticklish area a sphere of equal quantity made of wool. Then put on the plaster prepared with mill dust and frankincense, and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it is rolled down from above.
Then bind tightly under the ticklish area with a suitable bandage; and roll the ligature over the plaster from above; and attach his hand to his neck, and let it remain there without moving for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient be given little food until the limb becomes strengthened; for this promotes a quicker healing of it.
Thereafter apply a larger dressing after seven or five days, and let it be stimulated with movement. If therefore it is made firm and not softened, then it has already healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times, because of the moisture that happens to it or for some other reason; then you should perform on it a three-holed caustic according as has been premised above in the chapter about cauterization.
If all this is done and the ligament is released after seven days, but the joint does not become firm and the poultice and bandage are reapplied to it several times without its becoming secure, then falling off so that you cannot raise it up to above: know that the nerves of his arm towards the head of the shoulder have already been severed or stretched out or softened; and know that the joint will never become firm in its place.
Dislocations that are towards the chest and nipple or those that are reduced to the back are done with a push and extension of both hands until it returns, then it is secured on it all other bandages and care until healed.
If after healing there is heaviness in the limb and sluggishness in its movement, then let the patient use a bath many times until that heaviness eases and returns to his original nature.
Treatment of elbow dislocation:
You should know that the elbow joint is displaced with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and in fact it displaces towards all sides but especially towards the front or back.
And its displacement will not be hidden from you since it is visible falling down and also felt with any form of dislocation. And when you join the displaced elbow to its healthy one it is made clear to you. For the joint is twisted and cannot bend the arm nor touch its shoulder with it.
And you must hasten to reduce the dislocation immediately before it suffers from hot inflammation.
For if he develops a hot abscess, its reduction becomes difficult and it may not heal at all, especially when it is on the back. For it is worse than all other types of dislocations and causes intense pain; often death comes with it.
And when its restoration is possible among those that can be put back in place, it is done by extending his hand with both hands: and his arm should be stretched out, and the physician's hands are above the elbow and under it, and he himself pushes the joint with the thumbs of his hands together or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
Or the dislocation is at the front; then it returns thus, by doubling his hand with his own hand until he strikes with the root of his palm on the elbow that is dislocated.
But if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a violent, very strong extension; and it is done by two attendants extending his arm; and two other attendants should also hold him so that he cannot move during the extension.
Then the arm is turned round towards every side after a doubled cloth that is long or a wide strap has been wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with oil so that it is from those things which help to lubricate the joint and make it move more easily. Then he pushes the joint with a strong push until it returns to its place.
And after its reduction, an astringent plaster containing alum and egg white should be placed on it; and it is to be bound with a suitable bandage. The arm should be hung from the patient's neck and left there for several days.
Then it will dissolve. If the joint is then found to be back in its proper place, release the bandage from it and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not held firmly enough, then apply another plaster and bandage and leave it for several days until it tightens. Then release it.
But if there is any stiffness after the joint has been set and it is not in proper motion: then apply moist heat with bath water, light friction, and compression until there is relief; or place on the joint the hip of a fat wether, that is to say, mutton fat. Then bind it and leave it on day and night. Then take it off and put the patient in a bath. When he is sweating, then lightly rub down the joint with friction. Then repeat on the joint the fat of a wether, once, twice, and thrice with each bath until relief is obtained.
And if you wish, place on it the droppings of a cow, moistened, heated with butter and bind over them.
Do this several times. For it is eased thereby, and returns to its proper position.
Notes on the treatment of dislocations by Albucasis.
The shoulder joint is frequently dislocated.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. In contrast to other joints. However, it is necessary that the speed of its dislocation's reduction be immediate when it occurs before the place becomes swollen; otherwise, a hot swelling will occur there.
And the reduction of its dislocation is done by placing the patient's weak shoulder on a table, and the assistant extends their arm; then, the doctor places their palm over the protruding joint and pushes it until it returns to its original position.
However, one should consider that if the palm has already retreated towards the belly of the hand, then place the patient's weak arm on top of a table during extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is advancing towards the back of their hand, then have them place their hand underneath the table, i.e., the interior part of their hand should be placed on it so that the doctor's hand falls upon its protruding joint.
If it returns immediately, that is good; if not, then bind it with a swelling-reducing plaster and leave it alone without touching it again.
For if it does not tolerate it nor allows you to reduce its dislocation after several days have passed while the joint remains twisted; and this causes no harm to the patient except for softening their hand so they cannot grasp anything: then know that their nerves are either severed or severely damaged.
It is therefore not an option but to bind it with a caustic plaster near its rupture point. And perhaps it has no bearing on this issue.
Since therefore Almahassem does not return, then place upon him the plaster which we described. Then he should be bound, and left for five days; afterwards, the binding is removed and he is released, and the hand is exercised.
But if movement is lost and something serious happens to it; then treat it gently with warm water and massage several times until it is eased.
On the cure of dislocation of the fingers.
When any of the fingers are dislocated towards any side, or one of them is dislocated toward the back of its palm or belly; then extend the finger and push on the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then hold the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the side to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it until it becomes straight again on its own day.
So when night comes, bind it according to the very description; and do not cease from releasing it during the day: and exercise it with movement; then bind it at night.
Do this for several days until it is bound up.
And similarly do the same if it is dislocated towards the belly of its hand; and bind it to that side, and perform your first procedure on it until it is healed.
And similarly proceed when it is dislocated towards other parts.
Regarding the treatment of spinal dislocations.
When someone has a complete spinal or neck dislocation, or many vertebrae are displaced from their positions: there is then no cure for them, as death hastens towards the sick person.
And a sign of this is that the patient's ejection happens without their will and they cannot hold it back; and sometimes certain parts of their body become numb, or both legs, or both arms, or one of them; or one vertebra moves from its place.
It often gets displaced because it is often dislocated towards four parts.
The one that is displaced backwards is called gibbositas.
The cure for this is to consider at that time, if gibbositas happens from childhood: then there is no cure and it does not heal completely.
For the one that happens from an accident, or a blow, or something similar to those: the ancient masters have already spoken at length about methods of treatment. Most of which does not return with benefit.
I have however abbreviated from that which excuses little of its vastness due to my closeness to the intent: and by my exposition as I exposed it, and fashioned its implement in a way different from how they presented it and expounded upon it.
I say therefore, that there is no skill or healing for the gibbositas which happens from the front.
And similarly for the one that is done on two sides as well.
And it is not treated from them, except for the one that happens on the back itself, with what I am about to tell you: and it is, as the patient is stretched out on their face on a flat table near the wall: and a wet blanket is spread underneath so that their chest is not harmed.
Then a straight piece of wood is placed firmly fixed into the ground at the end of the table, around their head: and another piece of wood around their feet at the other end of the table: and an assistant holds this wood, which is not firmly fixed into the ground. And another assistant holds the other (piece of wood).
Then roll over the patient's head a soft but strong bandage: and extend the end of the bandage to the wood that is by their head: and tie it there.
Then tighten another bandage over their hips, and over their knees, and at their calves. Then gather all the bandages together, and tie them to the other wood that is by their feet.
Then each assistant should pull on the bandages towards their part of the wood, and not move the two woods from their places where they are fixed: but they must not be firmly fixed as we have said.
And the surgeon should place their palms on the spine with strength until it goes back into position: or they can put a board on top of it. Then they should tie their feet to the board until it goes back into position.
If, however, it does not return by this cure, then take a board whose length is about three arms. And let the groove in the wall of which we have spoken, which is near the weak spot, be the place into which the end of the board enters. Then place the middle of the board over the spine. And let the physician put his feet on the other end. And he should strap it tightly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a screw that is turned by hand: and it is such that you drive into the ground by the patient's head at the end of the bedpost two pieces of wood whose length is one arm; and at his head at the end of the other bedpost two more pieces of wood.
And let the length between each piece of wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made in every place through which the screw runs; and every piece of wood must be firmly driven into the ground to ensure it does not move at all.
And let a round piece of wood be inserted; and there is a screw in which the strap turns in two holes made in two pieces of wood.
And at its end is a hole in which the wood is fastened whose length is one palm with that which turns; and likewise there are two more holes in the other two pieces of wood.
Then tighten the straps which you tightened over the patient's chest with a screw that is by his head; and those which you tightened over his legs with the screw that is by his feet.
Then stand by every turn a minister who twists his hand with the ligature, with which the turnstile is rotated; and the doctor levels the vertebral bone, as we have said.
This is the form of the tourniquet that follows.
Then after the vertebral bone returns and the place is levelled, you should put on a drying plaster with egg white. Then apply a compress. After that, place on the plaster a wooden splint three fingers or so wide. And the length of it should be such as to cover the site of kyphosis and overlap the healthy portion of the vertebra.
Then bind with the ligature we described. And let the patient be fed with light nourishment until recovery.
But if there remains a prominence at the site upon completion of healing, then softening and soothing medicines must be applied along with prolonged use of the board we described. And now a leaden plate is applied on that site.
And sometimes there is a prominence at the last vertebra of the back which is thought to be due to dislocation and it is that bone which already stands out. For this reason the assumption should not be made with regard to this medicine because it may possibly cause death.
On treating a dislocated ankle.
You should know that dislocations of the ankle and shoulder do not occur unless there is a full dislocation; nor do they experience what other joints experience due to minor displacement and concavity.
The ankle joint, in fact, dislocates according to four directions. That is, because it dislocates forwards and backwards.
And indeed, most dislocations are inward; there is little forward or backward displacement.
The treatment of an inward dislocation is as follows: when you join the healthy leg of a patient to their affected one, it will be longer; the knee of the affected side will protrude more than that on the healthy side and they cannot touch their foot to their groin; there will also be a warm swelling following the groin due to the head of the thigh bone having reached this point.
The signs that an individual has suffered an outward dislocation are the opposite of those described above.
The signs that an individual has suffered a forward dislocation are as follows: the healthy leg is extended but cannot be folded without pain at the knee; if they try to walk, they can't bring their foot up in front of them and they retain urine; there will also be swelling around their groin during walking, causing them to tread on their heel.
The signs that an individual has suffered a backward dislocation are as follows: the knee cannot be straightened nor folded until the groin is; their leg will also appear shorter than the other and there will be softening around the groin with the head of the thigh bone moving into the hip region.
The reduction of these types of dislocations, however, must consider how long the patient has had this condition when treating it. Then, if you perform its reduction and it remains according to its own arrangement, then there is no cure for it at all. It should therefore not be assumed to have been thoroughly pierced.
In that case where the dislocation is recent and it belongs to one of the four types of dislocations, then make haste to roll the joint, extend it inwards and outwards, and move it right and left; perhaps this will result in a relapse, after which it won't require any other treatment.
If this does not work in that manner, then you must prepare a strong assistant and have them extend the patient's leg from below; either with their hands or with a bandage tied above the patient's knee; another assistant will extend the patient's hand from above while inserting his/her hands under the patient's armpits. Then bind with a gentle bandage over the hip bone, holding onto one end of another bandage.
And the extension will be either from the front of the groin to the fork area or from behind towards the back.
And let the extension of all these forces be in one direction until the patient is lifted off the ground with their body and remains hanging there.
This type of extension is actually the common type among the four types of dislocations.
If the dislocation returns with what we've said before then it is good; if not then every type of dislocation will require its own specific treatment that I will describe now.
During the actual reduction process when the dislocation is inwards then it's necessary for the patient to lie on their back on top of their healthy side. Then place a ligament over the root of the hip bone, at that point which is between the head of the hip and the region beneath the groin. Then extend the ligament upwards from the groin region to the higher part of the body, towards the fork (of the ribs).
Then another strong helper takes his arms, reduces the thick area of the weak hip, and stretches it outwards with a vigorous extension. Now he indeed returns to his place.
And this type of operation is easier than the other types of treatment in which this limb is reduced.
But if he refuses to obey you and does not enter with this type of treatment at all, then the two feet of the patient should be bound together along with a strong but soft bandage over both heels and both knees; each [band] should be four fingers long relative to its respective comparison point, and the sick leg must be extended more than the other by a length of two fingers.
Then the patient is to be suspended over the head of a wooden beam that is placed inside the house; its length from the ground should be equivalent to two arms' lengths.
Then order a strong lad to secure the head of the hip bone; another helper is hung alongside the patient and urges the young man who has secured the hip with strength because the joint returns swiftly to its place.
When it returns to its natural position and the dislocation is outwards, the patient must lie on a bench as we described for someone with a hump. And bind the bandage over his sick leg properly as well as over his chest.
Then place two pieces of wood, one at his feet and another at his head. Then a supplementary piece of wood should be placed at the center of the platform, secured firmly; over which are wrapped damp cloths, so as not to hurt the patient; and this wood should be situated between their hips, such that they aren't pulled down towards their lower parts during extension.
Then each assistant should pull on their side, while the physician balances out this movement with his own hand.
If it therefore yields to correction, that will be good.
And if not, place them on the board as we described for scoliosis, pressing equally. However, it is necessary that the patient lie on their healthy side.
And when the dislocation is forward, then it's necessary that the patient's leg be extended quickly; and this follows the same narrative of being on the platform; and the physician should place the palm of their right hand over the patient's groin. Then draw it in with the other hand, and while doing so, extend them downwards towards their knee.
And if the dislocation is backward: then it's not necessary that the patient be extended towards their feet and lifted up off of the ground; instead, they should be placed on a hard surface; just as we described for when their hip is dislocated outwardly, according to what we said about having them lie on two planks; and here too they are lying face down with their ligaments extended, just like what we discussed earlier.
And it's necessary that compression be applied with a board over the place where the joint emerges once reduction has been completed as should be done.
And the sign of its return will not be hidden from you: and that is to stretch the patient's two legs: and when you see them equal, and the patient tightens his leg, and extends it without hesitation: then know that now the limb has returned as it should: then join the two thighs, apply a plaster, and tighten with imemati, which is a wheel: so that it does not move at any part: and let the patient rest for three or four days.
Then untie the bandage and remove the plaster, compare the leg with the other one: then if you see them equal at rest, then know that the dislocation is now firmly set: so let the patient go for a walk.
And if you see any sign of softening: then go back and bandage it again, and tighten according to your initial tightening: and let it rest for three more days.
Then untie it and make the patient delay walking on both legs for a few days until it is well-consolidated.
Chapter: On Dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and backwards, that is, towards the back of the leg.
And it is not dislocated forwards.
The sign of its dislocation is when you ask the patient to apply his leg against his hip. But if it does not stick to the hip: then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all types of dislocation is to make the patient sit upright so that he can now extend his leg if possible over it, and have your back adhere to their face; then place their foot between your feet.
Then adhere their knee to your palms and straighten them between your fingers above their knee. Then apply your palm with strength to both sides of their knee. And the other attendant will stretch out their foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of restoration is that the leg will adhere to the thigh with ease, not painfully.
Then bandage it, and make sure that the leg adheres to the thigh. Then bind them together with thread for three or four days. Then release them.
And the patient should not walk for several days until they heal.
If the patient resists reduction of their limb, administer a strong extension with the ligaments described earlier for hip treatment until it returns.
§ On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Heel Bone.
Sometimes the heel bone is dislocated with a minor displacement, and sometimes it's dislocated further along. And its dislocation occurs either towards the inner or outer side.
The indication that this is a dislocation is when you see the inflated heel bone moving towards the side it was dislocated to.
The treatment for its removal is an easy return: it is to be extended gently by hand and straightened until it returns.
In treating it when it is dislocated according to the socket, you should have the patient sit upright: and a strong assistant should hold them firmly from behind at the middle of their back.
Then you hold their foot with your right hand from above, and with your left hand underneath the foot at the heel.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then, with your left hand using two fingers.
Then extend with the left [hand] and push the heel of the foot towards the shin with your right hand without force.
Do this twice as we described.
Then push the heel of the foot towards the shin for the third time, and you pull on the heel.
If therefore it returns on the first or second try in this way, and you see the foot is straight, that's good: if not, then repeat the procedure on it: for itself has returned.
If however the return is prevented with what we described: then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and fix a stick into the ground firmly so it falls between their thighs: and you should already have wrapped a cloth around them so they are not hurt.
Then the assistant should take hold of his hip; afterwards, another assistant should stretch out his foot, either with their hands or with a cord tied around the ankle. Then each assistant should stretch [the patient's leg] against the stretching of their peer; and a stick shall be placed between the two hips of the patient to restrain him so that his body is not drawn down towards its lower parts during the stretching.
Then the physician should correct the dislocation with his hands, while another assistant holds the healthy leg downwards. For the dislocation returns swiftly.
When the dislocation returns, and you are made aware of its return: place a plaster and bandage upon it, bind them tightly with cord, and secure the foot to the leg with a ligament.
And you must take heed or fear the nerves that are above the back of the heel, so as to ensure that the cord is not overly tight and injures them.
Then let [the patient] rest for two or three days. If the cord loosens, tighten it; then release [the patient] on the third or fourth day; and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if they try to walk before this period, it is not safe lest the joint be disrupted and destroyed anew, such that it will no longer respond to treatment.
But if a hot swelling occurs to him: then you must manage its reduction by means of what was said earlier in our treatise at another place about treatment, and an ointment until it is removed.
On dislocation of the toes and its treatment.
It is necessary to straighten what has been dislocated with a slight pull without effort; and this indeed is not difficult but rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in some of the joints of the instep; then it is necessary for the patient to sit with their foot on a level spot on the ground or on a board, and he should be standing as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot over that which is protruding from those joints. Then press it with your foot using force until it returns to its place; then you will see that it is now level and the protrusion at that spot is no longer visible.
Then place under his sole a board that will fit the whole foot; it should have two ends. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then release it and have him rest from walking for many days until he is bound and secured by the reduction.
On types of dislocations with wounds or fractures.
When something happens and you try to treat it and restore it, death often results; and for that reason one should not presume on treating an injury of this kind unless he is prudent in his art, experienced, merciful, pious, slow, unhurried, not rash or presumptuous: and let him use in the beginning medicines which only cool hot sores; and leave the patient to divine judgment. Unless you witness, or hope for salvation from destruction with mild illness, and in which something of hope appears. Try therefore to reduce it immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If therefore the limb returns according to our wish, then apply a regimen that calms the abscess, and treat the wound with what suits it from drying ointments.
If there is a dislocation with a fracture and separated splinters occur in the bone, then attempt to remove them, and do what we said about simple illnesses regarding what precedes in their places.
And beware according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear; as my previous instruction to you stated.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and safer for your body among them during your time.
